Commentary
This passage is an expanded commentary (sharh) on the definition of qiyas (syllogism) as 'a statement composed of propositions from which another statement follows essentially'. The commentator, likely drawing from the matn of al-Abhari or similar, explains the function of each condition in the definition. He begins by noting that 'statement' (qawl) includes all types of compounds, and 'composed of propositions' (mu'allaf min qadaya) excludes incomplete compounds and single propositions that imply their converse or contrapositive. He then discusses the removal of simple propositions (which are obviously not composed) and the removal of compound propositions (such as conditional statements) from the definition, providing two arguments: first, that the term 'propositions' in the definition should be understood as explicit, independent premises, and the second part of a conditional is not an explicit proposition; second, that in common usage 'propositions' implies multiple distinct statements, whereas a conditional is considered a single statement. He next explains that 'follows' (yalzimu) excludes induction (istiqra') and analogy (tamthil), because these do not produce certain knowledge; induction based on partial observation does not necessitate a universal conclusion (e.g., observing that some animals move their lower jaw when chewing does not entail that all animals do, as the example of the crocodile shows), and analogy (e.g., comparing bang to wine in intoxication does not necessarily entail prohibition due to the possibility that the 'illa of wine's prohibition might be specific to grape-derived alcohol). The commentator then addresses the condition 'by itself' (li-dhatihi), which excludes arguments that require an external premise to produce the conclusion, such as the syllogism of equality (qiyas al-musawat), where one says 'A equals B, B equals C' which requires an additional premise that things equal to the same thing are equal to each other). He also discusses how this condition excludes arguments that involve conversion (aks) of the premises, such as the proof that a part of a substance is a substance using contrapositive conversion, or the proof that some animals are rational using simple conversion. Finally, he refutes an objection that figures other than the first are excluded by 'by itself' by explaining that their productivity is not essentially immediate but requires conversion or other operations, which are internal to the syllogistic form.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator say the syllogism of equality (qiyas al-musawat) is excluded by the condition 'by itself' (li-dhatihi)?
A) Because it has more than two premises.
B) Because it requires an external premise to produce the conclusion.
C) Because it is not composed of propositions.
D) Because the conclusion does not follow at all.

Key Takeaway
The condition 'by itself' ensures that the conclusion is immediate from the premises; arguments requiring hidden premises or conversions are not essential syllogisms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the argument: 'The world is temporally originated. Everything temporally originated has a cause. Therefore the world has a cause.' Does this argument satisfy the condition 'by itself'? Yes, because the conclusion follows directly from the two premises without any external premise. Now consider: 'The world is temporally originated. If the world is temporally originated, then it has a cause. Therefore the world has a cause.' Is this a qiyas? (It is a conditional syllogism, modus ponens, which might also require an external premise about the conditional? Actually it does not. So it's valid. But the equality syllogism is different.)
?
Identify the external premise in the equality syllogism 'A=B, B=C, therefore A=C'. Write down the missing premise.

The Syllogism

Division of Qiyas: Istithna'i and Iqtirani
Author defines the division of qiyas based on presence of the conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any qiyas as either istithna'i or iqtirani by checking whether the conclusion is explicitly stated with its matter and form, and identify qiyas al-musawa as a special case requiring an external premise.

Core Idea
A syllogism is either istithna'i (conclusion present) or iqtirani (conclusion absent); qiyas al-musawa is neither unless supplemented.

In this passage, the author defines the two main types of qiyas based on whether the conclusion is explicitly stated. This division is foundational for understanding how arguments are structured in Islamic logic. We'll also examine the exceptional case of qiyas al-musawa, which appears to be a single syllogism but actually reduces to two.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The author begins by analyzing the term being classified: al-qiyas (the syllogism). The division is based on a property of the syllogism itself.
2
Extract the two types
The passage divides qiyas into istithna'i (conditional) and iqtirani (connective). The key criterion is whether the conclusion (al-qawl al-akhar) is mentioned within the syllogism.
Istithna'i has the conclusion; iqtirani does not.
3
Understand the criterion: matter and form
For istithna'i, the conclusion is mentioned with its matter (subject and predicate) and form (affirmative/negative relation). Iqtirani omits the conclusion entirely.
Matter = terms; form = arrangement.
4
Analyze the exception: qiyas al-musawa
Qiyas al-musawa seems to have the conclusion present but not essentially; it requires an external premise (e.g., 'the equal of the equal is equal'). The commentator explains it reduces to two syllogisms when the external premise is included.
Without the external premise, it is not essentially conveyive.
5
Apply the classification
Test a given syllogism: Does it contain the conclusion explicitly with terms and arrangement? If yes, it is istithna'i. If no, it is iqtirani. If it is qiyas al-musawa, it is only conveyive with the external premise.
Use the criteria from the passage.

فإن كان مذكوراً فيه بمادته وهيئته لكن لا لذاته بل بواسطة مقدمة خارجية، وهي أن مساوي المساوي مساو، وقياس المساواة مع هذه المقدمة الخارجية يرجع إلى قياسين وبدونها ليس من أقسام الموصل بالذات. فاعرف ذلك. والقول الآخر اللازم من القياس يسمى نتيجة ومطلوبا قوله فإن كان : أي القول الآخر الذي هو النتيجة، والمراد بمادته طرفاه المحكوم عليه و به والمراد بهيئته الترتيب الواقع بين طرفيه، سواء تحقق في ضمن الإيجاب أو السلب؛ فإنه قد يكون المذكور في الاستثنائي نقيض النتيجة كقولنا: فإن كان مذكورا لما فرغ من تعريف القياس شرع في تقسيمه إلى الاستثنائي والاقتراني، وإنما قدم الاستثنائي؛ لأن مفهومه وجودي ومفهوم الاقتراني عدمي والوجود مقدم على العدم كما لا يخفى، وضمير "كان" راجع إلى "القول" أي فإن كان القول موجودا في القياس وبدونها أي بدون المقدمة الخارجية ليس من أقسام الموصل بالذات، وأما معها وإن كان من أقسامه لكنه ليس قياسا واحدا بل قياسان. فاعرف ذلك : أي فاحفظ التحقيق المذكور بقوله : وقياس المساواة مع هذه المقدمة" إلخ. حاصل التحقيق أن لقياس المساواة اعتبارين: الأول: اعتباره مع تلك المقدمة الخارجية، وهو بهذا الاعتبار داخل تحت المعرف، فوجب إدخاله حينئذ تحت التعريف؛ لكونه حينئذ موصلا بالذات غير محتاج في الإيصال إلى مقدمة خارجية زائدة عليه، والاعتبار الثاني اعتباره مع خروج هذه المقدمة عنه وحينئذ ليس من أقسام القياس وخارج عن تعريفه بقوله : لذاته؛ فإن استلزامه للقول الآخر بواسطة الأمر الخارج لا لذاته. (إسماعيل) المحكوم عليه و به واعلم أن المراد بمادة القول الآخر طرفاه، فإن كان القياس استثنائيا أو اقترانيا حمليا فيكون طرفاه المحكوم عليه و به وإن كان اقترانيا شرطيا فطرفاه المقدم والتالي، فالمراد بمادته طرفاه المحكوم عليه و به، أو يمكن أن يقال: إن طرفي الشرطيات المحكوم عليه و به بالاتصال والانفصال أو سلبه. فافهم.
If it is mentioned in it with its matter and its form, but not essentially, rather by means of an external premise, which is that the equal of the equal is equal, and the syllogism of equality with this external premise reduces to two syllogisms, and without it it is not among the divisions of what is essentially conveyive. So know that. And the other statement necessarily following from the syllogism is called the result and the sought. His statement: 'If it is': i.e., the other statement which is the result, and by its matter is meant its two terms: the subject and the predicate, and by its form is meant the arrangement occurring between its two terms, whether realized within affirmation or negation; for it may be that what is mentioned in the conditional syllogism is the contradictory of the result, as in our saying: 'If it is mentioned'... When he finished defining the syllogism, he began dividing it into the conditional and the connective, and he gave precedence to the conditional because its concept is existential and the connective's concept is non-existential, and existence is prior to non-existence as is not hidden. And the pronoun 'it' refers to 'statement', i.e., if the statement exists in the syllogism, and without it (i.e., without the external premise) it is not among the divisions of what is essentially conveyive. But with it, although it is among its divisions, it is not one syllogism but two syllogisms. 'So know that': i.e., so memorize the verification mentioned by his saying 'the syllogism of equality with this premise' etc. The summary of the verification is that the syllogism of equality has two considerations: first, considering it with that external premise, and in this consideration it falls under the definiendum, so it must be included under the definition because it is then essentially conveyive, not needing an external premise beyond it for conveyance; and the second consideration is considering it when this premise is excluded, and then it is not among the divisions of the syllogism and falls outside its definition by his saying 'essentially', because its entailment of the other statement is by means of an external thing, not essentially. (Isma'il) ... and know that by the matter of the other statement is meant its two terms; if the syllogism is conditional or categorical affirmative, its two terms are the subject and predicate; if it is conditional connective, its two terms are the antecedent and consequent. So by its matter is meant its two terms, the subject and predicate, or it can be said that the two terms of conditionals are the subject and predicate by conjunction and disjunction or their negation. So understand.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is a definition and division of qiyas (syllogism). It begins by stating the condition for a syllogism to be classified as istithna'i (conditional): the conclusion (al-qawl al-akhar) must be mentioned within the syllogism with its matter (the subject and predicate) and its form (the logical arrangement). However, an exception is made for qiyas al-musawa (syllogism of equality), which is not essentially conveyive but becomes so through an external premise. The commentator explains that such a syllogism actually reduces to two syllogisms when including the external premise. The passage then discusses the terminology: 'matter' refers to the terms (subject and predicate or antecedent/consequent), and 'form' refers to the relational arrangement. The commentator also explains why the author gave precedence to istithna'i over iqtirani: because the concept of istithna'i involves existence (the conclusion is mentioned), while iqtirani involves non-existence (the conclusion is not mentioned). The passage concludes with a clarification that in a conditional syllogism, the terms can be considered as subject and predicate by way of conjunction or disjunction.

Key Takeaway
The primary division of qiyas is based on whether the conclusion is explicitly present; qiyas al-musawa is an edge case that reduces to two syllogisms when including the external premise.

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
Distinguishing Istithna'i and Iqtirani Syllogisms
Author introduces the division and gives examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between istithna'i and iqtirani syllogisms based on whether the conclusion (al-qawl al-akhar) is explicitly mentioned in the premises.

Core Idea
A syllogism either states its conclusion (istithna'i) or only its raw terms (iqtirani).

The author opens a section on the division of syllogisms by introducing a key distinction: some syllogisms explicitly mention the conclusion in the premises, while others do not. This determines whether the syllogism is called istithna'i (exceptive) or iqtirani (connective). Understanding this division is the first step in classifying any qiyas you encounter in Islamic logic texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the presence of the conclusion
Look for a statement that exactly matches the conclusion (or its contradictory) in the premises. If found, the syllogism is istithna'i. Example: 'If this is a human, then it is an animal; but it is not an animal' — here the contradictory of the conclusion 'not human' is mentioned.
2
Check for the absence of the conclusion
If the conclusion is not mentioned, the syllogism is iqtirani. The premises contain the terms (matter) but not the final relational structure (form). Example: 'The world is changing, and everything changing is originated' — the conclusion 'The world is originated' is not stated.
3
Subdivide iqtirani into hamli and sharti
Once you know it's iqtirani, determine if the premises are categorical (hamli) or conditional (sharti). If all premises are subject-predicate statements, it's hamli. If at least one premise is a conditional (if-then) or disjunctive, it's sharti.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فائدة فاستثنائي وإلا فاقتراني حملي أو شرطي، وموضوع المطلوب إن كان هذا إنسانا كان حيوانا لكنه ليس بحيوان ينتج أن هذا ليس بإنسان، والمذكور في القياس: هذا إنسان، وقد يكون المذكور فيه عين النتيجة كقولك في المثال المذكور: "لكنه إنسان" ينتج أن هذا حيوان قوله فاستثنائي: لاشتماله على كلمة الاستثناء أعني لكن قوله: وإلا: أي وإن لم يكن القول الآخر مذكورا في القياس بمادته وهيئته وذلك بأن يكون مذكورا بمادته لا بهيئته؛ إذ لا يعقل وجود الهيئة بدون المادة، وكذا لا يعقل قياس لا يشتمل على شيء من أجزاء النتيجة المادية والصورية؛ ومن هذا علم أنه لو حذف قوله: بمادته لكان أولى. قوله: فاقتراني: لاقتران حدود المطلوب فيه وهي الأصغر والأكبر والأوسط. قوله حملي أي القياس الاقتراني ينقسم إلى حملي وشرطي؛ لأنه إن كان مركبا من الحمليات الصرفة فحملي، نحو: العالم متغير، وكل يتصور وذلك: أي نفي هذا المجموع ههنا في نفس الأمر إنما يتصور بأن يكون القول الآخر مذكورا بمادته لا بهيئته ولا بأمر آخر مذكورا بمادته لا بهيئته؛ فإن الاحتمالات العقلية ترتقي إلى الثلاثة الأول: أن يكون مذكورا فيه بمادته لا بهيئته، والثاني: أن يكون مذكورا بهيئته لا بمادته، والثالث: أن لا يكون مذكورا فيه لا بمادته ولا بهيئته. الثالث باطل؛ فإنه يمتنع أن يكون القياس غير مشتمل على الأجزاء المادية والصورية جميعاً؛ فإنه حينئذ لا كونه موصلا إلى النتيجة كما لا يخفى والثاني أيضاً باطل؛ فإن الهيئة العارضة للأجزاء المادية للنتيجة غير منفكة عنها، فكيف يتصور وجود الهيئة بدون المادة إلا أن يلزم ثبوت العارض بدون المعروض ووجود الملزوم بدون اللازم وهو باطل، فتعين الاحتمال الأول. (إسماعيل فإن قيل: الاحتمال الأول أيضاً باطل؛ لأن نفي الهيئة يستلزم نفي المادة أيضاً؛ إذ وجود المادة بدون الهيئة محال قلنا: المراد الهيئة الخاصة الواقعة في النتيجة، ولا يلزم من نفي الخاص نفي العام فيجوز أن يكون للمادة هيئة أخرى. وجود الهيئة لأن الهيئة عارضة للمادة ولازمة لها، ووجود العارض بدون المعروض واللازم بدون الملزوم محال فكيف يعقل؟ أولى وجه الأولوية الاختصار في العبارة مع حصول المقصود؛ فإنه اللائق بشأن الماتن خصوصاً لمن يكون بصدد تهذيب الكلام وتقريب المرام. إسماعيل) حدود المطلوب : أي حدود التي لها مدخل في حصوله، فلا يرد أن الوسط خارج عن المطلوب يعني لما كان القياس الاقتراني مشتملا على أداة الجمع والاقتران، وهي الواو الواصلة سمي اقترانيا.
Benefit: So it is conditional, otherwise it is connective, categorical or conditional. And the subject of the sought: If this is a human then it is an animal, but it is not an animal, so it yields that this is not a human. And what is mentioned in the syllogism is: 'this is a human'. And it may be that what is mentioned in it is the very result, as in your saying in the mentioned example: 'but it is a human' yields that this is an animal. His statement: 'so it is conditional': because it contains the exception word, namely 'but'. His statement: 'otherwise': i.e., and if the other statement is not mentioned in the syllogism with its matter and form, and that is by being mentioned with its matter but not its form; for it is not intelligible that form exists without matter, and likewise it is not intelligible that there be a syllogism without including any of the material and formal parts of the result; from this it is known that if he had omitted his saying 'with its matter', it would have been better. His statement: 'so it is connective': because of the conjunction of the limits of the sought in it, which are the minor, the major, and the middle. His statement: 'categorical': i.e., the connective syllogism is divided into categorical and conditional; if it is composed purely of categoricals, it is categorical, e.g., 'the world is changing, and everything changing is originated'. And his statement: 'and that': i.e., the negation of this totality here in reality is only conceivable by the other statement being mentioned with its matter not with its form, and not with anything else—mentioned with its matter not with its form; for the rational possibilities rise to three: first, that it is mentioned in it with its matter not its form; second, that it is mentioned with its form not its matter; third, that it is not mentioned in it neither with its matter nor its form. The third is false, for it is impossible that the syllogism not include all the material and formal parts; then it would not be conveyive to the result, as is not hidden. The second is also false; for the form that is accidental to the material parts of the result is inseparable from them, so how can form exist without matter unless it implies the subsistence of the accident without the substance and the existence of the necessary without the necessary condition, which is false. So the first possibility is determined. (Isma'il: If it is said: the first possibility is also false, because the negation of form necessitates the negation of matter also, since matter without form is impossible; we say: what is meant is the specific form occurring in the result, and the negation of the specific does not necessitate the negation of the general, for matter can have another form. The existence of form is because form is accidental to matter and necessary for it, and the existence of an accident without the substance and the necessary without the necessary condition is impossible, so how can it be conceived? The better, the reason for priority is to shorten the expression while achieving the purpose; for that befits the status of the author, especially for one who is engaged in refining speech and bringing closer the goal. Isma'il) The limits of the sought: i.e., the limits that have a role in its attainment, so it is not objected that the middle is outside the sought. That is because the connective syllogism includes the connective tool, which is the particle 'wa' (and), so it is called connective.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of the division of qiyas into istithna'i and iqtirani, and further subdivision of iqtirani into hamli (categorical) and sharti (conditional). The author first gives a 'benefit' (fa'ida) that provides a rule: if the conclusion (al-qawl al-akhar) is mentioned, it is istithna'i; otherwise, it is iqtirani. Examples of istithna'i are given using conditional statements like 'If this is a human, then it is an animal; but it is not an animal; therefore this is not a human.' In this case, the contradictory of the conclusion is mentioned. It is also possible for the very conclusion to be mentioned, as in 'but it is a human; therefore this is an animal.' The commentator explains that the word 'istithna'i' comes from the exception word 'lakinna' (but). For iqtirani, the conclusion is not mentioned with its form; the matter (terms) is present but not the specific relation. The commentator argues that it is impossible for form to exist without matter, so the conclusion can only be present by its matter, not its form. Then a detailed argument is presented: there are three possibilities for the presence of the conclusion in an iqtirani syllogism: (1) mentioned by matter only, (2) mentioned by form only, (3) not mentioned at all. The third is false because a syllogism must contain some parts of the result to be conveyive. The second is false because form cannot exist without its matter. Therefore only the first is possible. An objection is raised: if matter cannot exist without form, then even the first possibility is false. The answer distinguishes between the specific form of the result and form in general; matter can have a different form. The passage concludes by affirming that the iqtirani syllogism is called 'connective' because it uses the particle 'wa' (and) to connect the premises.

الاقتراني
الحملي
الشرطي

B) The conclusion or its contradictory is mentioned in the premises
C) The premises are all categorical
D) The middle term is the subject of the major premise

Key Takeaway
A syllogism is istithna'i if the conclusion appears in the premises; otherwise it is iqtirani, which further divides into hamli (categorical) and sharti (conditional).

The Syllogism
intermediate

Three Possibilities for the Conclusion in Iqtirani Syllogisms
Author argues and proves the only valid possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three rational possibilities for the presence of the conclusion in an iqtirani syllogism and prove that only one is valid using logical reasoning.

Core Idea
Only the presence of the conclusion's matter (not its form) is logically possible in an iqtirani syllogism.

After establishing the division of iqtirani, the passage delves into a deeper logical analysis: in an iqtirani syllogism, the conclusion (al-qawl al-akhar) is not present as a whole. But it must be present in some way for the syllogism to convey the result. The author identifies three logical possibilities, then eliminates two through careful reasoning, leaving only one valid scenario.

Guided Path
1
Enumerate the three possibilities
List the three rational possibilities for the presence of the conclusion in an iqtirani syllogism: (1) mentioned with its matter but not its form, (2) mentioned with its form but not its matter, (3) not mentioned at all.
2
Eliminate the third possibility
Argue that the third possibility is impossible because a syllogism that contains no parts of the result (neither matter nor form) cannot be conveyive of that result. If it had no connection to the conclusion, it wouldn't be a valid syllogism.
3
Eliminate the second possibility
The second possibility is impossible because form cannot exist without matter. Form is an accident ('arid) that inheres in matter; it is inseparable. If form were present without matter, that would imply an accident without a substance, which is impossible.
4
Validate the first possibility
The first possibility remains: the conclusion is present by its matter (the terms) but not by its specific form. This is possible because the matter can be present with a different form (the form of the premise relationship). The negation of a specific form does not entail the negation of form in general.
This addresses the objection raised in the marginal note.

(إسماعيل فإن قيل: الاحتمال الأول أيضاً باطل؛ لأن نفي الهيئة يستلزم نفي المادة أيضاً؛ إذ وجود المادة بدون الهيئة محال قلنا: المراد الهيئة الخاصة الواقعة في النتيجة، ولا يلزم من نفي الخاص نفي العام فيجوز أن يكون للمادة هيئة أخرى. وجود الهيئة لأن الهيئة عارضة للمادة ولازمة لها، ووجود العارض بدون المعروض واللازم بدون الملزوم محال فكيف يعقل؟ أولى وجه الأولوية الاختصار في العبارة مع حصول المقصود؛ فإنه اللائق بشأن الماتن خصوصاً لمن يكون بصدد تهذيب الكلام وتقريب المرام. إسماعيل) حدود المطلوب : أي حدود التي لها مدخل في حصوله، فلا يرد أن الوسط خارج عن المطلوب يعني لما كان القياس الاقتراني مشتملا على أداة الجمع والاقتران، وهي الواو الواصلة سمي اقترانيا.
Benefit: So it is conditional, otherwise it is connective, categorical or conditional. And the subject of the sought: If this is a human then it is an animal, but it is not an animal, so it yields that this is not a human. And what is mentioned in the syllogism is: 'this is a human'. And it may be that what is mentioned in it is the very result, as in your saying in the mentioned example: 'but it is a human' yields that this is an animal. His statement: 'so it is conditional': because it contains the exception word, namely 'but'. His statement: 'otherwise': i.e., and if the other statement is not mentioned in the syllogism with its matter and form, and that is by being mentioned with its matter but not its form; for it is not intelligible that form exists without matter, and likewise it is not intelligible that there be a syllogism without including any of the material and formal parts of the result; from this it is known that if he had omitted his saying 'with its matter', it would have been better. His statement: 'so it is connective': because of the conjunction of the limits of the sought in it, which are the minor, the major, and the middle. His statement: 'categorical': i.e., the connective syllogism is divided into categorical and conditional; if it is composed purely of categoricals, it is categorical, e.g., 'the world is changing, and everything changing is originated'. And his statement: 'and that': i.e., the negation of this totality here in reality is only conceivable by the other statement being mentioned with its matter not with its form, and not with anything else—mentioned with its matter not with its form; for the rational possibilities rise to three: first, that it is mentioned in it with its matter not its form; second, that it is mentioned with its form not its matter; third, that it is not mentioned in it neither with its matter nor its form. The third is false, for it is impossible that the syllogism not include all the material and formal parts; then it would not be conveyive to the result, as is not hidden. The second is also false; for the form that is accidental to the material parts of the result is inseparable from them, so how can form exist without matter unless it implies the subsistence of the accident without the substance and the existence of the necessary without the necessary condition, which is false. So the first possibility is determined. (Isma'il: If it is said: the first possibility is also false, because the negation of form necessitates the negation of matter also, since matter without form is impossible; we say: what is meant is the specific form occurring in the result, and the negation of the specific does not necessitate the negation of the general, for matter can have another form. The existence of form is because form is accidental to matter and necessary for it, and the existence of an accident without the substance and the necessary without the necessary condition is impossible, so how can it be conceived? The better, the reason for priority is to shorten the expression while achieving the purpose; for that befits the status of the author, especially for one who is engaged in refining speech and bringing closer the goal. Isma'il) The limits of the sought: i.e., the limits that have a role in its attainment, so it is not objected that the middle is outside the sought. That is because the connective syllogism includes the connective tool, which is the particle 'wa' (and), so it is called connective.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of the division of qiyas into istithna'i and iqtirani, and further subdivision of iqtirani into hamli (categorical) and sharti (conditional). The author first gives a 'benefit' (fa'ida) that provides a rule: if the conclusion (al-qawl al-akhar) is mentioned, it is istithna'i; otherwise, it is iqtirani. Examples of istithna'i are given using conditional statements like 'If this is a human, then it is an animal; but it is not an animal; therefore this is not a human.' In this case, the contradictory of the conclusion is mentioned. It is also possible for the very conclusion to be mentioned, as in 'but it is a human; therefore this is an animal.' The commentator explains that the word 'istithna'i' comes from the exception word 'lakinna' (but). For iqtirani, the conclusion is not mentioned with its form; the matter (terms) is present but not the specific relation. The commentator argues that it is impossible for form to exist without matter, so the conclusion can only be present by its matter, not its form. Then a detailed argument is presented: there are three possibilities for the presence of the conclusion in an iqtirani syllogism: (1) mentioned by matter only, (2) mentioned by form only, (3) not mentioned at all. The third is false because a syllogism must contain some parts of the result to be conveyive. The second is false because form cannot exist without its matter. Therefore only the first is possible. An objection is raised: if matter cannot exist without form, then even the first possibility is false. The answer distinguishes between the specific form of the result and form in general; matter can have a different form. The passage concludes by affirming that the iqtirani syllogism is called 'connective' because it uses the particle 'wa' (and) to connect the premises.

Key Terms
الاحتمالات العقلية
الهيئة
المادة
العارض
المعروض
الموصل بالذات

Checkpoint
Why is the second possibility (conclusion present by form only) considered false?
A) Because the syllogism would then have no matter
B) Because form cannot exist without matter (accident requires substance)
C) Because the syllogism would be invalid
D) Because form is always accompanied by its specific matter

Key Takeaway
In an iqtirani syllogism, the conclusion can only be present by its matter (terms) because form is an accident that requires matter, and a syllogism without any trace of the conclusion cannot be conveyive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Construct a similar three-possibility proof in the context of usul al-fiqh: The ruling of an act is either wajib, mandub, or mubah. It is not wajib. It is not mandub. Therefore it is mubah. Identify the disjunctive syllogism and explain why the elimination works.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Distinguishing Istithna'i and Iqtirani Syllogisms
Author defines istithna'i and iqtirani with material-formal condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between istithna'i and iqtirani syllogisms by identifying whether the 'other premise' is mentioned with its matter but not its form.

Core Idea
A syllogism is istithna'i iff the 'other premise' is present with its matter but missing the specific form of the conclusion.

In this passage, the author defines two fundamental types of syllogism in Islamic logic: the exceptive (istithna'i) and the conjunctive (iqtirani). The key to distinguishing them lies not merely in the presence of 'but' but in how the omitted premise relates to the conclusion's matter and form. Understanding this subtle condition is essential for correctly classifying syllogisms in your own reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Locate the two main categories
Find where the author states that a syllogism is either istithna'i or iqtirani. Notice the marker word 'otherwise' (wa illa).
The division is exhaustive: every syllogism falls into one of these two.
2
Understand the defining condition for istithna'i
The author says istithna'i includes the word of exception (but), and that the other premise must be mentioned with its matter but not its form. This means the subject and predicate of the conclusion appear, but the logical form (e.g., 'if...then') is not repeated.
The example illustrates: 'but it is not an animal' uses the same matter (human, animal) but not the conditional form.
3
Explore the three possibilities for the other premise
The commentator lists three ways the other premise could be present: (1) with matter but not form, (2) with form but not matter, (3) with neither. Determine which is feasible.
This is a rational analysis to isolate the only viable option.
4
Eliminate the impossible possibilities
Possibility (3) is impossible because a syllogism must have some matter and form. Possibility (2) is impossible because form cannot exist without matter. Thus only (1) remains, which matches the istithna'i condition.
This shows why the condition is necessary and sufficient.
5
Apply the distinction to a new example
Take a categorical syllogism: 'All A are B; all B are C; so all A are C.' This is iqtirani because all premises are explicitly stated in full. Contrast with an istithna'i: 'If A is B then C is D; but A is B; so C is D.' Here the second premise 'but A is B' has the matter but not the conditional form.
Practice identifying which type a given syllogism is.

Source Text

فائدة: فاستثنائي وإلا فاقتراني حملي أو شرطي، وموضوع المطلوب إن كان هذا إنسانا كان حيوانا لكنه ليس بحيوان ينتج أن هذا ليس بإنسان، والمذكور في القياس: هذا إنسان، وقد يكون المذكور فيه عين النتيجة كقولك في المثال المذكور: "لكنه إنسان" ينتج أن هذا حيوان قوله فاستثنائي: لاشتماله على كلمة الاستثناء أعني لكن قوله: وإلا: أي وإن لم يكن القول الآخر مذكورا في القياس بمادته وهيئته وذلك بأن يكون مذكورا بمادته لا بهيئته؛ إذ لا يعقل وجود الهيئة بدون المادة، وكذا لا يعقل قياس لا يشتمل على شيء من أجزاء النتيجة المادية والصورية؛ ومن هذا علم أنه لو حذف قوله: بمادته لكان أولى. قوله: فاقتراني: لاقتران حدود المطلوب فيه وهي الأصغر والأكبر والأوسط. قوله حملي أي القياس الاقتراني ينقسم إلى حملي وشرطي؛ لأنه إن كان مركبا من الحمليات الصرفة فحملي، نحو: العالم متغير، وكل متغير حادث فالعالم حادث، وإلا فشرطي. وذلك: أي نفي هذا المجموع ههنا في نفس الأمر إنما يتصور بأن يكون القول الآخر مذكورا بمادته لا بهيئته ولا بأمر آخر مذكورا بمادته لا بهيئته؛ فإن الاحتمالات العقلية ترتقي إلى الثلاثة الأول: أن يكون مذكورا فيه بمادته لا بهيئته، والثاني: أن يكون مذكورا بهيئته لا بمادته، والثالث: أن لا يكون مذكورا فيه لا بمادته ولا بهيئته. الثالث باطل؛ فإنه يمتنع أن يكون القياس غير مشتمل على الأجزاء المادية والصورية جميعاً؛ فإنه حينئذ لا كونه موصلا إلى النتيجة كما لا يخفى والثاني أيضاً باطل؛ فإن الهيئة العارضة للأجزاء المادية للنتيجة غير منفكة عنها، فكيف يتصور وجود الهيئة بدون المادة إلا أن يلزم ثبوت العارض بدون المعروض ووجود الملزوم بدون اللازم وهو باطل، فتعين الاحتمال الأول. (إسماعيل فإن قيل: الاحتمال الأول أيضاً باطل؛ لأن نفي الهيئة يستلزم نفي المادة أيضاً؛ إذ وجود المادة بدون الهيئة محال قلنا: المراد الهيئة الخاصة الواقعة في النتيجة، ولا يلزم من نفي الخاص نفي العام فيجوز أن يكون للمادة هيئة أخرى. وجود الهيئة لأن الهيئة عارضة للمادة ولازمة لها، ووجود العارض بدون المعروض واللازم بدون الملزوم محال فكيف يعقل؟ أولى وجه الأولوية الاختصار في العبارة مع حصول المقصود؛ فإنه اللائق بشأن الماتن خصوصاً لمن يكون بصدد تهذيب الكلام وتقريب المرام. إسماعيل) حدود المطلوب : أي حدود التي لها مدخل في حصوله، فلا يرد أن الوسط خارج عن المطلوب يعني لما كان القياس الاقتراني مشتملا على أداة الجمع والاقتران، وهي الواو الواصلة سمي اقترانيا.
Benefit: Then it is istithna'i (exceptive), otherwise it is iqtirani (conjunctive), categorical or conditional. The subject of the desired [conclusion] is [like] 'If this is a human, then it is an animal; but it is not an animal, so it yields that this is not a human', and what is mentioned in the syllogism is 'this is a human'. And sometimes what is mentioned in it is exactly the conclusion, as you say in the mentioned example: 'but it is a human' yields that this is an animal. His saying 'istithna'i' because it includes the word of exception, namely 'but'. His saying 'otherwise' meaning: if the other premise is not mentioned in the syllogism with its matter and form, such that it is mentioned with its matter but not its form; for it is inconceivable for form to exist without matter, and likewise it is inconceivable for a syllogism that does not include any of the material and formal parts of the conclusion. From this it is known that if he had omitted his saying 'with its matter' it would be more appropriate. His saying 'iqtirani' due to the conjunction of the limits of the desired [conclusion] in it, which are the minor, the major, and the middle. His saying 'categorical' meaning the conjunctive syllogism is divided into categorical and conditional; because if it is composed of pure categoricals, then it is categorical, such as: 'The world is changing, and everything changing is temporally originated, so the world is temporally originated'; otherwise it is conditional. That is: the negation of this whole here in reality is only conceivable by the other premise being mentioned with its matter but not its form, and not by something else. For the rational possibilities rise to three: first, that it be mentioned in it with its matter but not its form; second, that it be mentioned with its form but not its matter; third, that it be mentioned with neither its matter nor its form. The third is false, for it is impossible for a syllogism to lack all material and formal parts, for then it would not be a means to the conclusion, as is not hidden. The second is also false, for the form that inheres in the material parts of the conclusion is not separable from them, so how can the existence of form without matter be conceived, unless it necessitates the existence of an accident without its substrate and the existence of the necessary without the necessary, which is false. So the first possibility is determined. (Ismail: If it is said: the first possibility is also false, because the negation of form necessitates the negation of matter also, since the existence of matter without form is impossible. We say: what is meant is the specific form occurring in the conclusion, and the negation of the specific does not necessitate the negation of the general; so it is possible for the matter to have another form. The existence of form is because form is accidental to matter and necessary to it, and the existence of an accident without its substrate and the necessary without the necessary is impossible, so how can it be conceived? The more appropriate is the conciseness of expression while achieving the purpose, for it is fitting for the author, especially for one who is in the position of refining speech and making the goal approachable. Ismail) 'Limits of the desired' meaning the limits that have a role in its attainment, so it is not objected that the middle term is outside the desired. It means: since the conjunctive syllogism includes the tool of conjunction, which is the connecting 'waw', it is called 'iqtirani'.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage defines and distinguishes between two types of syllogisms: istithna'i (exceptive) and iqtirani (conjunctive). The author begins by stating that a syllogism is istithna'i if it contains the word of exception (like 'but'), otherwise it is iqtirani, which is further divided into categorical (hamli) and conditional (sharti). He then explains that for a syllogism to be istithna'i, the other premise (i.e., the one not explicitly stated in the conclusion's matter and form) must be mentioned with its matter but not its form. This is because form cannot exist without matter, and a syllogism must include material and formal parts to lead to the conclusion. The commentator then discusses three possibilities for how the other premise could be mentioned: (1) with matter but not form, (2) with form but not matter, (3) with neither. The third is impossible because a syllogism must have both matter and form. The second is impossible because form cannot separate from matter. Thus only the first possibility remains valid. A potential objection is raised: if form is negated, matter is also negated because matter cannot exist without form. The response clarifies that what is negated is the specific form of the conclusion, not all form; matter can still have another form. The passage also explains why the conjunctive syllogism is called iqtirani: because it conjoins the three limits (minor, major, middle). The commentary notes that the term 'limits of the desired' refers to those limits that contribute to the conclusion, so the middle term is not considered outside the desired.

Key Takeaway
A syllogism is istithna'i when the 'other premise' appears with the conclusion's matter but without its specific form, and this condition is both necessary and sufficient.

The Syllogism
beginner

Identifying Asghar, Akbar, Awsat in Categorical Syllogisms
Author defines the minor, major, and middle terms in a categorical syllogism and gives examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the asghar (minor), akbar (major), and awsat (middle) terms in any given categorical syllogism from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
The three terms of a categorical syllogism are defined by their logical role: subject (asghar), predicate (akbar), and the mediating term (awsat).

When you encounter a categorical syllogism in Islamic logic, you need to quickly identify which term is the minor, which is the major, and which is the middle. This lesson teaches you exactly how to do that using the examples from the classical tradition, such as the proof for the createdness of the world.

Guided Path
1
Find the conclusion (natija)
The conclusion is the statement the syllogism aims to prove. For example, in 'the world is temporally originated', this is the conclusion. Identify its subject and predicate.
2
Identify the subject of the conclusion as asghar (minor term)
The subject of the conclusion is called the minor term (asghar). In the example 'the world is temporally originated', 'the world' is the asghar.
3
Identify the predicate of the conclusion as akbar (major term)
The predicate of the conclusion is called the major term (akbar). In the example, 'temporally originated' is the akbar.
4
Find the term repeated in both premises and not in the conclusion as awsat (middle term)
The middle term (awsat) appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. In the syllogism 'the world is changing; everything changing is temporally originated', the term 'changing' appears in both premises and is absent from the conclusion, so it is the awsat.
The middle term mediates between the two extremes and is essential for the syllogism to produce knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فائدة: الحملي يسمى "أصغر" ومحموله "أكبر" والمتكرر "أوسط" : متغير حادث فالعالم حادث، وإلا فشرطي، سواء تركب من الشرطيات الصرفة نحو كلما كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود وكلما كان النهار موجودا فالعالم مضيء، فكلما كانت الشمس طالعة فالعالم مضيء، أو تركب من الحملية والشرطية نحو: كلما كان هذا الشيء إنسانا كان حيوانا، وكل حيوان جسم، فكلما كان هذا الشيء إنسانا كان جسما، والمصنف الله قدم البحث عن الاقتراني الحملي؛ لكونه أبسط من الشرطي. قوله: من الحملي: أي من الاقتراني الحملي قوله :أصغر لكون الموضوع في الأغلب أخص من المحمول وأقل أفرادا منه فيكون المحمول أكبر وأكثر أفرادا قوله والمتكرر أوسط: لتوسطه بين الطرفين. صرح في شرح من الشرطيات الصرفة فيه ثلاثة احتمالات :الأول أن يكون مركبا من الشرطيتين المتصلتين، والثاني: أن يكون مركبا من المنفصلتين، والثالث: أن يكون مركبا من متصلة ومنفصلة. وفيما يتركب من الحملية والشرطية احتمالان: الأول: ما يتركب من الحملية والمتصلة والثاني ما يكون مركبا منهما ومن المنفصلة، فالاحتمالات كلها في القياس الشرطي ترتقي إلى خمسة، فمثال الاثنين مذكور في الشرح، وأمثلة البواقي ظاهرة بأدنى تأمل. إسماعيل) لكونه أبسط أي لكون بعض أفراده وهو الاقتراني الحملي أبسط وأقل أجزاء من أفراد الاستثنائي مطلقا؛ ولأن مباحث الاقتراني أبسط وأوفر من مباحث الاستثنائي، فقوله : "أبسط" على الأول من البساطة، وعلى الثاني من البسط. فافهم من الحملي فيه أن هذه الاصطلاحات لا تختص بالاقتراني الحملي، وهو ما كان مركبا من حمليتين صرفة بل يجري في الاقترانيات الشرطية، وهي ما لم يكن كذلك، كما به المصنف الرسالة، فالأولى أن يقول : المحكوم عليه في المطلوب يسمى أصغر والمحكوم به أكبر. (برهان) في الأغلب أخص: إنما قيد بقوله: في الأغلب؛ لأن الموضوع قد يكون مساويا للمحمول كما يقال: كل إنسان ناطق، وكل ناطق ضاحك، فكل إنسان ضاحك، وقد يكون أعم منه كما يقال بعض الحيوان إنسان، وكل إنسان ضاحك، فبعض الحيوان ضاحك. إسماعيل) والمتكرر أوسط: اعلم أن المجهول التصوري يكون مجهولا بكنهه ورسمه، فيطلب كنهه ورسمه والمجهول التصديقي إنما يكون مجهولا من حيث النسبة بين طرفيها يعني لا يعلم أن نسبة الأكبر إلى الأصغر إيجابي أو سلبي، والعلم ههنا لا يحصل بمجرد الطرفين وإلا لم يكن نظريا، فلا بد من أمر ثالث يناسب الطرفين؛ إذ لو لم يكن نسبة إلى شيء منهما أو كان له نسبة إلى أحد منهما دون الآخر لا يحصل منه النسبة بين الطرفين وإن كنت على خفاء من ذلك فلم تحتاج إلى المشاطة والدلالة في وصال المحبوب
Benefit: The categorical [syllogism] is called 'minor term' (asghar) and its predicate 'major term' (akbar) and the repeated [term] 'middle term' (awsat): 'changing, temporally originated, so the world is temporally originated'. Otherwise it is conditional, whether it is composed of pure conditionals like 'Whenever the sun is shining, daytime exists; and whenever daytime exists, the world is illuminated; so whenever the sun is shining, the world is illuminated', or composed of categoricals and conditionals like 'Whenever this thing is a human, it is an animal; and every animal is a body; so whenever this thing is a human, it is a body'. And the author (may Allah prolong his benefit) presented the discussion of the conjunctive categorical first because it is simpler than the conditional. His saying 'from the categorical' means from the conjunctive categorical. His saying 'minor term' because the subject is in most cases more specific than the predicate and has fewer individuals than it, so the predicate is larger and has more individuals. His saying 'and the repeated term is middle term' because it mediates between the two extremes. He stated in the commentary: 'Of the pure conditionals there are three possibilities: first, that it be composed of two conditional propositions; second, that it be composed of two disjunctive propositions; third, that it be composed of a conditional and a disjunctive. And in what is composed of categoricals and conditionals there are two possibilities: first, what is composed of a categorical and a conditional; second, what is composed of them and a disjunctive. So the possibilities in the conditional syllogism amount to five. The example of two [types] is mentioned in the commentary; the examples of the rest are apparent with minimal contemplation. (Ismail) 'Because it is simpler' means because some of its individuals, namely the conjunctive categorical, is simpler and has fewer parts than individuals of the exceptive syllogism absolutely; and because the topics of the conjunctive are simpler and more abundant than the topics of the exceptive. So his saying 'simpler' in the first sense is from simplicity, and in the second sense from expansion. So understand from the categorical that these terminologies are not specific to the conjunctive categorical, i.e., what is composed of two pure categoricals, but also apply to conjunctive conditional syllogisms, which are not like that, as indicated by the author in the epistle. So it would be better for him to say: the subject of judgment in the desired [conclusion] is called minor term and the predicate is called major term. (Burhan) 'In most cases more specific': he qualified with his saying 'in most cases' because the subject may be equal to the predicate, as is said: 'Every human is rational; and every rational is capable of laughter; so every human is capable of laughter', and it may be more general than it, as is said: 'Some animal is a human; and every human is capable of laughter; so some animal is capable of laughter.' (Ismail) 'And the repeated term is middle term': Know that the conceptual unknown (majhul tasawwuri) is unknown in its essence and description, so its essence and description are sought; while the assertive unknown (majhul tasdiqi) is unknown only in terms of the relation between its two sides, meaning it is not known whether the relation of the major to the minor is affirmative or negative. And knowledge here is not obtained by the two extremes alone, otherwise it would not be theoretical; so a third thing related to both extremes is necessary. For if there were no relation to either of them, or if it had a relation to one but not the other, the relation between the two extremes would not be obtained. And if you are in obscurity about that, then you do not need the combing and the indication in the union of the beloved.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage defines the technical terms used in the categorical (hamli) conjunctive syllogism: the subject term is called 'asghar' (minor), the predicate term 'akbar' (major), and the term repeated in both premises 'awsat' (middle). The author provides an example: 'The world is changing; everything changing is temporally originated; therefore the world is temporally originated.' Here, 'world' is the minor term, 'temporally originated' is the major term, and 'changing' is the middle term. The reason for these names is that in most cases the subject (minor) is more specific and has fewer individuals than the predicate (major), which is more general and has more individuals; the middle term mediates between them. The passage then compares the categorical syllogism with the conditional (sharti) syllogism, noting that the categorical is simpler, so the author presented it first. The commentator explains that 'simpler' (abst) can mean either 'simpler in parts' or 'more expanded in topics,' depending on context. It is clarified that these terminologies (asghar, akbar, awsat) are not exclusive to pure categorical conjunctive syllogisms but also apply to conditional conjunctive syllogisms. The commentator also notes that in the definition, the author qualified 'in most cases' because sometimes the subject may be equal to the predicate (e.g., 'Every human is rational; every rational is capable of laughter; so every human is capable of laughter') or more general (e.g., 'Some animal is a human; every human is capable of laughter; so some animal is capable of laughter'). Finally, the passage discusses the nature of the unknown in syllogisms: the conceptual unknown concerns the essence of a thing, while the assertive unknown concerns the relation between two terms, and a third term (the middle) is needed to obtain knowledge of that relation.

Key Terms
أصغر (asghar)
أكبر (akbar)
أوسط (awsat)
حملي (hamli)
شرطي (sharti)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the syllogism: 'Every human is rational; every rational is capable of laughter; therefore every human is capable of laughter,' which term is the 'awsat' (middle term)?
A) Human
B) Rational
C) Capable of laughter
D) Laughter

Key Takeaway
The asghar, akbar, and awsat terms are defined by their roles in the conclusion and premises, not by inherent generality – though the asghar is usually more specific.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the following syllogism from fiqh: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; every nabidh (date wine) is an intoxicant; therefore every nabidh is forbidden.' Identify the asghar, akbar, and awsat terms.
?
Create your own categorical syllogism on an Islamic topic (e.g., from kalam, fiqh, or usul al-fiqh) and explicitly label the minor, major, and middle terms.

The Syllogism
beginner
5 min
The Three Terms: Minor, Major, and Middle
Defines the three key terms of a syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the minor, major, and middle terms in any categorical syllogism and explain why the middle term is necessary.

Core Idea
The middle term is the mediator that ties the minor and major together.

This passage from the commentary on al-Tahzeeb introduces the fundamental terminology of syllogistic logic. Every categorical syllogism has a subject (minor), a predicate (major), and a term that appears in both premises but not the conclusion (middle). Recognizing these parts is the first step to evaluating any syllogism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion (natija)
Look for the statement that is being proved—usually the last sentence in the syllogism.
2
Find the subject and predicate in the conclusion
The subject of the conclusion is the minor (asghar); the predicate is the major (akbar).
3
Locate the repeated term in the premises
Find the term that appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. That is the middle (awsat).
4
Understand why the middle is necessary
Without the middle term, the minor and major cannot be connected, and no inference can be drawn.
The commentator explains that the middle mediates between the two extremes.

Benefit: The categorical is called 'minor' and its predicate 'major' and the repeated 'middle': variable occurring, so the world is occurring; otherwise conditional, whether it is composed of pure conditionals like: Whenever the sun is rising, daytime exists, and whenever daytime exists, the world is illuminated, so whenever the sun is rising, the world is illuminated; or composed of categorical and conditional like: Whenever this thing is a human, it is an animal, and every animal is a body, so whenever this thing is a human, it is a body. His statement: 'and the repeated is middle' because it mediates between the two extremes. He explicitly stated in the commentary: from pure conditionals there are three possibilities: first, that it is composed of two conditionals conjunctive; second, composed of two disjunctive; third, composed of conjunctive and disjunctive. Regarding what is composed of categorical and conditional, there are two possibilities: first, what is composed of categorical and conjunctive; second, what is composed of them and disjunctive. Thus all possibilities in conditional syllogism amount to five. The example of the two is mentioned in the commentary, and examples of the rest are apparent with minimal reflection. (Isma'il) 'because it is simpler' i.e. because some of its individuals, namely the categorical conjunctive, are simpler and have fewer parts than individuals of the exceptive absolutely; and because the investigations of the conjunctive are simpler and more abundant than those of the exceptive. So his statement 'simpler' is, in the first sense, from simplicity, and in the second sense, from expansion. Understand from 'from the categorical' that these terminologies are not exclusive to the categorical conjunctive, which is composed of two pure categoricals, but apply to conditional conjunctives, which are not like that, as the author did in the epistle. So it is better to say: the subject in the sought conclusion is called minor, and the predicate major. (Burhan) 'in most cases more specific': He qualified with 'in most cases' because the subject may be equal to the predicate, as is said: every human is rational, and every rational is laughing, so every human is laughing; and it may be more general, as is said: some animal is human, and every human is laughing, so some animal is laughing. (Isma'il) 'and the repeated is middle': Know that the conceptual unknown is unknown in its essence and description, so its essence and description are sought; the assertoric unknown is unknown only in terms of the relation between its two extremities, meaning one does not know whether the relation of the major to the minor is affirmative or negative. And knowledge here is not obtained by the two extremities alone, otherwise it would not be theoretical. So a third thing that is appropriate for the two extremities is necessary; for if it had no relation to either of them or had a relation to one without the other, the relation between the two extremities would not be obtained. Even if you are in obscurity about that, you do not need to comb and indicate in reaching the beloved.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary that defines key terminology of categorical syllogisms and then classifies conditional syllogisms into five types based on their composition. The author begins by stating a 'benefit' (fa'ida) that in a categorical syllogism, the subject of the conclusion is called 'minor' (asghar), its predicate 'major' (akbar), and the term repeated in both premises is called 'middle' (awsat). This is illustrated with examples: if the world is changing and occurring, then it is occurring — a simple categorical. Then it explains conditional syllogisms, which can be composed of pure conditionals (e.g., 'When the sun rises, day exists; when day exists, the world is illuminated; therefore when the sun rises, the world is illuminated') or a mix of categorical and conditional premises (e.g., 'When this is a human, it is an animal; every animal is a body; therefore when this is a human, it is a body'). The author notes that the text prioritized the categorical conjunctive syllogism because it is simpler than the conditional. The commentary then explains that the terms 'minor', 'major', and 'middle' are not exclusive to pure categoricals but also apply to conditional conjunctives. It clarifies that the subject is called 'minor' because it is often more specific and has fewer individuals than the predicate, though this is not always the case (e.g., 'some animal is human' where the subject is more general). The middle term is so called because it mediates between the minor and major. Finally, the passage discusses that in conditional syllogisms, there are five possibilities: three when composed of pure conditionals (two conjunctives, two disjunctives, or one of each) and two when mixed with categoricals (categorical with conjunctive, or categorical with disjunctive). Examples for two are given, and the rest are said to be apparent.

أكبر
أوسط

The Syllogism

The Five Types of Conditional Syllogisms
Classifies conditional syllogisms into five types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any conditional syllogism into one of five types based on its composition (pure conditionals or mixed with categoricals).

Core Idea
Conditional syllogisms come in exactly five possible compositions, each with its own structure.

After explaining the basic terminology of categorical syllogisms, the commentator turns to conditional syllogisms (al-qiyas al-sharti). He enumerates the possible ways such a syllogism can be constructed, arriving at five distinct types. Understanding this classification helps in analyzing the validity of conditional arguments in Islamic disciplines.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish pure conditional from mixed
Check whether all premises are conditional statements or if one is categorical.
2
For pure conditionals, identify the types of conditionals
Determine if the premises are both conjunctive (muttasil), both disjunctive (munfasil), or one of each.
Pure conditionals have three subtypes.
3
For mixed syllogisms, note the categorical premise
Identify whether the categorical premise is combined with a conjunctive or a disjunctive conditional.
Mixed syllogisms have two subtypes.
4
Count the total possibilities
Add the three pure subtypes and the two mixed subtypes to get five.
5
Practice constructing examples
Create an example for each type to see how the structure works.
The commentator says examples for two are given; the rest are obvious.

The Four Figures of the Syllogism
Defines the four figures based on middle term position

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four figures of the categorical syllogism based on the position of the middle term and explain why the first figure is considered the most natural.

Core Idea
The first figure is intuitive; the others are derived by rearranging the middle term.

In this lesson, we examine the four figures of the categorical syllogism as defined by classical logicians. The passage from the Tahzeeb al-Mantiq explains how the position of the middle term (al-awsat) in the premises determines the figure. Understanding this classification is fundamental for evaluating syllogistic validity, especially in Islamic theological arguments where the first figure is the gold standard.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three terms
In a categorical syllogism, there are three terms: the subject (minor term, al-asghar) of the conclusion, the predicate (major term, al-akbar) of the conclusion, and the middle term (al-awsat) that connects them. First, locate these in a given syllogism.
For example, in the syllogism 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; every nabidh is intoxicant; therefore nabidh is forbidden', the minor term is 'nabidh', the major term is 'forbidden', and the middle term is 'intoxicant'.
2
Determine the position of the middle term in each premise
In the minor premise, note whether the middle term is the subject or predicate. Do the same for the major premise. This gives four possible arrangements.
3
Apply the figure definitions
Using the positions, classify the figure: (1) middle predicate of minor and subject of major → first figure; (2) predicate of both → second; (3) subject of both → third; (4) subject of minor and predicate of major → fourth.
The first figure is the only one where the middle term naturally connects from minor to major without reversing roles.
4
Understand the natural order and intuition of the first figure
The first figure follows the natural order: minor → middle → major. This order preserves the subject as subject and predicate as predicate in the conclusion. The other figures require reduction to the first figure to be validated.
The passage states that the first figure's production is intuitive (badihi) because of this natural order.
5
Appreciate the analogies
The passage gives two analogies: (a) a square: premises as one side, conclusion as opposite, common subject as third side, common predicate as fourth; (b) a family: minor premise as mother, major as father, middle as transferred matter, conclusion as child. These analogies help visualize the logical relations.

الأشكال الأربعة
وما فيه الأصغر "صغرى" والأكبر "كبرى"، والأوسط إما محمول الصغرى وموضوع الكبرى فهو الشكل الأول أو محمولهما فالثاني أو موضوعهما فالثالث أو عكس الأول فالرابع،
:قوله وما فيه الأصغر : أي المقدمة التي فيها الأصغر، وتذكير الضمير نظرا إلى لفظ الموصول. قوله: صغرى: لاشتمالها على الأصغر . قوله كبرى أي ما فيه الأكبر كبرى؛ لاشتمالها على الأكبر قوله: الشكل الأول: يسمى أولا؛ لأن إنتاجه بديهي وإنتاج البواقي نظري يرجع إليه، فيكون أسبق وأقدم في العلم قوله فالثاني: لاشتراكه مع الأول في أشرف المقدمتين أعني الصغرى. قوله : فالثالث: لاشتراكه مع الأول في أخس المقدمتين أعني الكبرى.
الشكل: نقل عن أبي العباس اللوكري تسميته بالشكل من قبيل أنه شبه بالشكل المربع من أشكال الهندسة؛ وذلك أن المقدمتين المقترنتين على استقامة شبهتا بضلع واحد من أضلاع المربع، والنتيجة شبهت بالضلع الذي يقابله، واشتراك موضوع المقدمة الصغرى وموضوع النتيجة شبه بالضلع الثالث، واشتراك محمول المقدمة الكبرى ومحمول النتيجة شبه بالضلع الرابع المقابل للثالث فتسمية القياس بالشكل على طريق التشبيه. قال الصدر الشيرازي في حواشي شرح حكمة "الإشراق" بعد نقل هذا الكلام وكذا تسمية الصغرى بالأم والكبرى بالأب والحد الأوسط بالمادة الفضلية المتكررة المنتقلة من ظهر الأب إلى بطن الأم، سيما إذا كان متوسطا بين محمول الصغرى وموضوع الكبرى كما في السياق الأتم والنتيجة بالولد كلام تشبيهي في غاية الحسن نقلا عن "شرح المرقاة" للمحقق الخير آبادي. لأن إنتاجه إلخ ولأن الأوسط في الشكل الأول على ترتيب وضع المطلوب، فموضوعه فيه موضوع و محموله فيه محمول، فهو أقرب من الأشكال في الشكل إليه، فجعل مرتبته أولى وفي الثاني موضوعه الذي هو أشرف الأجزاء باق على ما كان فصار مرتبته ،ثانية وفي الثالث محموله باق على ما كان فصار مرتبته ثالثة، وفي الرابع ليس شيء من جزئي المطلوب على حاله فجعلت مرتبة رابعة. بديهي لكونه على النظم الطبيعي، وهو أن ينتقل من الأصغر إلى الأوسط ومن الأوسط إلى الأكبر؛ لئلا يتغير حال الأصغر والأكبر عما هما عليه في النتيجة، وهذا النظم إنما هو في الشكل الأول؛ فلذا وضع في المرتبة الأولى. أعني الصغرى لكونها مشتملة على أشرف طرفي المطلوب أعني الموضوع؛ لأنه ذات وأصل والمحمول حال وتابع له والذات أشرف من الصفة والمتبوع من التابع ومن ههنا ظهر كون الكبرى أخس المقدمتين؛ لكونها مشتملة على أخس طرفي المطلوب أعني المحمول الذي هو حال وتابع للموضوع. إسماعيل)
The Four Figures. And that which contains the minor is 'minor premise' and the major is 'major premise', and the middle is either the predicate of the minor and subject of the major — that is the first figure; or the predicate of both — that is the second; or the subject of both — that is the third; or the opposite of the first — that is the fourth. His statement: 'and that which contains the minor' i.e. the premise that contains the minor, and the masculine pronoun is due to the form of the relative. His statement: 'minor premise' because it includes the minor. His statement: 'major premise' i.e. that which contains the major is major premise because it includes the major. His statement: 'the first figure' is called first because its production is intuitive and the production of the rest is theoretical and returns to it, so it is prior and earlier in knowledge. His statement: 'then the second' because it shares with the first the nobler of the two premises, namely the minor premise. His statement: 'then the third' because it shares with the first the baser of the two premises, namely the major premise. Figure: It is narrated from Abu al-Abbas al-Lawkari that its naming as 'figure' is by way of analogy with the square figure from geometry: the two premises placed in a line are likened to one side of the square, the conclusion is likened to the opposite side, the commonality of the subject of the minor premise and the subject of the conclusion is likened to the third side, and the commonality of the predicate of the major premise and the predicate of the conclusion is likened to the fourth side opposite the third. Thus naming the syllogism as 'figure' is by way of analogy. al-Sadr al-Shirazi said in his glosses on the commentary of Hikmat al-Ishraq after relating this speech: likewise naming the minor premise as mother, the major premise as father, the middle term as the superfluous recurring matter that transfers from the back of the father to the womb of the mother, especially when it mediates between the predicate of the minor and subject of the major as in the complete context, and the conclusion as child — this is analogical speech of the utmost beauty, transmitted from 'Sharh al-Marqat' by al-Muhaqqiq al-Khayrabadi. 'Because its production etc.': And because the middle in the first figure is in the order of the sought conclusion, so its subject is the subject and its predicate is the predicate, so it is the closest of the figures to it, thus its rank is made first. In the second, its subject which is the nobler part remains as it was, so its rank becomes second. In the third, its predicate remains as it was, so its rank becomes third. In the fourth, none of the two parts of the sought conclusion is in its state, so its rank is made fourth. 'Intuitive' because it is on the natural order, which is to move from the minor to the middle and from the middle to the major, so that the state of the minor and major does not change from what they are in the conclusion. This order is only in the first figure; therefore it is placed in the first rank. 'I mean the minor premise' because it includes the nobler of the two ends of the sought conclusion, namely the subject; for it is essence and origin, and the predicate is state and dependent on it, and essence is nobler than attribute, and the followed is nobler than the follower. From here it becomes apparent that the major premise is the baser of the two premises because it includes the baser of the two ends, namely the predicate which is state and dependent on the subject. (Isma'il)
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage defines the four figures of the categorical syllogism, based on the position of the middle term (al-awsat) in the two premises. The figure is determined by whether the middle term is subject or predicate in each premise. The first figure occurs when the middle is predicate of the minor premise and subject of the major. The second figure when it is predicate of both premises. The third when it is subject of both. The fourth when it is the opposite of the first (i.e., subject of minor and predicate of major). The commentary explains that the first figure is called 'first' because its validity is intuitive (badihi), while the other figures' validity is theoretical and must be reduced to the first. It also explains the order of precedence: the second figure shares with the first the noble minor premise (subject), the third shares the baser major premise (predicate), and the fourth has neither in the original order. An interesting analogy is cited from Abu al-Abbas al-Lawkari, comparing the syllogism to a square: the two premises are like one side, the conclusion the opposite side, the common subject of minor and conclusion a third side, and the common predicate of major and conclusion the fourth side. Another analogy from al-Sadr al-Shirazi compares the minor premise to mother, major to father, middle to the transferred matter, and conclusion to child. The passage concludes by emphasizing that the first figure has the natural order (al-nazm al-tabi'i) from minor to middle to major, preserving the roles of subject and predicate in the conclusion.

Key Takeaway
The four figures of the categorical syllogism are distinguished by the position of the middle term, with the first figure being the only intuitive one because it follows the natural order from minor to middle to major.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Conditions of the First Figure: Why the Minor Must Be Affirmative and Actual
Commentator explains conditions of first figure and defends against an objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a minor premise in a first-figure syllogism satisfies the conditions of being affirmative and actual, and distinguish a purely affirmative premise from an affirmative with a negative predicate.

Core Idea
The first figure requires pure affirmation and actuality in the minor—apparent exceptions involve affirmatives in force of negation.

The first figure of the categorical syllogism is the most powerful but also the most restrictive. In this passage, the commentator explains why the minor premise must be affirmative and actual (not merely potential) and why the major must be universal. He then addresses an objection that seems to show a negative minor working, revealing a crucial nuance: a premise like 'Void is not existent' is actually an affirmative with a negative predicate, not a pure negative. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly evaluating syllogistic validity in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three conditions for the first figure
The passage states: إيجاب الصغرى و فعليتها مع كلية الكبرى. This means the minor must be affirmative (subject affirmed of middle), actual (subject actually has the middle), and the major must be universal.
These conditions ensure the syllogism's validity.
2
Understand why the minor must be affirmative and actual
The judgment in the major premise applies only to what actually possesses the middle term. If the minor does not affirm actual possession, the judgment cannot transfer to the minor term. فلو لم يحكم في الصغرى بأن الأصغر يثبت له الأوسط بالفعل لم يلزم تعدى الحكم.
فعلية (actuality) prevents merely potential possession.
3
Understand why the major must be universal
The middle term is predicated of the minor. If the major only judges some of the middle, the minor might not be included in that part. كلية الكبرى guarantees that the minor falls under the entire middle term.
Particular major leaves a gap.
4
Analyze the objection and the response
The objection: 'Void is not existent; everything not existent is not perceptible; therefore void is not perceptible.' This seems to have a negative minor. The commentator responds that the minor is actually an affirmative with a negative predicate (الخلاء ليس بموجود is affirmative with predicate 'not existent'). The middle term is 'not existent', not 'existent'. Hence the minor is not purely affirmative but in force of negation. Pure affirmation (الإيجاب الصرف) is required.
The distinction between a negative premise and an affirmative with negative predicate is key.
