شرائط الشكل الأول
ويشترط في الأول إيجاب الصغرى و فعليتها مع كلية الكبرى؛
:قوله فالرابع لكونه في غاية البعد عن الأول قوله وفعليتها ليتعدى الحكم من الأوسط إلى الأصغر؛ وذلك لأن الحكم في الكبرى إيجابا كان أو سلبا إنما هو على ما يثبت له الأوسط بالفعل بناء على مذهب الشيخ، فلو لم يحكم في الصغرى بأن الأصغر يثبت له الأوسط بالفعل لم يلزم تعدى الحكم من الأوسط إلى الأصغر قوله مع كلية الكبرى ليلزم اندراج الأصغر في الأوسط، فيلزم من الحكم على الأوسط الحكم على الأصغر؛ وذلك لأن الأوسط يكون محمولا هنا على الأصغر، ويجوز أن يكون المحمول أعم من الموضوع، فلو حكم في الكبرى على بعض الأوسط لاحتمل أن يكون الأصغر غير مندرج في ذلك البعض، فلا يلزم من الحكم على ذلك البعض
و فعليتها ولو قال: إيجاب الصغرى فعليتها لكان أولى؛ لأنه بهذا الدليل كما ثبت كون فعلية الصغرى شرطا لإنتاج الشكل الأول كذلك يثبت كون إيجابها شرطا له يعني اشترط لإنتاج الشكل الأول إيجاب الصغرى؛ ليتجاوز الحكم بالأكبر على الأوسط في الكبرى إلى الأصغر بواسطة الأوسط.
"أن الخلاء ليس
وذلك إلخ يعني إن تجاوز الحكم المذكور إنما يتصور إذا كانت الصغرى موجبة وفعلية؛ لأن الحكم في الكبرى إيجابا أو سلبا إنما هو على ما ثبت له الأوسط، فلو لم يكن في الصغرى كذلك لم يتجاوز الحكم الذي بالأكبر على ما ثبت له الأوسط بالفعل إلى الأصغر كما لا يخفى. (عبد) يثبت له الأوسط وهذا إنما يتصور إذا كانت الصغرى موجبة فعلية؛ إذ لولا ذلك لم يلزم تعدي الحكم من الأوسط إلى الأصغر. فإن قيل: لا نسلم أن الصغرى لو لم تكن موجبة لما تعدى الحكم بالأكبر من الأوسط إلى الأصغر للأمرين؛ لأن قولنا الخلاء ليس بموجود، وكل ما ليس بموجود ليس محسوس، ينتج محسوس" مع أن الصغرى سالبة. قلنا: إن الصغرى موجبة سالبة المحمول، يعني إن الخلاء موضوع وليس موجود مع أداة السلب محموله لا موجود فقط، فالحد الأوسط هو ليس موجود بشهادة أن النسبة السلبية جعلت مرآة للأفراد في الكبرى أي جعل ليس بموجود فقط. والجواب التوفيقي أن الإيجاب الصرف الذي لم يكن في قوة السلب شرط في صغرى الشكل الأول والموجبة السالبة المحمول في قوة السالبة والإنتاج في مادة دون مادة لا يناسب عموم قواعد الفن. وكل حجر "جسم، ينتج الإنسان ليس بجسم، وهو فقولنا: "الإنسان ليس بحجر، كاذب؛ لكون الصغرى سالبة المحمول.
Conditions of the first figure. It is required in the first figure that the minor premise be affirmative and actual, along with the universality of the major premise. His statement: 'the fourth because it is at the utmost distance from the first'; his statement: 'and its actuality' so that the judgment transfers from the middle to the minor. That is because the judgment in the major premise, whether affirmative or negative, is only on that for which the middle term is actually established, according to the doctrine of the teacher. So if it were not judged in the minor that the minor term is actually established for the middle, the transfer of judgment from the middle to the minor would not be necessary. His statement: 'with the universality of the major premise' to necessitate the inclusion of the minor under the middle, so the judgment on the middle entails the judgment on the minor. That is because the middle here is predicated of the minor, and it is possible for the predicate to be more general than the subject. So if the judgment in the major were on some of the middle, it would be possible that the minor is not included in that part, and then the judgment on that part would not necessitate the judgment on the minor. 'And its actuality' — if he had said: 'the affirmation of the minor's actuality' it would have been better, because by this proof, just as the actuality of the minor is established as a condition for the first figure to produce, so also its affirmation is established as a condition. He means: it is required for the first figure to produce that the minor be affirmative, so that the judgment of the major on the middle in the major premise passes to the minor through the middle. 'That void is not...' and so on means that the transfer of the mentioned judgment is only conceivable if the minor is affirmative and actual; because the judgment in the major, affirmative or negative, is only on what the middle is established for. So if it were not so in the minor, the judgment of the major on what is actually established for the middle would not transfer to the minor, as is not hidden. (Abd) 'is established for the middle' and this is only conceivable if the minor is affirmative and actual; because otherwise the transfer of judgment from the middle to the minor would not be necessary. If it is said: We do not concede that if the minor were not affirmative, the judgment of the major would not transfer from the middle to the minor for two reasons; because our statement 'void is not existent, and everything not existent is not perceptible' produces 'not perceptible' even though the minor is negative. We say: The minor is affirmative with a negative predicate, meaning that 'void' is the subject and 'not existent' with the negative particle is its predicate, not just 'nonexistent'. So the middle term is 'not existent', evidence being that the negative relation was made a mirror for individuals in the major, i.e., it made 'not existent' only. And the conciliatory answer is that pure affirmation which is not in the force of negation is a condition in the minor of the first figure, and the affirmative with negative predicate is in the force of negation, and production in some matter but not others does not suit the generality of the rules of the art. And every stone is a body, produces 'human is not a body', and our statement 'human is not stone' is false because the minor is affirmative with negative predicate.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the conditions of the first figure of the categorical syllogism. The commentator explains that the first figure requires the minor premise to be affirmative and actual (i.e., the subject actually has the middle term), and the major premise must be universal. The reasoning is that the judgment in the major premise (whether affirmative or negative) applies only to those individuals that actually possess the middle term. If the minor does not actually affirm the middle of the minor term, the judgment cannot transfer. Similarly, the major must be universal so that the minor term is guaranteed to be included in the middle term; if the major were particular, the minor might not be under the part of the middle that is judged. The commentator then addresses an objection: an example like 'Void is not existent; everything not existent is not perceptible' seems to produce a valid conclusion ('Void is not perceptible') with a negative minor and a universal major. The response is that the minor 'Void is not existent' is actually an affirmative with a negative predicate, where the middle term is 'not existent'. Such an affirmative in force of negation does not satisfy the condition of pure affirmation required for the first figure. A pure affirmative minor must be without the force of negation. Thus the condition stands.

Key Terms
إيجاب الصغرى (ījāb al-ṣughrā): The minor premise must be affirmative (subject is predicated of the middle term). [Note: Consistent across logical texts.]
فعلية الصغرى (fiʿliyyat al-ṣughrā): The minor premise must assert that the subject actually possesses the middle term (not merely potentially). [Note: This concept is emphasized in post-Avicennan logic to ensure transitivity.]
كلية الكبرى (kulliyyat al-kubrā): The major premise must be universal (all or none of the middle term). [Note: Standard condition across all figures.]
الأوسط (al-awsaṭ): The middle term that mediates between the minor and major terms. [Note: Unchanged.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The first figure requires the minor to be purely affirmative and actual; premises that are affirmative in force of negation do not satisfy the condition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Syllogism

Productive Moods and the Weaker Premise Rule in the First Figure
Commentator outlines the productive moods of the first figure and the rule that the conclusion follows the weaker premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four productive moods of the first figure and apply the rule that the conclusion follows the weaker premise.

Core Idea
Only four premise combinations yield a conclusion in the first figure, and that conclusion always follows the weaker premise.

In the previous lesson, you learned the necessary conditions for a productive first-figure syllogism: the minor premise must be affirmative and actual, and the major must be universal. Now we explore which specific combinations of premises (moods) are valid and how the conclusion is determined. The commentator also introduces a powerful general rule: the conclusion always follows the weaker premise—whether weaker in quantity (particular) or quality (negative). Mastering this rule will allow you to check any syllogism instantly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditions
Recall that the first figure requires an affirmative actual minor and a universal major. Without these, the figure is sterile. The conditions restrict the possible premise combinations from sixteen to four.
These conditions are the foundation for the productive moods.
2
List the four valid premise combinations
With the conditions applied, the valid minor-major pairs are: (1) universal affirmative + universal affirmative, (2) particular affirmative + universal affirmative, (3) universal affirmative + universal negative, (4) particular affirmative + universal negative. These are the four productive moods of the first figure.
Any other combination is invalid.
3
Apply the weaker premise rule
The conclusion's quantity and quality depend on the 'weaker' premise. Particularity is weaker than universality, and negation is weaker than affirmation. If both occur, the conclusion is negative particular. If only particularity, it is affirmative particular. If only negation, it is negative universal. If neither, it is universal affirmative.
This rule works for the first figure and most others, with some exceptions in the third and fourth figures.
4
Verify with Islamic examples
Construct examples from Islamic sciences. For mood (1): 'Every intoxicant is forbidden, every khamr is an intoxicant, therefore every khamr is forbidden' (Barbara). For mood (3): 'No intoxicant is permissible to consume, every khamr is an intoxicant, therefore no khamr is permissible to consume' (Celarent). Check that the conclusion matches the rule.
Always use real Islamic rulings to test your understanding.

لينتج الموجبتان مع الموجبة الكلية
الحكم على الأصغر كما يشاهد في قولك كل إنسان حيوان، وبعض الحيوان فرس. قوله: لينتج الموجبتان أي الكلية والجزئية واللام فيه للغاية، أي أثر هذه الشروط أن ينتج الصغرى الموجبة الكلية والموجبة الجزئية مع الكبرى الموجبة الكلية الموجبتين، ففي الأول تكون النتيجة موجبة كلية، وفي الثاني موجبة جزئية، وأن ينتج الصغريان الموجبتان مع السالبة الكلية الكبرى: السالبتين الكلية والجزئية على ما سبق تفصيله، وأمثلة الكل واضحة.

So that the two affirmatives produce with the affirmative universal — judgment on the minor as observed in your statement: 'Every human is an animal, and some animal is a horse.' His statement: 'So that the two affirmatives produce' meaning the universal and the particular, and the laam is for purpose, i.e., the effect of these conditions is that the universal affirmative minor and the particular affirmative minor with the universal affirmative major produce the two affirmatives; in the first the conclusion is universal affirmative, in the second particular affirmative, and that the two affirmative minors with the universal negative major produce the two negatives, universal and particular, as previously detailed, and examples of all are clear. In the first: like 'Every human is an animal', 'Every animal is a body', so 'Every human is a body'; and 'Some animal is human', 'Every human is laughing', so some animal is laughing. (Abd) 'And examples of all' etc. Its summary: the rational possibilities were sixteen, resulting from multiplying the four minors by the four majors likewise. From the condition of affirmation of the minor, eight drop out: the two negative minors with the four majors; and from the universality of the major, four drop out: the two particular majors with the two affirmative minors. Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the first figure, now focusing on which moods are productive given the conditions. The commentator explains that with the conditions (affirmative actual minor, universal major), four valid moods arise: (1) universal affirmative minor with universal affirmative major gives universal affirmative conclusion; (2) particular affirmative minor with universal affirmative major gives particular affirmative; (3) universal affirmative minor with universal negative major gives universal negative; (4) particular affirmative minor with universal negative major gives particular negative. The example 'Every human is an animal, some animal is a horse' is used to show a non-productive combination because the major premise is particular. The commentator then introduces the general rule that the conclusion follows the weaker premise: particularity and negation are the 'weaker' qualities. If both occur, the conclusion is negative particular; if only particularity, then affirmative particular; if only negation, then negative universal. This rule applies to all figures except some moods of the third and fourth. Finally, the commentator summarizes the sixteen possible combinations of minors and majors, and explains that only four survive after applying the conditions. A table is referenced with symbols: 'ب' for remaining moods, with abbreviations for conclusion types (نم for universal affirmative, etc.). This passage emphasizes the importance of memorizing the rule about the weaker premise.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Conditions of the First Figure and Its Valid Moods
Author states conditions and gives examples of valid moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two conditions for a valid first-figure syllogism (affirmative minor and universal major) and determine whether a given pair of premises yields a valid conclusion.

Core Idea
The first figure requires an affirmative minor and a universal major — all other combinations are invalid.

The first figure of the categorical syllogism is the most powerful because its conclusion follows immediately once its conditions are met. In this lesson, you will learn the exact conditions that make a first-figure syllogism valid, and see how they produce the four valid moods. Understanding these conditions is essential before you can trust any syllogistic proof in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Check the minor premise for affirmation
The minor premise (the one containing the subject of the conclusion) must be affirmative. Look at the quantity: it can be universal or particular, but its quality must be positive (affirmative).
2
Check the major premise for universality
The major premise (the one containing the predicate of the conclusion) must be universal. It can be affirmative or negative, but its quantity must cover all individuals of the middle term.
3
Determine the mood from the premises
If both conditions are met, identify the mood by the quality and quantity of the premises: the minor and major each can be universal (U) or particular (P), affirmative (A) or negative (N). Only four moods are valid: UA+UA, PA+UA, UA+UN, PA+UN.
These correspond to Barbara, Darii, Celarent, Ferio in traditional nomenclature.
4
Write the conclusion according to the mood
For UA+UA: universal affirmative; for PA+UA: particular affirmative; for UA+UN: universal negative; for PA+UN: particular negative. The conclusion's subject is the minor term, predicate the major term.

Conditions of the first figure: that the two affirmatives produce with the universal affirmative the judgment on the minor as seen in your saying: 'Every human is an animal, and some animal is a horse.' His saying: 'that the two affirmatives produce' means the universal and particular, and the lam is for purpose, i.e., the effect of these conditions is that the affirmative universal minor and affirmative particular minor with the affirmative universal major produce the two affirmatives. In the first, the conclusion is universal affirmative; in the second, particular affirmative. And that the two affirmative minors with the universal negative major produce the universal negative and particular negative according to the detail mentioned earlier. The examples of all are clear. 'As seen' etc.: for some animal which is judged by the major is not the same as some animal by which the minor is judged; so the judgment of horseness on some animal does not extend to the minor because it is not subsumed under this 'some'; it is another 'some'. So in the first: such as 'Every human is an animal', 'Every animal is a body', so 'Every human is a body'; and 'Some animal is human', 'Every human is laughing', so 'Some animal is laughing'. And know that the conclusion follows the inferior of the two premises, and inferiority is particularity and negation. If both occur together in the figure, the conclusion is particular negative; if the first occurs without the second, the conclusion is particular affirmative; if the second occurs without the first, the conclusion is universal negative. So understand and memorize, for it will benefit you in all conclusions. It applies to all except in the conclusions of all moods of the third figure and most moods of the fourth figure, because its eight moods produce only particulars except the third mood of them as will come. 'And examples of all' etc.: its summary is that the possible combinations were sixteen resulting from multiplying the four minors by the four majors likewise. Dropped due to condition of affirmative minor: eight—the two negative minors with the four majors. Dropped due to universal major: four—the two particular majors with the two affirmative minors. The affirmative universal: (dropped), (dropped); the affirmative particular: (produced?), (dropped)?... (table omitted) This figure is composed of the two affirmatives and the two negatives, universal and particular, such as: 'Every human is an animal', 'Nothing of the animal is a stone', so 'Nothing of the human is a stone'; and 'Some animal is human' and 'Nothing of the human is neighing', so 'Some animal is not neighing'. It is self-evident, not needing reflection: meaning its production of its conclusions is self-evident, but the conclusion itself is theoretical by necessity because it is obtained by reflection. For if it is said: the conclusion depends on the universality of the major and vice versa because the minor is among the middle, so circularity occurs, so the first figure is not productive, let alone its production being evidently necessary. We reply: the major only needs knowledge of the particulars in summary; otherwise, the judgment of its universality would not be sound due to the infinity of individuals, and the author needs it in his detailed knowledge. Also, the theoretical and the self-evident differ by description; sometimes a thing, when described by one description, is judged theoretically, and when described by another, is self-evident. For what is other than God, when described as 'world', is judged to be temporal, theoretically, and when described as 'changing', is self-evident. And here likewise: because affirming or negating the major of the minor when known, if described by the middle, is self-evident, and if described by the minor, it is not known, like the changing and the temporal.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage explains the conditions for the validity of the first figure of the categorical syllogism and defends its claim to self-evident production. The author begins by stating the conditions: the minor must be affirmative, and the major must be universal. This yields four valid moods: two affirmative (universal and particular) and two negative (universal and particular) depending on the major. The example 'Every human is an animal, some animal is a horse' shows that the conclusion 'some human is a horse' does not follow because the 'some animal' in the major is not the same as the 'some animal' subsumed under 'human'—illustrating why the major must be universal. The commentary then gives standard first figure examples: 'Every human is an animal, every animal is a body, therefore every human is a body' (universal affirmative) and 'Some animal is human, every human is laughing, therefore some animal is laughing' (particular affirmative). It introduces the rule that the conclusion follows the inferior premise: if a premise is particular or negative, the conclusion matches the lower quality. An enumeration of possible premise combinations (16) shows that only four remain after applying the conditions: the two negative minors and the two particular majors are eliminated. A table (partially garbled in OCR) is then referenced with symbols: ba for remaining, sin for dropped, and mnemonics for conclusion types. The passage then asserts that the first figure's production is self-evident (badihi), but the conclusion itself is theoretical (nazari) because it requires reflection. An objection is raised: if the conclusion depends on the major's universality and vice versa, circularity results. The reply distinguishes between summary knowledge (ijmali) of particulars—the major only needs that—and detailed knowledge; the theoretical vs. self-evident distinction is a difference in description, not in truth. For example, the same thing (the world) is judged temporal under the description 'world' (theoretical) but self-evidently changing under 'changing'. Similarly, the middle term makes the syllogism self-evident, while the minor term does not.

The Syllogism

The Rule of the Inferior Premise and the Enumeration of Moods
Author explains the 'inferior premise' rule and enumerates the 16 premise combinations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that the conclusion follows the inferior premise (in quality and quantity) and reconstruct the 16 possible premise combinations to identify the 4 valid first-figure moods.

Core Idea
The conclusion always matches the lower premise; particularity and negation outweigh universality and affirmation.

After establishing the conditions, the author gives a powerful rule: the conclusion follows the 'inferior' of the two premises—meaning it takes the lower quality (negation over affirmation) and the lower quantity (particular over universal). He also enumerates all sixteen possible combinations of premises and shows why only four survive after applying the conditions. This systematic approach helps you quickly determine the conclusion of any valid first-figure syllogism.

Guided Path
1
Understand the 'inferior' premise concept
Inferiority (akhassiyya) means the premise that is 'lower' in a hierarchy: particularity is inferior to universality, and negation is inferior to affirmation. The conclusion will match the inferior premise in both quality and quantity.
2
Apply the inferiority rule to the four valid moods
For the moods: if both premises are affirmative and universal, the conclusion is universal affirmative (no inferiority). If minor is particular and major universal (both affirmative), the conclusion is particular affirmative (inferior in quantity). If major is negative universal and minor affirmative universal, the conclusion is universal negative (inferior in quality). If minor is particular and major negative universal, the conclusion is particular negative (inferior in both).
This matches the examples in the text: UA+UA → UA; PA+UA → PA; UA+UN → UN; PA+UN → PN.
3
Enumeration of the sixteen combinations
There are four types of minors (UA, PA, UN, PN) and four types of majors (UA, PA, UN, PN), giving 16 pairs. The condition 'minor must be affirmative' eliminates all pairs with negative minors (8 pairs). The condition 'major must be universal' eliminates pairs with particular majors (4 pairs from the remaining 8). This leaves exactly 4 valid pairs.
The text uses a table with symbols: ba (ب) for remaining, sin (س) for dropped, and mnemonic letters for conclusions.
4
Practice with the table's symbols
The author assigns letters: under 'ba' for remaining, 'nun mim' (نم) for universal affirmative, 'nun sin' (نس) for universal negative, 'nun waw' (نو) for particular affirmative, 'nun lam' (نل) for particular negative. These help memorize the conclusion type directly from the mood.

أن ينتج الصغرى الموجبة الكلية والموجبة الجزئية مع الكبرى الموجبة الكلية الموجبتين، ففي الأول تكون النتيجة موجبة كلية، وفي الثاني موجبة جزئية، وأن ينتج الصغريان الموجبتان مع السالبة الكلية الكبرى: السالبتين الكلية والجزئية على ما سبق تفصيله، وأمثلة الكل واضحة. كما يشاهد إلخ فإن بعض الحيوان الذي هو محكوم عليه بالأكبر غير بعض الحيوان الذي حكم به على الأصغر، فالحكم على بعض الحيوان بالفرسية لا يتعدى إلى الأصغر؛ لعدم كونه مندرجا تحت هذا البعض إنما هو بعض آخر. ففي الأول: مثل "كل إنسان حيوان"، "وكل حيوان جسم"، "فكل إنسان جسم"، "وبعض الحيوان إنسان"، "وكل إنسان ضاحك"، فبعض الحيوان ضاحك. واعلم أن النتيجة تكون تابعة لأخس المقدمتين، والأخسية إنما هي الجزئية والسلب فإن وجدتا في الشكل معا يكون النتيجة سالبة جزئية، وإن وجد الأول دون الثاني كانت النتيجة موجبة جزئية، وإن وجد الثاني دون الأول كانت النتيجة سالبة كلية فافهم واحفظ؛ فإنه ينفعك في جميع النتائج، وجاء في كلها إلا في نتائج جميع ضروب الشكل الثالث وأكثر ضروب الشكل الرابع؛ لأن ضروبه ثمانية لا ينتج إلا جزئية إلا الضرب الثالث منهما كما سيحيء. وأمثلة الكل إلخ حاصله أن الاحتمالات العقلية كانت ستة عشر حاصلة من ضرب الصغريات الأربع في الكبريات كذلك، وسقط من شرط إيجاب الصغرى ثمانية الصغريان السالبتان مع الكبريات الأربع ومن كلية الكبرى أربعة الكبريان الجزئيتان مع الصغريين الموجبتين، بقي أربعة فأمثلة الكل باقية كانت أو ساقطة، ومراتب الضروب الباقية تعدادها بالترتيب الموضوع لها ونتائجها واضحة من هذا الجدول، فعليك بالباء رمزا من الباقي والسين من الساقط ونم تحت الباء من النتيجة الموجبة الكلية ونس من النتيجة السالبة الكلية ونو من النتيجة الموجبة الجزئية ونل من النتيجة السالبة الجزئية، والرقم الفوقاني على الباءات من التعداد. الكبريات الموجبة الموجبة السالبة السالبة الصغريات الكلية الجزئية الكلية الجزئية الموجبة الكلية س س الموجبة الجزئية بنه س نل ب ٤ س السالبة الكلية س س س س السالبة الجزئية س س س س تألف هذا الشكل من الموجبتين والسالبتين الكلية والجزئية مثل: كل إنسان حيوان، ولا شيء من الحيوان بحجر، فلا شيء من الإنسان بحجر، وبعض الحيوان إنسان ولا شيء من الإنسان بصاهل، فبعض الحيوان ليس بصاهل. بديهي غير محتاج إلى النظر يعني أن إنتاجه لنتائجه بديهي، وأما نفس النتيجة فنظرية بالضرورة؛ لحصولها بالنظر. فإن قيل: إن النتيجة موقوفة على كلية الكبرى وبالعكس؛ لأن الأصغر من جملة الأوسط فيلزم الدور، فلا يكون الشكل الأول منتجا فضلا عن أن يكون إنتاجه بينا ضروريا. قلنا: إن الكبرى إنما تحتاج إلى علم الجزئيات إجمالا وإلا لما صح الحكم بصدق كليته؛ لعدم تناهي الأفراد، والمصنف إنما يحتاج في علمه التفصيلي إليها، وأيضا النظرية والضرورية مختلفتان بالعنوان فرب شيء إذا عبر بعنوان يكون الحكم عليه بأمر نظريا، وإذا عبر بعنوان آخر يكون بديهيا؛ فإن ما سوى الله تعالى إذا عبر بعنوان العالم يكون الحكم عليه بالحادث نظريا وإذا عبر بعنوان المتغير يكون بديهيا وههنا كذلك؛ لأن إيجاب الأكبر للأصغر أو سلبه عند معلوم إذا عبر عنه بعنوان الأوسط، وغير معلوم إذا عبر بعنوان الأصغر كالمتغير والحادث.
Conditions of the first figure: that the two affirmatives produce with the universal affirmative the judgment on the minor as seen in your saying: 'Every human is an animal, and some animal is a horse.' His saying: 'that the two affirmatives produce' means the universal and particular, and the lam is for purpose, i.e., the effect of these conditions is that the affirmative universal minor and affirmative particular minor with the affirmative universal major produce the two affirmatives. In the first, the conclusion is universal affirmative; in the second, particular affirmative. And that the two affirmative minors with the universal negative major produce the universal negative and particular negative according to the detail mentioned earlier. The examples of all are clear. 'As seen' etc.: for some animal which is judged by the major is not the same as some animal by which the minor is judged; so the judgment of horseness on some animal does not extend to the minor because it is not subsumed under this 'some'; it is another 'some'. So in the first: such as 'Every human is an animal', 'Every animal is a body', so 'Every human is a body'; and 'Some animal is human', 'Every human is laughing', so 'Some animal is laughing'. And know that the conclusion follows the inferior of the two premises, and inferiority is particularity and negation. If both occur together in the figure, the conclusion is particular negative; if the first occurs without the second, the conclusion is particular affirmative; if the second occurs without the first, the conclusion is universal negative. So understand and memorize, for it will benefit you in all conclusions. It applies to all except in the conclusions of all moods of the third figure and most moods of the fourth figure, because its eight moods produce only particulars except the third mood of them as will come. 'And examples of all' etc.: its summary is that the possible combinations were sixteen resulting from multiplying the four minors by the four majors likewise. Dropped due to condition of affirmative minor: eight—the two negative minors with the four majors. Dropped due to universal major: four—the two particular majors with the two affirmative minors. Remaining are four. So the examples of all, whether remaining or dropped, and the ranks of the remaining moods, their enumeration in the order assigned to them, and their conclusions are clear from this table. So upon you use 'ba' as symbol for remaining, 'sin' for dropped, 'nun mim' under 'ba' for universal affirmative conclusion, 'nun sin' for universal negative, 'nun waw' for particular affirmative, 'nun lam' for particular negative, and the superscript number on the 'ba's from the enumeration. The majors: affirmative, affirmative, negative, negative; the minors: universal, particular, universal, particular. The affirmative universal: (dropped), (dropped); the affirmative particular: (produced?), (dropped)?... (table omitted) This figure is composed of the two affirmatives and the two negatives, universal and particular, such as: 'Every human is an animal', 'Nothing of the animal is a stone', so 'Nothing of the human is a stone'; and 'Some animal is human' and 'Nothing of the human is neighing', so 'Some animal is not neighing'. It is self-evident, not needing reflection: meaning its production of its conclusions is self-evident, but the conclusion itself is theoretical by necessity because it is obtained by reflection. For if it is said: the conclusion depends on the universality of the major and vice versa because the minor is among the middle, so circularity occurs, so the first figure is not productive, let alone its production being evidently necessary. We reply: the major only needs knowledge of the particulars in summary; otherwise, the judgment of its universality would not be sound due to the infinity of individuals, and the author needs it in his detailed knowledge. Also, the theoretical and the self-evident differ by description; sometimes a thing, when described by one description, is judged theoretically, and when described by another, is self-evident. For what is other than God, when described as 'world', is judged to be temporal, theoretically, and when described as 'changing', is self-evident. And here likewise: because affirming or negating the major of the minor when known, if described by the middle, is self-evident, and if described by the minor, it is not known, like the changing and the temporal.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage explains the conditions for the validity of the first figure of the categorical syllogism and defends its claim to self-evident production. The author begins by stating the conditions: the minor must be affirmative, and the major must be universal. This yields four valid moods: two affirmative (universal and particular) and two negative (universal and particular) depending on the major. The example 'Every human is an animal, some animal is a horse' shows that the conclusion 'some human is a horse' does not follow because the 'some animal' in the major is not the same as the 'some animal' subsumed under 'human'—illustrating why the major must be universal. The commentary then gives standard first figure examples: 'Every human is an animal, every animal is a body, therefore every human is a body' (universal affirmative) and 'Some animal is human, every human is laughing, therefore some animal is laughing' (particular affirmative). It introduces the rule that the conclusion follows the inferior premise: if a premise is particular or negative, the conclusion matches the lower quality. An enumeration of possible premise combinations (16) shows that only four remain after applying the conditions: the two negative minors and the two particular majors are eliminated. A table (partially garbled in OCR) is then referenced with symbols: ba for remaining, sin for dropped, and mnemonics for conclusion types. The passage then asserts that the first figure's production is self-evident (badihi), but the conclusion itself is theoretical (nazari) because it requires reflection. An objection is raised: if the conclusion depends on the major's universality and vice versa, circularity results. The reply distinguishes between summary knowledge (ijmali) of particulars—the major only needs that—and detailed knowledge; the theoretical vs. self-evident distinction is a difference in description, not in truth. For example, the same thing (the world) is judged temporal under the description 'world' (theoretical) but self-evidently changing under 'changing'. Similarly, the middle term makes the syllogism self-evident, while the minor term does not.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Self-Evident Production and the Objection of Circularity
Author defends claim that first figure production is self-evident and answers circularity objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the first figure's production is self-evident while its conclusions are theoretical, and analyze the response to the circularity objection using the distinction between summary and detailed knowledge and the role of description.

Core Idea
The first figure's process is self-evident, but the conclusion itself requires reflection — and the circularity objection fails due to the distinction between summary and detailed knowledge.

The author boldly claims that the first figure's production of conclusions is self-evident (badihi), yet the conclusions themselves are theoretical (nazari). This may seem contradictory: how can a self-evident process yield a non-self-evident result? An objector raises a deeper issue: if the conclusion depends on the major's universality and the major depends on knowing the conclusion's individuals, there is circularity. In this lesson, you will see how the author resolves these puzzles using key epistemological distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish 'production' from 'conclusion'
The author says: إنتاجه لنتائجه بديهي (its production of its conclusions is self-evident), but نفس النتيجة نظرية (the conclusion itself is theoretical). The act of syllogizing in the first figure is immediate once premises are known, but the conclusion as a piece of knowledge is not known without that act — it requires reflection.
This is similar to: the rule of inference is obvious, but the derived proposition is not obvious without the inference.
2
Understand the circularity objection
The objector argues: the conclusion (e.g., 'every human is a body') depends on the major premise being universal ('every animal is a body'). But the major's universality depends on knowing that all animals, including humans, are bodies — which is the conclusion itself. So there is a circle (دور), making the first figure unproductive.
3
Analyze the author's first reply: summary vs. detailed knowledge
The author replies that the major premise only requires summary (ijmali) knowledge of the particulars, not detailed (tafsili) knowledge. To know that 'every animal is a body', one does not need to have explicitly verified each individual animal; a general insight suffices. The detailed knowledge of each case (including humans) is what the syllogism provides.
4
Analyze the author's second reply: description changes epistemic status
The author gives an example: the same reality — 'what is other than God' — when described as 'world' (العالم) is judged to be temporal (حادث) theoretically, but when described as 'changing' (المتغير) is judged to be temporally originated (or changeable) self-evidently. Similarly, the connection between the major and minor terms is self-evident when viewed through the middle term (كالأوسط), but not when viewed through the minor term alone. So the first figure's validity is self-evident because the middle term makes the relation obvious.

According to the author, why is the first figure's production self-evident while the conclusion is theoretical?
A) The premises are always true, so the conclusion is obvious.
B) The middle term makes the connection obvious, but the conclusion is obtained through reflection.

Key Takeaway
The first figure's logical process is self-evident because of the middle term's role, but the conclusions it yields are theoretical and require reflection to derive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the syllogism: 'Every human is an animal, every animal is mortal, therefore every human is mortal.' The objector says the major 'every animal is mortal' depends on knowledge of all animals including humans, which is the conclusion. Using the author's first reply, explain why this is not circular.

The Key Condition of the Second Figure
Author states condition of quality difference in premises for second figure validity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the essential condition for the second figure of the categorical syllogism and explain why two affirmative premises yield no valid conclusion in this figure.

Core Idea
In the second figure, validity demands difference in quality between the two premises.

A categorical syllogism's figure is determined by the position of the middle term. In the second figure, the middle term is the predicate of both premises. For this figure to produce a valid conclusion, the premises must differ in quality—one affirmative and one negative. This lesson examines why this condition is necessary and how it operates, based on the classical logical tradition as presented in the commentary on the conditions of the second figure.

Guided Path
1
Recall the structure of the second figure
In the second figure, the middle term (M) is the predicate of both premises: the major premise is P-M, the minor premise is S-M. The conclusion is S-P. Write down this form to visualize.
The major term is P, the minor is S.
2
Identify the quality condition
The passage states that in the second figure, the premises must differ in quality. This means one must be affirmative and the other negative. Check if the commentary confirms this as a necessary condition for validity.
3
Test with an invalid example
Consider a second figure with both premises affirmative: 'Every human is an animal; every horse is an animal.' No valid conclusion about humans and horses follows. Why? Because the middle term 'animal' is undistributed in both premises (predicate of affirmative propositions), so no connection is established.
This illustrates the logical failure without quality difference.
4
Test with a valid example
Now use one affirmative and one negative: 'Every human is an animal; no stone is an animal.' This yields 'No stone is human' (valid Camestres). The negative premise distributes the middle term, enabling a valid inference.
The mood is AEE (or AEO) in the second figure.
5
Understand the self-evidence claim
The commentary notes that the production of valid conclusions in the second figure from the four categorical proposition types (A, E, I, O) is self-evident once the quality condition is met. Compare this to other figures where conditions are less intuitive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شرائط الشكل الثاني الموجبتين ومع السالبة السالبتين بالضرورة، وفي الثاني اختلافهما في الكيف قوله: الموجبتين: أي ينتج الكلية والجزئية. قوله :والسالبتين أي ينتج الكلية والجزئية. قوله: بالضرورة: متعلق بقوله: ينتج. والمقصود الإشارة إلى أن إنتاج هذا الشكل للمحصورات الأربع بديهي بخلاف إنتاج سائر الأشكال لنتائجها كما سيجيء تفصيلها قوله: وفي الثاني اختلافهما: أي يشترط في هذا الشكل بحسب الكيفية اختلاف المقدمتين في السلب والإيجاب؛ وذلك لأنه لو تألف هذا الشكل من الموجبتين.

His saying: 'and in the second their difference' means that in this figure, according to quality, the difference of the two premises in negation and affirmation is required; because if this figure were composed of the two affirmatives...

Commentary
This passage explains the conditions for the second figure of the categorical syllogism. The author states that the second figure produces universal and particular conclusions from two affirmative premises as well as from two negative premises with necessity, but the key condition is that the premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative). The commentary clarifies that 'the two affirmatives' refers to producing universal affirmative and particular affirmative conclusions, and similarly for the two negatives. The phrase 'necessarily' indicates that the production of the four categoricals in this figure is self-evident, unlike other figures. The condition of 'difference in quality' is explained: if both premises were affirmative, no conclusion would follow. The text cuts off, but the intended example would show that in the second figure, if both premises are affirmative (e.g., 'Every human is an animal, every stone is an animal'), no valid conclusion about the relationship between human and stone can be drawn because the middle term (animal) is not distributed properly. Thus, the second figure requires one premise negative to produce a valid conclusion (typically negative).

The Circularity Objection and the Summary Knowledge Response
Commentary responds to an objection that the first figure's production is not evident due to circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the objection of circularity against the first figure and explain how the requirement of summary knowledge resolves it.

Core Idea
The major premise needs only summary knowledge of its particulars, not detailed knowledge.

The first figure is considered evidently productive, but an objection claims that the major premise's universality requires knowledge of all its individuals, leading to circularity. The commentator responds by distinguishing between summary and detailed knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Locate the claim that the first figure's production is not evident because the conclusion depends on the major's universality and vice versa, causing circularity.
2
Understand the circularity
The conclusion gives detailed knowledge of the minor term, which is an individual of the middle term; therefore the major premise seems to depend on the conclusion.
This is a classic logical puzzle.
3
Examine the response
The commentator says the major only needs summary (ijmali) knowledge of its particulars, not detailed (tafsili) knowledge.
Otherwise, no universal judgment would be possible.
4
Assess why this avoids circularity
Summary knowledge does not require knowing each individual; the conclusion provides detailed knowledge, but that is an additional benefit, not a prerequisite.
Thus, the first figure remains evidently productive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بديهي غير محتاج إلى النظر يعني أن إنتاجه لنتائجه بديهي، وأما نفس النتيجة فنظرية بالضرورة؛ لحصولها بالنظر. فإن قيل: إن النتيجة موقوفة على كلية الكبرى وبالعكس؛ لأن الأصغر من جملة الأوسط فيلزم الدور، فلا يكون الشكل الأول منتجا فضلاً عن أن يكون إنتاجه بينا ضروريا. قلنا: إن الكبرى إنما تحتاج إلى علم الجزئيات إجمالا وإلا لما صح الحكم بصدق كليته؛ لعدم تناهي الأفراد، والمصنف إنما يحتاج في علمه التفصيلي إليها، وأيضاً النظرية والضرورية مختلفتان بالعنوان فرب شيء إذا عبر بعنوان يكون الحكم عليه بأمر نظريا، وإذا عبر بعنوان آخر يكون بديهيا؛ فإن ما سوى الله تعالى إذا عبر بعنوان العالم يكون الحكم عليه بالحادث نظريا وإذا عبر بعنوان المتغير يكون بديهيا وههنا كذلك؛ لأن إيجاب الأكبر للأصغر أو سلبه عند معلوم إذا عبر عنه بعنوان الأوسط، وغير معلوم إذا عبر بعنوان الأصغر كالمتغير والحادث.
Evident, not requiring consideration, meaning that its production of its conclusions is evident, but the conclusion itself is theoretical necessarily because it is obtained through consideration. If it is said: The conclusion depends on the universality of the major and vice versa, because the minor is among the individuals of the middle, so circularity follows, so the first figure would not be productive, let alone its production be evidently necessary. We say: The major only needs knowledge of the particulars in summary, otherwise the judgment of the truth of its universality would not be valid due to the infinity of individuals, and the author only needs it in his detailed knowledge. Also, the theoretical and the necessary differ in aspect; for sometimes a thing, if expressed under one aspect, the judgment about it is theoretical, and if expressed under another aspect, it is evident. For example, other than God—if expressed under the aspect of 'the world', the judgment of its temporality is theoretical, and if expressed under the aspect of 'the changing', it is evident. Similarly here, because the affirmation or negation of the major for the minor, when known, if expressed under the aspect of the middle, it is unknown, and if expressed under the aspect of the minor, it is like 'the changing' and 'the temporal'.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection that the first figure's conclusion cannot be evident because the major premise's universality itself depends on knowledge of its particulars, leading to circularity. The commentator responds first by distinguishing between summary knowledge (necessary for the major) and detailed knowledge (which requires the conclusion). Then he explains that the distinction between necessary (badihi) and theoretical (nazari) knowledge is relative to aspect: the same fact can be known necessarily under one description and theoretically under another. He gives the example of the temporality of the world: under the description 'world' it is theoretical, but under 'changing' it is evident. Similarly, the conclusion's truth is evident when expressed with the middle term, but theoretical when expressed with the minor term alone.

D) He says the objection is invalid because the minor term is not among the individuals of the middle.

Key Takeaway
The first figure's evident production is defended by distinguishing summary from detailed knowledge, avoiding circularity.

The Aspect Distinction: One Truth, Two Modes of Knowledge
Commentary responds to an objection that the first figure's production is not evident due to circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the same truth can be both evident and theoretical under different aspects, and apply this to the first figure's conclusion.

Core Idea
A thing's knowability changes with its aspect: 'the world' is theoretical, 'the changing' is evident.

The commentator offers a second response to the circularity objection: the distinction between necessary and theoretical knowledge is relative to the aspect (i'tibar) under which something is considered. He illustrates with the example of the world's temporality.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the principle
The same proposition can be known necessarily under one description and theoretically under another.
2
Study the example
The world's temporality is theoretical when considered as 'world' (requires proof), but evident when considered as 'changing' (change is observed).
This is because 'changing' directly implies temporality.
3
Apply to the syllogism
The conclusion 'P is A' is evident when expressed with the middle term (e.g., because P is M), but theoretical when expressed with the minor term alone.
Thus, the first figure's production is evident in one respect.
4
Recognize the significance
This shows that evidentness is not absolute but relative to the aspect; the objector's confusion arises from disregarding this relativity.

Two Conditions of the Second Figure: Quality Difference and Universal Major
Commentary explains the two necessary conditions of the second figure syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and verify the two necessary conditions of a second-figure categorical syllogism: (1) the premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative), and (2) the major premise must be universal.

Core Idea
Second figure needs quality difference and universal major to produce a certain conclusion.

The second figure of categorical syllogism has specific conditions for validity. The commentator explains two key conditions: the premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative), and the major premise must be universal. Violating either condition leads to conclusions that vary in truth value depending on the subject matter, showing that no necessary conclusion follows. Understanding these conditions helps you evaluate the validity of arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure
Confirm the syllogism is in the second figure by checking that the middle term is the predicate in both premises. This is the defining structure of shakl al-thani.
The middle term links the two premises but is not present in the conclusion.
2
Check quality difference
Examine the quality of each premise. One must be affirmative (ijabi) and the other negative (salbi). If both are affirmative or both negative, the condition is violated.
The commentator shows that with two affirmatives, the conclusion can be true or false depending on the subject.
3
Check universality of major
Examine the quantity of the major premise. It must be universal (kulli), either affirmative or negative. If it is particular (juz'i), the condition is violated.
The commentator demonstrates that a particular major leads to varied truth values when the subject changes.
4
Understand the rationale
The commentator's examples show that when either condition is missing, the conclusion is not uniquely determined. This indicates that the syllogism is unproductive (ghayr muntij) and does not yield a necessary conclusion.
For instance, changing 'horse' to 'speaker' in the example flips the truth of the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: وفي الثاني اختلافهما: أي يشترط في هذا الشكل بحسب الكيفية اختلاف المقدمتين في السلب والإيجاب؛ وذلك لأنه لو تألف هذا الشكل من الموجبتين يحصل الاختلاف، وهو أن يكون الصادق في نتيجة القياس الإيجاب تارة والسلب أخرى؛ فإنه لو قلنا: كل إنسان حيوان، وكل ناطق حيوان كان الحق الإيجاب، ولو بدلنا الكبرى بقولنا: كل فرس حيوان، كان الحق السلب، وكذا الحال لو تألف من السالبتين كقولنا: لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر، ولا شيء من الناطق بحجر كان الحق الإيجاب، ولو بدلنا الكبرى بقولنا: لا شيء من الفرس بحجر كان الحق السلب، والاختلاف دليل عدم الإنتاج؛ فإن النتيجة هي القول الآخر الذي يلزم من المقدمتين، فلو كان اللازم من المقدمتين الموجبة لما كان الحق في بعض المواد هو السالبة، ولو كان اللازم منهما السالبة لما صدق في بعض المواد الموجبة. قوله وكلية الكبرى: أي ويشترط في الشكل الثاني بحسب الكم كلية الكبرى؛ إذ عند جزئيتها يحصل الاختلاف كقولنا: إنسان ناطق، وبعض الحيوان ليس بناطق كان الحق الإيجاب، ولو قلنا بعض الصاهل ليس بناطق، كان الحق السلب يحصل الاختلاف والسر فيه أنه يجوز أن يكون الأشياء المتخالفة مشتركة في ثبوت شيء واحد لها، فالحق حينئذ السلب، وكذا يجوز أن يكون الأشياء المتوافقة أيضاً مشتركة في ثبوت أمر، فالحق حينئذ الإيجاب.
His statement: 'And in the second [figure] their difference': meaning it is required in this figure according to quality that the two premises differ in negation and affirmation; because if this figure were composed of two affirmative [premises] difference would occur, namely that the truth in the conclusion of the syllogism is sometimes affirmation and sometimes negation. For if we say: 'Every human is an animal, and every speaker is an animal', the truth is affirmation, and if we change the major by saying 'Every horse is an animal', the truth is negation. Similarly if it were composed of two negatives like our saying: 'Nothing of human is a stone, and nothing of speaker is a stone', the truth is affirmation, and if we change the major by saying 'Nothing of horse is a stone', the truth is negation. Difference is the evidence of non-production; for the conclusion is the other statement that follows from the two premises. So if the necessary from the two premises were affirmative, the truth would not be negation in some matters, and if the necessary from them were negative, the affirmative would not be true in some matters. His statement: 'And the universality of the major': meaning it is required in the second figure according to quantity that the major be universal; because when it is particular, difference occurs, as in our saying: 'A human is a speaker, and some animal is not a speaker', the truth is affirmation, and if we say 'Some neighing [thing] is not a speaker', the truth is negation. Difference occurs, and the secret is that it is possible for diverse things to share in the affirmation of one thing for them, so the truth then is negation, and similarly it is possible for agreeing things to also share in the affirmation of a matter, so the truth then is affirmation.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the conditions of the second figure of the categorical syllogism. The commentator explains two necessary conditions: first, the two premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative); second, the major premise must be universal. The commentary uses examples from common discourse (like 'every human is an animal') to illustrate why these conditions are necessary. If both premises were affirmative, the conclusion could be either affirmative or negative depending on the subject matter, which shows that the syllogism would not produce a necessary conclusion. Similarly, if the major premise is particular, the conclusion varies. The commentator also explains that the reason for requiring difference in quality is that if the same quality were in both premises, the conclusion would not be uniquely determined; the variation in truth value across different material (e.g., changing the middle term) demonstrates that no single conclusion follows necessarily.

Key Terms
الشكل الثاني
اختلاف المقدمتين
كلية الكبرى

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The two premises must differ in quality.
D) The minor premise must be universal.

Key Takeaway
A valid second-figure syllogism requires that the premises differ in quality and that the major premise is universal; otherwise, the conclusion is not necessary.

Why the Major Must Be Universal in the Second Figure
Commentator explains why the major premise must be universal in the second figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why the major premise must be universal in the second figure and test whether a given second-figure syllogism is productive.

Core Idea
Without a universal major, the second figure produces conflicting conclusions—a sign of non-production.

In the second figure, the middle term is the predicate in both premises. The commentator shows that if the major premise is not universal (i.e., particular), the same syllogistic form can yield both a true affirmative and a true negative conclusion depending on the subject matter. This 'ikhtilaf' proves the syllogism is non-productive. Understanding this condition is crucial for constructing valid categorical syllogisms in Islamic logic, especially in Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure and the condition
Recall that in the second figure, the middle term is the predicate in both premises. The condition discussed is that the major premise must be universal (either universal affirmative or universal negative).
2
Understand the problem of ikhtilaf
Ikhtilaf means that from the same syllogistic form, the conclusion can be true in one subject matter and false in another, or even opposite truth values. The commentator gives examples: with a particular major, changing the minor subject yields opposite truth values in the conclusion.
3
Apply the test
Given a second-figure syllogism with a particular major, construct two instances: one where the conclusion is true and another where it is false. For example, minor: 'Some human is rational', major: 'Some animal is not rational' (particular negative). With minor subject 'human', the conclusion 'Some human is not rational' is false? Actually check the text: They used human and then horse. We'll use the passage examples.
The test shows that the syllogism does not produce a necessary conclusion.
4
Recognize the logical reason
The underlying reason is that a particular major allows both agreeing and differing things to share or not share the predicate, so the conclusion varies. A universal major eliminates this ambiguity.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكلية الكبرى. يحصل الاختلاف، وهو أن يكون الصادق في نتيجة القياس الإيجاب تارة والسلب أخرى؛ فإنه لو قلنا: كل إنسان حيوان، وكل ناطق حيوان كان الحق الإيجاب، ولو بدلنا الكبرى بقولنا: كل فرس حيوان، كان الحق السلب، وكذا الحال لو تألف من السالبتين كقولنا: لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر، ولا شيء من الناطق بحجر كان الحق الإيجاب، ولو بدلنا الكبرى بقولنا: لا شيء من الفرس بحجر كان الحق السلب، والاختلاف دليل عدم الإنتاج؛ فإن النتيجة هي القول الآخر الذي يلزم من المقدمتين، فلو كان اللازم من المقدمتين الموجبة لما كان الحق في بعض المواد هو السالبة، ولو كان اللازم منهما السالبة لما صدق في بعض المواد الموجبة. قوله وكلية الكبرى: أي ويشترط في الشكل الثاني بحسب الكم كلية الكبرى؛ إذ عند جزئيتها يحصل الاختلاف كقولنا: إنسان ناطق، وبعض الحيوان ليس بناطق كان الحق الإيجاب، ولو قلنا بعض الصاهل ليس بناطق، كان الحق السلب. يحصل الاختلاف والسر فيه أنه يجوز أن يكون الأشياء المتخالفة مشتركة في ثبوت شيء واحد لها، فالحق حينئذ السلب، وكذا يجوز أن يكون الأشياء المتوافقة أيضاً مشتركة في ثبوت أمر، فالحق حينئذ الإيجاب، وهذا ظاهر من كلام الشارح. إسماعيل) كذا الحال لو تألف من سالبتين: فإنه كما يجوز اشتراك أمور متخالفة ومتوافقة في الإيجاب كذلك يجوز اشتراك الأشياء المتخالفة والمتوافقة في السلب أيضاً، فيكون الحق على الأول السلب وعلى الثاني الإيجاب. إسماعيل) القول الآخر: ومعنى آخريته أن لا يكون إحدى مقدمتي القياس الاقتراني من الصغرى والكبرى والاستثنائي من الشرطية الواضعة أو الرافعة، وأما أن لا يكون جزء من إحدى مقدمتيه فغير ملتزم. يك روزي) لما كان الحق إلخ هذا ظاهر في غاية الظهور؛ فإن الأمرين المتناقضين يمتنع أن يكونا لازمين لشيء واحد كيف ولو كان ثبوتهما لهذا الشيء دائما بحيث لا ينفك كل منهما من هذا الشيء أصلا فيلزم اجتماع المتنافيين وهو باطل، وإن كان كل منهما في زمان عدم ثبوت الآخر فلا يكون كل منهما لازما لذلك الشيء؛ فإن اللازم لا ينفك عن الملزوم في أي مادة فرضت، وههنا قد انفك كل منهما عنه في زمان ثبوت الآخر. هذا خلف. إسماعيل)
And the universality of the major. The difference occurs, i.e., that the truth in the conclusion of the syllogism is affirmative sometimes and negative other times. For if we say: 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every rational being is an animal', the truth is affirmative; and if we change the major to 'Every horse is an animal', the truth is negative. Similarly if it is composed of two negatives as we say: 'No human is a stone' and 'No rational being is a stone', the truth is affirmative; and if we change the major to 'No horse is a stone', the truth is negative. The difference indicates non-production; for the conclusion is the other proposition that follows from the two premises. So if what follows from the two affirmative premises were always affirmative, the truth would not be negative in some matters; and if what follows from them were always negative, the affirmative would not be true in some matters. He said: 'And universality of the major': i.e., it is required in the second figure with respect to quantity the universality of the major; because when it is particular, the difference occurs as we say: 'Some human is rational' and 'Some animal is not rational', the truth is affirmative; and if we say 'Some neigher is not rational', the truth is negative. The difference occurs and the secret of it is that it is permissible for different things to share in the affirmation of one thing, so the truth then is negative; similarly it is permissible for agreeing things also to share in the affirmation of a matter, so the truth then is affirmative. And this is apparent from the words of the commentator (Isma'il). Similarly if it is composed of two negatives: for just as it is permissible for different things and agreeing things to share in affirmation, likewise it is permissible for different things and agreeing things to share in negation, so the truth in the first case is negative and in the second affirmative. (Isma'il) The other proposition: and the meaning of its 'otherness' is that it is not one of the two premises of the categorical syllogism (the minor and major) nor of the conditional syllogism (the placing or removing conditional), and not being part of one of its premises is not required. (Yak Ruzi) For the truth etc. This is extremely apparent; for two contradictory things cannot be necessary consequences of one thing; how? If they were both constantly attendant on this thing such that each never separates from it at all, then the two contradictories would coexist, which is false; and if each were at a time of absence of the other, then each would not be a necessary consequence of that thing; for the necessary consequence never separates from its ground in any matter assumed, and here each has separated from it in the time of existence of the other. This is a contradiction. (Isma'il)
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory commentary on the condition that the major premise in the second figure must be universal. The commentator begins by stating the problem: if the major is not universal, the same syllogistic form can produce both true affirmative and true negative conclusions depending on the subject matter (material). He gives examples: with 'Every human is an animal' and 'Every rational being is an animal', the conclusion is affirmative (every rational is an animal), but if we replace the major with 'Every horse is an animal', the conclusion becomes negative (no rational is a horse). Similarly for two negative premises. This variation in truth value proves that the syllogism does not produce a necessary conclusion (i.e., it is non-productive). The commentator then explains that the condition is explicitly 'universality of the major' meaning the major must be a universal proposition. When the major is particular (e.g., 'Some animal is not rational'), the same issue arises: with one minor (human) the conclusion is affirmative, with another (horse) it becomes negative. The underlying reason is that different things can coexist under a common predicate, and a particular major cannot rule out both possibilities. The commentary also clarifies that the 'other proposition' refers to the conclusion not being one of the premises, and argues against the impossibility of contradictory consequents. The examples are purely logical, but the reasoning is essential for understanding why the second figure requires a universal major to guarantee a single conclusion.

Key Terms
كلية الكبرى
الاختلاف
النتيجة هي القول الآخر

A) Because the minor is not universal.
B) Because the major is particular negative, leading to different truth values in different subject matters.
C) Because the premises are both affirmative.
D) Because the conclusion would be negative.

Key Takeaway
In the second figure, a particular major premise leads to ikhtilaf, making the syllogism non-productive; thus the major must be universal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Syllogism

Modal Conditions for the Second Figure: Disjunctive Requirements
Commentator enumerates modal conditions for valid syllogisms in the second figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two disjunctive modal conditions required for a valid second-figure syllogism and evaluate whether a given pair of modal premises satisfies them.

Core Idea
The second figure's modal conditions are disjunctions, not exclusive prerequisites.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks the modal conditions for a productive second-figure syllogism. These conditions are disjunctive (muraddad), meaning they offer alternatives. Misreading them—thinking a possible premise is always required—is a common error the commentator warns against.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two requirements
The commentator states that there are two modal conditions. Locate 'الأمران' and then 'الأول' and 'ثانيهما'. These are the two disjunctive conditions.
The first condition is about the minor being perpetual or the major being convertible; the second about possibility and necessity.
2
Analyze the first disjunctive condition
The first condition says: either the minor is perpetual/necessary, OR the major belongs to the six types of propositions whose universal negatives convert simply. Understand why: in the second figure, we often need to convert the major, and that conversion is only guaranteed for those six types.
The six types are well-known in modal logic; they include necessary, perpetual, etc.
3
Analyze the second disjunctive condition
The second condition says: either neither premise is possible (general or special), OR (if one premise is possible) the other must be necessary (absolute or conditional). Note that the possible premise can be either the minor or the major.
The commentator clarifies that this includes two sub-options: minor possible + major necessary, or major possible + minor necessary.
4
Avoid the common mistake
The commentator warns that the author's phrasing 'and the possible with the necessary' might make some think a possible premise is always required. But that is false: the condition is satisfied if both premises are non-possible. Only when a possible premise appears does the other need to be necessary.
The disjunction is in the sense of 'preventing emptiness' (man' al-khuluw), meaning at least one must hold; both can hold together.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله مع دوام الصغرى: أي يشترط في هذا الشكل بحسب الجهة أمران: الأول: أحد الأمرين هو إما أن يصدق الدوام على الصغرى بأن تكون دائمة أو ضرورية، وإما أن تكون الكبرى من القضايا الست التي تنعكس سوالبها لا من السبع التي لا تنعكس سوالبها. أي يشترط إلخ: أي يشترط في إنتاج الشكل الثاني بحسب الجهة أمران: أحدهما: مفهوم مردد بين كون الصغرى إحدى الدائمتين أو كون الكبرى من القضايا الست التي تنعكس سوالبها الكلية بالعكس المستوي، وثانيهما: مفهوم مردد بين أن لا يكون شيء من المقدمتين ممكنة ولا خاصة وأن يكون الصغرى إحدى الممكنتين والكبرى ضرورية مطلقة أو مشروطة عامة أو خاصة، وأن يكون الكبرى إحدى الممكنتين والصغرى ضرورية مطلقة. (أبو الفتح) الأول أحد الأمرين تحقيق المقام وتنقيح المرام أن إنتاج الشكل الثاني مشروط بحسب الجهة بأمرين، كل واحد منهما مفهوم مردد، أما الأمر الأول: فمفهوم مردد بين كون الصغرى إحدى الدائمتين وكون الكبرى من القضايا التي تنعكس سوالبها الكلية بالعكس المستوي. وأما الأمر الثاني فمفهوم مردد بين أن لا يكون شيء من المقدمتين ممكنة لا عامة ولا خاصة وأن يكون وهذا أي كون شيء من المقدمتين إحدى الممكنتين أيضاً مفهوم مردد بين أن يكون الصغرى إحدى الممكنتين والكبرى ضرورية أو مشروطة عامة أو خاصة وأن يكون الكبرى إحدى الممكنتين والصغرى ضرورية، وإلى هذا المفهوم المردد الأخير أشار المصنف بقوله: "وكون الممكنة مع الضرورية أو الكبرى المشروطة" فقد علمت من هذا البيان الرفيع الشأن أن المصنف ترك الأمر الأول من الأمر الثاني وذكر الأمر الثاني من المردد بين أمرين فمن قال الممكنة مما لابد منها في الشكل الثاني متمسكا بكلام المصنف فقد خسر خسرانا مبينا؛ لأن معنى قوله: "وكون الممكنة مع الضرورية" أن الممكنة وجدت في الشكل الثاني، فعلى تقدير وجودها لابد أن يكون مع الضرورية أو كبرى مشروطة، وإن لم يوجد فيه فلا حاجة إليه. فافهم ولا تكن من المتعصبين إما أن يصدق الدوام إلخ : فإن قلت: قد يكون الشكل الثاني مركبا من صغرى دائمة وكبرى مشروطة مثلا، فحينئذ الدوام صادق على الصغرى مع كون الكبرى من القضايا المنعكسة السوالب قلت لا بأس فيه؛ فإن الترديد ليس على سبيل الحقيقية ولا على سبيل منع الجمع بل على سبيل منع الخلو، ولا مضايقة في اجتماع كلا الأمرين. (إسماعيل)
He said: 'With the permanence of the minor': i.e., it is required in this figure with respect to modality two things: First: one of two things is either that permanence is true of the minor by it being perpetual or necessary, or that the major is among the six propositions whose negatives are conversed, not among the seven whose negatives are not conversed. 'i.e., it is required etc.': i.e., for the production of the second figure with respect to modality, two things are required: one of them is a disjunctive concept between the minor being one of the two perpetuals, or the major being among the six propositions whose universal negatives are conversed by simple conversion; and the second is a disjunctive concept between that neither premise is possible (neither general nor particular), and that (the minor being one of the two possibles and the major being absolute necessary or conditional general or particular, and that the major being one of the two possibles and the minor being absolute necessary). (Abu al-Fath) The first: one of the two things. Verification of the station and refinement of the goal: That production of the second figure is conditioned with respect to modality by two things, each of which is a disjunctive concept. As for the first: it is a disjunctive concept between the minor being one of the two perpetuals and the major being among the propositions whose universal negatives are conversed by simple conversion. As for the second: it is a disjunctive concept between that neither premise is possible (neither general nor particular) and that (i.e., something from the two premises being one of the two possibles) is also a disjunctive concept between that the minor is one of the two possibles and the major is necessary or conditional general or particular, and that the major is one of the two possibles and the minor is necessary. And to this last disjunctive concept the author pointed by his saying: 'And the possible with the necessary or the conditional major'. So you have learned from this lofty statement that the author omitted the first part of the second condition and mentioned the second part of the disjunction. Therefore, whoever says that the possible is indispensable in the second figure, clinging to the author's words, has incurred a clear loss; because the meaning of his saying 'And the possible with the necessary' is that the possible is found in the second figure, so upon the supposition of its existence, it must be with the necessary or a conditional major; but if it does not occur in it, then there is no need for it. So understand and do not be among the fanatics. 'Either permanence is true etc.': For if you say: The second figure may be composed of a perpetual minor and a conditional major for example, then here permanence holds true of the minor while the major is among the propositions whose negatives are conversed. I say: There is no harm in that; for the disjunction is not in the manner of truth (exclusive) nor in the way of preventing combination, but in the way of preventing emptiness, and there is no difficulty in the combination of both matters. (Isma'il)
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage is an expand commentary on the modal conditions (shurut bihasab al-jiha) for a valid syllogism in the second figure. The commentator first explains the phrase 'with the permanence of the minor' from the matn. This refers to two requirements. Requirement 1 is a disjunction: either the minor premise is a perpetual (da'ima) or necessary proposition, or the major premise belongs to the six types of propositions whose universal negatives can be simply converted (i.e., the contradictory converse yields a true universal). Requirement 2 is another disjunction: either neither premise is a possible proposition (including both general and special possibility), or if one premise is possible, then the other must be necessary (absolute or conditional) – specifically, either the minor is possible and the major is necessary/conditional, or the major is possible and the minor is necessary. The commentator cites Abu al-Fath to clarify this structure. Then he provides 'achievement of the station' (tahqiq al-maqam) to refine the understanding: the author (musannif) of the matn, in stating 'and the possible with the necessary or the conditional major', only mentioned the second part of the second disjunction (i.e., when one premise is possible). So it would be a mistake to think that a possible premise is always required in the second figure; it is only required when one premise is possible, but the second figure can be productive without any possible premise at all. The commentator strongly warns against fanaticism on this point. Finally, he addresses a potential objection: what if the minor is perpetual and the major is conditional? The answer is that the two alternative conditions (perpetual minor vs. convertible major) are not exclusive; they can both hold simultaneously without contradiction, because the disjunction is not in the sense of preventing union but in the sense of preventing emptiness (i.e., at least one must hold, but both may hold). The explanation uses purely logical language but is essential for understanding modal syllogistics, particularly in the second figure.

Key Terms
دوام الصغرى
القضايا الست التي تنعكس سوالبها
الممكنة مع الضرورية
مفهوم مردد

Checkpoint
In the second figure, if the major premise is a possible proposition and the minor is absolute necessary, does this syllogism satisfy the modal conditions for production?
A) No, because the premises must both be necessary.
B) Yes, because the second disjunctive condition allows either premise to be possible as long as the other is necessary.
C) No, because the major must be perpetual.
D) Yes, only if the major is conditional.

Key Takeaway
The modal conditions for the second figure are two disjunctions: one regarding permanence/conversion, the other regarding possibility/necessity. Neither condition is single-valued; each offers alternatives that can be satisfied in multiple ways.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Syllogism
