Two Modal Conditions for Second Figure Syllogisms
Explains two modal conditions for valid second figure syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the two modal conditions required for a valid second figure syllogism.

Core Idea
Second figure modal validity requires either a perpetual minor or a convertible negative major, and the possible must pair with the necessary.

The second figure of the categorical syllogism has specific modal constraints beyond its formal rules. This passage from the Tahzeeb explains two disjunctive conditions that govern the modal status of the minor and major premises. Mastering these conditions ensures that the syllogism produces a consistent universal negative conclusion regardless of how the premises are instantiated. Let us unpack each condition and its proof through examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first condition (al-shart al-awwal)
The first condition is disjunctive: either the minor premise must be perpetual (dā'ima) or necessary (ḍarūriyya), OR the major premise must belong to the six proposition types whose negative universals convert simply (in'ikās). Find the two alternatives stated in the text.
The text mentions 'six propositions' (al-qadaya al-sitt) whose negatives convert, as opposed to 'seven' that do not.
2
Identify the second condition (al-shart al-thānī)
The second condition also offers alternatives: the possible premise (al-mumkina) can only be used in the second figure if paired with a necessary premise (ḍarūriyya) or a conditioned major (mashrūṭa). Note the specific constraints: if the possible is minor, the major must be necessary or conditioned; if the possible is major, the minor must be necessary only.
The text summarises this in one sentence: 'if the possible is minor, the major must be necessary or conditioned; if the possible is major, the minor must be necessary only.'
3
Understand the proof (dālīl al-sharṭayn)
The proof is that without these conditions, the same syllogistic form could yield different conclusions when the major premise is replaced. The passage gives two examples: one for loss of the first condition, one for loss of the second. In each, swapping the major changes the conclusion from affirmative to negative, which shows invalidity.
The examples use astronomical and mundane terms (lunar eclipse, donkey, etc.), but the principle applies to any Islamic science argument.
4
Apply to an Islamic science example
Take a kalam argument: 'Every necessary being is eternal; no contingent being is eternal; therefore no contingent being is a necessary being.' This is a valid second figure syllogism (Camestres). Now consider modal versions: if the minor 'Every necessary being is eternal' is necessary (ḍarūriyya), the first condition is satisfied. If the major 'No contingent being is eternal' is also necessary, the second condition is satisfied (possible not involved). Check what happens if the major were only possible.
Modal conditions ensure the conclusion is universal negative and does not vary with different major premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شرائط الشكل الثاني مع دوام الصغرى أو انعكاس سالبة الكبرى وكون الممكنة مع الضرورية أو الكبرى المشروطة؛ لينتج الكليتان سالبة كلية. قوله مع دوام الصغرى: أي يشترط في هذا الشكل بحسب الجهة أمران: الأول: أحد الأمرين هو إما أن يصدق الدوام على الصغرى بأن تكون دائمة أو ضرورية، وإما أن تكون الكبرى من القضايا الست التي تنعكس سوالبها لا من السبع التي لا تنعكس سوالبها. والثاني أحد الأمرين: وهو أن الممكنة لا تستعمل في هذا الشكل إلا مع الضرورية، سواء كانت الضرورية صغرى أو كبرى أو مع كبرى مشروطة عامة أو خاصة، وحاصله أن الممكنة إن كانت صغرى كانت الكبرى ضرورية أو مشروطة عامة أو خاصة، وإن كانت كبرى كانت الصغرى ضرورية لا غير. ودليل الشرطين: أنه لولا هما لزم اختلاف النتيجة. مثال فقدان الشرط الأول: كل منخسف مظلم ما دام منخسفا ولا شيء من القمر بمظلم وقت التربيع لا دائما فينتج موجبة هي كل منخسف قمر، ولو بدلنا الكبرى بقولنا: لا شيء من الشمس مظلم وقت عدم الكسوف لا دائما فينتج سالبة هي لا شيء من المنخسف بشمس. ومثال فقدان الشرط الثاني: كل حمار مركوب زيد بالإمكان ولا شيء من الناهق بمركوب زيد دائما ينتج موجبة هي كل حمار ناهق، ولو بدلنا الكبرى بقولنا: لا شيء من الفيل بمركوب زيد دائما ينتج سالبة، أي لا شيء من الحمار بفيل.
Conditions for the second figure: with the permanence of the minor or the conversion of the negative of the major, and the possible being with the necessary or the conditioned major, so that the two universal [premises] produce a universal negative. His saying 'with the permanence of the minor' means: two things are required in this figure with respect to modality: First, one of two alternatives: either permanence is true of the minor such that it is perpetual or necessary, or the major is among the six propositions whose negative universals convert, not among the seven whose negative universals do not convert. And the second [condition] is one of two alternatives: that the possible is not used in this figure except with the necessary, whether the necessary is minor or major, or with a major that is conditioned generally or specifically. Its summary: if the possible is minor, the major must be necessary or conditioned generally or specifically; if it is major, the minor must be necessary only. The proof of the two conditions: without them, difference in conclusion would occur. Example of loss of the first condition: 'Every lunar eclipse is dark as long as it is eclipsed, and nothing of the moon is dark at the time of quadrature not always' yields a positive conclusion 'Every lunar eclipse is the moon'; if we replace the major with 'Nothing of the sun is dark at the time of no eclipse not always' it yields a negative conclusion 'Nothing of the lunar eclipse is the sun.' Example of loss of the second condition: 'Every donkey is ridden by Zaid possibly, and nothing of the braying is ridden by Zaid always' yields a positive conclusion 'Every donkey is braying'; if we replace the major with 'Nothing of the elephant is ridden by Zaid always' it yields a negative conclusion 'Nothing of the donkey is an elephant.'
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage explains the two paired modal conditions required for a valid second figure syllogism. The first condition is disjunctive: either the minor premise must be perpetual or necessary (dawam), or the major premise must be among the six propositions whose negative universals convert by simple conversion. The second condition is also disjunctive but focuses on the possible: the possible premise can only be used with a necessary or conditioned counterpart; specifically, if the possible is minor, the major must be necessary or conditioned; if the possible is major, the minor must be necessary only. The commentary then gives a proof: without these conditions, the same premises would yield differing conclusions, which is invalid. Two examples illustrate: the first example changes the major to show a difference in quality of conclusion; the second example similarly shows a difference when the major is replaced. The text emphasizes that the conditions are not both required simultaneously but are alternative requirements.

The second figure requires specific modal conditions to ensure a consistent universal negative conclusion; these conditions are disjunctive and involve the permanence of the minor or convertibility of the major's negative, and the pairing of possible with necessary.

Deriving the Four Productive Moods of the Second Figure
Enumerates the four productive moods of the second figure and proves by elimination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to replicate the elimination process to derive the four productive moods of the second figure from the sixteen possible combinations.

Core Idea
Twelve invalid moods are eliminated by just two conditions.

This passage shows how the second figure's productive moods are not just listed but derived systematically. By applying two conditions, twelve of sixteen possible combinations are ruled out, leaving only the four that produce valid conclusions. This reveals the elegance of Aristotelian logic as adapted by Islamic logicians.

Guided Path
1
Enumerate all 16 possible moods
The second figure has four possible majors (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative) and four possible minors (the same four). Multiplying gives 16 combinations. List them systematically.
This is the total set before any elimination.
2
Apply the first condition: opposite quality
The first condition eliminates moods where both premises are affirmative or both negative. This removes 8 combinations: the 4 where both are affirmative (AA, AI, IA, II) and the 4 where both are negative (EE, EO, OE, OO).
This condition ensures the middle term is distributed at least once.
3
Apply the second condition: major must be universal
From the remaining 8 (opposite quality), eliminate those where the major is particular. That removes 4: the 2 with particular affirmative major (IE, IO) and the 2 with particular negative major (EA, EI).
The second figure requires the major to be universal to avoid an undistributed middle.
4
Identify the four remaining moods
The surviving 4 moods are: universal affirmative major with universal negative minor (AEE-2), universal affirmative major with particular negative minor (AOO-2), universal negative major with universal affirmative minor (EAE-2), and universal negative major with particular affirmative minor (EIO-2). These are the productive moods.
These correspond to the moods Cesare, Camestres, Festino, and Baroco in the standard square.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage explains how to derive the four productive moods of the second figure. The author states that the productive moods are four: (1) universal affirmative major with universal negative minor, (2) universal affirmative major with particular negative minor, (3) universal negative major with universal affirmative minor, (4) universal negative major with particular affirmative minor. The proof uses the two previously established conditions to eliminate the other twelve combinations from the total sixteen possible. The elimination is two-fold: the first condition removes eight moods where both premises are affirmative or both negative; the second condition removes four more where the major is particular affirmative with two negatives, or particular negative with two affirmatives. Thus only four remain. This demonstrates the conciseness of the logical system.

The Syllogism

Two Conditions for Valid Second-Figure Syllogisms
Commentary states two conditions for valid syllogisms in the second figure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two conditions for valid syllogisms in the second figure and evaluate whether a given modal syllogism satisfies them.

Core Idea
The second figure requires a universal negative conclusion and special modal restrictions.

In the second figure, not every combination of premises yields a valid syllogism. The commentary explains two crucial conditions: one for the quality of the conclusion, and another for modal propositions. Understanding these conditions ensures you can construct valid syllogisms and avoid fallacies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first condition
The first condition is that the premises must be such that they yield a universal negative conclusion. This is the minimal requirement for the second figure.
If the conclusion is not universal negative, the syllogism is invalid regardless of modal considerations.
2
Identify the second condition
The second condition concerns modal premises: the possible (mumkina) can only be used with the necessary (daruriyya) or a conditioned (mashruta) major. The possible cannot appear without these restrictions.
This condition applies only when one premise is possible; if no premise is possible, no restriction is needed.
3
Distinguish the two subcases
If the possible is the minor premise, the major must be necessary or conditioned (general or specific). If the possible is the major premise, the minor must be necessary only.
The author omitted the case where no premise is possible; the commentator clarifies that the possible is not obligatory.
4
Understand the proof
Without these conditions, the conclusion would vary (ikhtilaf al-natija). That is, changing a premise while keeping the same figure leads to different conclusions, indicating invalidity.
Consistency of conclusion across similar forms is a sign of validity.
5
Test with examples
The eclipse example violates the first condition: it yields an affirmative conclusion. Changing the major produces a negative conclusion, showing inconsistency. The donkey example violates the second condition: with the same minor and different majors, the conclusion changes.
These examples illustrate why the conditions are necessary.

Conditions of the second figure: [first] both premises must be such that they produce a universal negative conclusion. And the second is one of two things: that the possible (mumkina) is not used in this figure except with the necessary (daruriyya), whether the necessary is minor or major, or with a major that is conditioned (mashruta) general or specific. Its summary: if the possible is minor, the major must be necessary or conditioned general or specific; if it is major, the minor must be necessary only. The proof of the two conditions: that without them, the conclusion would vary. Detail is not suitable for this abridgement. His statement: 'so that both produce' — the productive moods in this figure are also four, resulting from multiplying the universal affirmative major with the two negative minors (universal and particular) and the universal negative major. And the second etc.: explanation: the second matter is a concept that is ambiguous between that none of the two premises be possible (not general nor specific) and that it be. This, i.e., that one of the two premises is one of the two possibles, is also ambiguous between that the minor be one of the two possibles and the major be necessary or conditioned general or specific, and that the major be one of the two possibles and the minor be necessary. The author omitted the first part of the second matter and mentioned the second part saying: 'and the possible's occurrence with the necessary or with a conditioned major.' The meaning: if the possible occurs in the second figure, it must be with the necessary or a conditioned major; if it does not occur, no problem. Whoever says that the possible is necessary in the second figure, clinging to the author's words, has indeed lost a manifest loss. (Abd) The conclusion would vary, e.g.: Every eclipsed [body] is dark as long as it is eclipsed, and nothing of the moon is dark at the time of quadrature not always, so it produces an affirmative conclusion: every eclipsed is a moon. If we change the major to: nothing of the sun is dark at the time of no eclipse not always, it produces a negative conclusion: nothing of the eclipsed is a sun. This is an example of lacking the first condition. Example of lacking the second condition: Every donkey is Zayd's mount possibly, and nothing of the brayer is Zayd's mount always, produces an affirmative conclusion: every donkey is a brayer. If we change the major to: nothing of the elephant is Zayd's mount always, it produces a negative conclusion: nothing of the donkey is an elephant.

Commentary
This passage explains two conditions for valid syllogisms in the second figure. The first condition is that the premises must be such that they yield a universal negative conclusion. The second condition concerns modal premises: the possible (mumkina) can only be used with the necessary (daruriyya) or with a conditioned (mashruta) major. The author clarifies that if the possible is the minor, the major must be necessary or conditioned; if the possible is the major, the minor must be necessary. The proof given is that without these conditions, the conclusion would vary. The commentator then elaborates that the author omitted the case where no premise is possible and only mentioned the case where one premise is possible, emphasizing that the possible is not obligatory in the second figure. Finally, examples illustrate violation of each condition: in the first example, changing the major to a different negative premise changes the conclusion from affirmative to negative, showing inconsistency. In the second example, using an unnecessary major also changes the conclusion. These examples demonstrate why the conditions are necessary.

The Four Moods of the Second Figure
Enumerates the four productive moods of the second figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four productive moods of the second figure and state their conclusions based on premise quantity, quality, and order.

Core Idea
The second figure only yields negative conclusions—universal or particular.

In the second figure of the categorical syllogism, the middle term is the predicate in both premises. This figure never produces affirmative conclusions. The author of our text lists exactly four productive moods, each defined by the quantity and quality of the minor and major premises. Understanding these moods is essential for constructing valid proofs in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figure
Confirm the syllogism is in the second figure: the middle term must be the predicate in both the major and minor premises. For example, in 'Every B is A; no C is A', the middle term A is predicate in both.
2
Classify the premises by quantity and quality
Determine whether each premise is universal or particular, affirmative or negative. The text groups moods by whether both premises are universal or mixed in quantity.
3
Apply the mood rules
If both premises are universal: minor affirmative + major negative → universal negative (mood 1); minor negative + major affirmative → universal negative (mood 2). If mixed: minor particular affirmative + major universal negative → particular negative (mood 3); minor particular negative + major universal affirmative → particular negative (mood 4).
The conclusion is always negative; never affirmative.
4
Verify with the example
For mood 4: 'Some animal is not human; every rational is human' yields 'Some animal is not rational'. Check that the conclusion follows and is negative.

لإيجابه: أي لإيجاب نقيض النتيجة صغرى الشكل الأول؛ لأن الشكل الثاني لا ينتج إلا سالبة فيكون نقيضها موجبة ألبتة.
Conditions of the second figure: and the two that differ in quantity also produce a particular negative by contradiction or conversion of the major or minor premise. Then the ordering among the two affirmative minors: the first mood is composed of two universal premises with a minor affirmative, e.g., every B is A, and no C is A; the second mood is composed of two universal premises with a minor negative, e.g., no B is A, and every C is A; in both the conclusion is a universal negative, e.g., no C is B. The author indicated these two by his saying: 'the two universal ones produce a universal negative.' The third mood is composed of a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major, e.g., some B is A, and no C is A; the fourth mood is composed of a particular negative minor and a universal affirmative major, e.g., some B is not A, and every C is A; in both the conclusion is a particular negative, e.g., some C is not B. The author indicated these two by his saying: 'and the two that differ in quantity also': meaning just as they differ in quality based on what preceded in the conditions, they produce a particular negative. His saying: 'by contradiction' means the proof of the production of these moods for these two conclusions are matters: the first is contradiction, which is to take the contradictory of the conclusion to make it an affirmative minor. And the fourth mood etc., like: some animal is not human, and every rational is human, produces some animal is not rational. So the second figure produces two conclusions: universal negative and particular negative, not the four quantified propositions. Particular negative: And this table encompasses all sixteen possible moods; the productive ones are four, and the invalid ones are twelve. You should study this table so that the ranks of the remaining moods, their order, number, and conclusions become clear to you. The majors: affirmative, affirmative, negative, negative; the minors: universal, particular, universal, particular; universal affirmative: S, S, ?????, S; particular affirmative: S, S, ?????, S; universal negative: S, S, S; particular negative: ?????, S, S, S. His saying: 'for its affirmation': meaning to make the contradictory of the conclusion an affirmative minor of the first figure, because the second figure only produces a negative, so its contradictory is necessarily affirmative.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the conditions and moods of the second figure of the categorical syllogism. The author first states that in the second figure, any mood that differs in quantity (i.e., has one universal and one particular premise) also yields a particular negative conclusion, and this can be proven by three methods: contradiction (al-khalf), conversion of the major, or conversion of the minor. He then describes the four productive moods of the second figure. The first two moods have both premises universal: in the first mood the minor is affirmative and the major negative ('every B is A, no C is A'), and in the second mood the minor is negative and the major affirmative ('no B is A, every C is A'). Both produce a universal negative conclusion. The next two moods have mixed quantity: the third mood has a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major, the fourth has a particular negative minor and a universal affirmative major. Both produce a particular negative conclusion. The author notes that the second figure never yields affirmative conclusions—only universal or particular negatives. He then provides an example for the fourth mood: 'some animal is not human, and every rational is human, therefore some animal is not rational.' Following this, he presents a table that exhaustively enumerates all 16 possible premise combinations (four types of major × four types of minor) for the second figure, marking which are productive (4) and which are invalid (12). Finally, he begins to explain the first proof method (contradiction): one takes the contradictory of the conclusion (which is necessarily affirmative because the conclusion is negative) and uses it as the minor premise of a first-figure syllogism. This is the first step in the proof by contradiction, which will be elaborated in subsequent lessons.

The Syllogism

Proving Second Figure Moods: Contradiction (al-Khalf) and Conversion
Introduces proof methods for second figure moods, focusing on contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to use the method of contradiction (al-khalf) to prove the validity of a second figure syllogism by reducing it to the first figure.

Core Idea
In the second figure, we prove conclusions by assuming the contradictory and deriving a contradiction using the first figure.

The second figure's moods are not self-evident; they require proof. The author introduces two proof methods: contradiction (al-khalf) and conversion (aks). The most powerful method is al-khalf: we assume the contradictory of the desired conclusion (which is affirmative because the conclusion is negative), combine it with one of the original premises, and form a first-figure syllogism that contradicts the other original premise. This establishes the original conclusion by reductio ad absurdum.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion and its contradictory
Start with a valid second figure mood, e.g., first mood: every B is A; no C is A; therefore no C is B. The conclusion 'no C is B' is universal negative. Its contradictory is 'some C is B' (particular affirmative).
The contradictory must be affirmative because the conclusion is negative.
2
Use the contradictory as a minor premise in the first figure
Take the contradictory (some C is B) as the minor premise of a first-figure syllogism. Combine it with the original major premise (no C is A) or minor premise (every B is A). The text suggests using it as a minor with the major to derive a contradiction with the minor.
The chosen combination must produce a conflict with one of the original premises.
3
Derive the contradiction
From 'some C is B' (minor) and 'no C is A' (major), by first figure (Ferio: no C is A, some C is B → some B is not A?) Actually careful: The first-figure syllogism: major: no C is A; minor: some C is B; conclusion: some B is not A? That does not directly contradict 'every B is A'. Alternatively, use the other premise: 'some C is B' with 'every B is A' yields 'some C is A' (first figure Darii). But 'no C is A' is the major, so 'some C is A' contradicts 'no C is A'. Thus we have contradiction.
The contradiction proves the original conclusion must be true.
4
Generalize the method
For any second figure mood, the same procedure works: assume the contradictory of the conclusion (affirmative), combine with the appropriate original premise to form a first-figure syllogism whose conclusion contradicts the other original premise.
This method is called 'khalf' and is a standard proof technique in Islamic logic.

Conditions of the second figure: and the two that differ in quantity also produce a particular negative by contradiction or conversion of the major or minor premise. Then the ordering among the two affirmative minors: the first mood is composed of two universal premises with a minor affirmative, e.g., every B is A, and no C is A; the second mood is composed of two universal premises with a minor negative, e.g., no B is A, and every C is A; in both the conclusion is a universal negative, e.g., no C is B. The author indicated these two by his saying: 'the two universal ones produce a universal negative.' The third mood is composed of a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major, e.g., some B is A, and no C is A; the fourth mood is composed of a particular negative minor and a universal affirmative major, e.g., some B is not A, and every C is A; in both the conclusion is a particular negative, e.g., some C is not B. The author indicated these two by his saying: 'and the two that differ in quantity also': meaning just as they differ in quality based on what preceded in the conditions, they produce a particular negative. His saying: 'by contradiction' means the proof of the production of these moods for these two conclusions are matters: the first is contradiction, which is to take the contradictory of the conclusion to make it an affirmative minor. And the fourth mood etc., like: some animal is not human, and every rational is human, produces some animal is not rational. So the second figure produces two conclusions: universal negative and particular negative, not the four quantified propositions. Particular negative: And this table encompasses all sixteen possible moods; the productive ones are four, and the invalid ones are twelve. You should study this table so that the ranks of the remaining moods, their order, number, and conclusions become clear to you. His saying: 'for its affirmation': meaning to make the contradictory of the conclusion an affirmative minor of the first figure, because the second figure only produces a negative, so its contradictory is necessarily affirmative.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the conditions and moods of the second figure of the categorical syllogism. The author first states that in the second figure, any mood that differs in quantity (i.e., has one universal and one particular premise) also yields a particular negative conclusion, and this can be proven by three methods: contradiction (al-khalf), conversion of the major, or conversion of the minor. He then describes the four productive moods of the second figure. The first two moods have both premises universal: in the first mood the minor is affirmative and the major negative ('every B is A, no C is A'), and in the second mood the minor is negative and the major affirmative ('no B is A, every C is A'). Both produce a universal negative conclusion. The next two moods have mixed quantity: the third mood has a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major, the fourth has a particular negative minor and a universal affirmative major. Both produce a particular negative conclusion. The author notes that the second figure never yields affirmative conclusions—only universal or particular negatives. He then provides an example for the fourth mood: 'some animal is not human, and every rational is human, therefore some animal is not rational.' Following this, he presents a table that exhaustively enumerates all 16 possible premise combinations (four types of major × four types of minor) for the second figure, marking which are productive (4) and which are invalid (12). Finally, he begins to explain the first proof method (contradiction): one takes the contradictory of the conclusion (which is necessarily affirmative because the conclusion is negative) and uses it as the minor premise of a first-figure syllogism. This is the first step in the proof by contradiction, which will be elaborated in subsequent lessons.

The Syllogism
intermediate

Proofs in the Third Figure: Contradiction and Conversion
Author applies contradiction and conversion to the third figure, limiting conversion to certain moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply proof by contradiction (khalf) and conversion of the major (aks al-kubra) to validate third-figure categorical syllogisms in Islamic logical arguments.

Core Idea
Two methods prove the third figure: contradiction works for all moods, conversion only for universal negative majors.

The third figure of the categorical syllogism presents unique challenges because the middle term is the subject in both premises. Classical logicians developed two methods to establish its validity: proof by contradiction (khalf) and conversion of the major (aks al-kubra). This lesson unpacks how each method works, when it applies, and why conversion is limited to certain moods, using the standard example from the tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two proof methods
The passage introduces two methods: khalf (من الخلف) and aks al-kubra (من عكس الكبرى). Khalf works by assuming the opposite of the conclusion and deriving a contradiction. Aks al-kubra converts the major premise to reduce the syllogism to the first figure.
Khalf is also called reductio ad impossibile.
2
Understand the contradiction procedure
To prove by contradiction, assume the contradictory of the conclusion as true. Use this assumed premise as the minor with the original major premise in a first-figure syllogism. The derived conclusion will contradict the original minor premise, which is known true, thereby proving the assumption false and the original conclusion true.
The contradictory of the conclusion is always a particular affirmative, while the original major is universal, ensuring the first figure works.
3
Work through the khalf example
Take the syllogism: 'Every human is an animal' (minor), 'No stone is an animal' (major), therefore 'No human is a stone' (conclusion). Assume the contradictory: 'Some human is a stone'. Using this as minor with the major 'No stone is an animal' in the first figure yields 'Some human is not an animal', which contradicts the true minor 'Every human is an animal'. Thus the original conclusion is proven.
This is a second-figure example used to illustrate khalf; the same logic applies to the third figure.
4
Apply conversion of the major
For moods where the major is universal negative (e.g., 'No A is B'), convert it to 'No B is A'. The resulting syllogism becomes a valid first-figure syllogism. Example: Convert 'No stone is an animal' to 'No animal is a stone', then with 'Every human is an animal', we obtain 'No human is a stone'.
Universal negative converts to itself; universal affirmative converts only to particular affirmative, which is insufficient for a first-figure major.
5
Recognize the limitation of conversion
Conversion works only for the first and third moods of the third figure, where the major is universal negative. In the other two moods, the major is universal affirmative, which converts to particular affirmative, not suitable as a major in the first figure. Additionally, those minors are negative, unfit for a first-figure minor.
This limitation is explicitly stated in the passage and highlighted in the glosses.

وكبرى القياس أي وأن يجعل كبرى الشكل الثاني كبرى الشكل الأول؛ لأن كلية الكبرى شرط في الشكل الثاني، فيكون كبراه صالحة لكبروية الشكل الأول ما ينافي الصغرى وتصوير الخلف أن نقول: كل إنسان حيوان، ولا شيء من الحجر بحيوان ينتج: لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر، فإذا لم تصدق هذه النتيجة يصدق نقيضها؛ لأن ارتفاع النقيضين محال، وهو بعض الإنسان ،حجر، فإذا جعل هذا النقيض صغرى لتلك الكبرى، ويقال بعض الإنسان ،حجر، ولا شيء من الحجر بحيوان ينتج من الشكل الأول بعض الإنسان ليس بحيوان، وهذا مناف بل مناقض لصغرى الشكل الثاني أعني كل إنسان حيوان وهذا مسلم الثبوت، فيكون النتيجة الحاصلة من الشكل الأول كاذبة ومنشأه ليس إلا الصغرى فيكون نقيضها حقا، وهو لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر، وهذا عين نتيجة الشكل الثاني، وقس عليه العمل في سائر الضروب ما ينافي الصغرى: وهذا المحال لم ينشأ تلقاء الكبرى ولا من تلك الهيئة؛ فإن الكبرى مفروض الصدق كالصغرى والشكل الأول بديهي الإنتاج، من فمنشأه ليس إلا نقيض النتيجة فهو باطل، فالنتيجة حق، وقس على هذا إنتاج الضروب الأخر. إسماعيل) وهذا: أي الخلف جار في الضروب الأربعة كلها؛ فإن نقيض النتيجة في كل من هذه الضروب لا يكون إلا جزئية؛ فإن النتيجة في كلها سالبة كلية أو جزئية، ونقيض السالبة يكون موجبة وكبرى القياس كلية بلا ريب؛ فإن الشكل الثاني يشترط بحسب الكمية فيه كلية الكبرى. إسماعيل) وذلك أي عكس الكبرى، وتصويره: أن نقول: كل إنسان حيوان ولا شيء من الحجر بحيوان ينتج: لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر ولو انعكس الكبرى لكان شكلا أولا ؛ لأن صغرى الشكل الثاني كصغرى الشكل الأول؛ لأن الحد الأوسط محمول فيهما، وإنما المخالفة في الكبرى، فلما عكسنا صار شكلا أولا بالضرورة :هكذا كل إنسان حيوان ولا شيء من الحيوان بحجر فينتج تلك النتيجة، ولما كان العكس جاريا في الضربين دون الأمر الثالث قدمه عليه. (عبد)
Conditions of the third figure: Then the conclusion, and in the third figure, the major premise of the syllogism, because of its universality, is major; so that from the first figure something contradictory to the minor premise is produced. This applies to all four moods. The second method is conversion of the major to reduce to the first figure to produce the desired conclusion. However, this only works in the first and third moods because their majors are universal negative, which convert as themselves. As for the other two, their majors are universal affirmative, which convert only to particular affirmative, not suitable as a major in the first figure, especially since their minors are also negative and not valid as minors in the first figure. 'And the major of the syllogism' means that one makes the major of the second figure the major of the first figure, because the universality of the major is a condition in the second figure, so its major is suitable for being a major in the first figure, contradicting the minor. And the depiction of contradiction: we say: every human is an animal, and no stone is an animal, produces: no human is a stone. If this conclusion is not true, then its contradictory is true, because the rise of both contradictories is impossible. That contradictory is 'some human is a stone.' So when we make this contradictory a minor for that major, and we say 'some human is a stone, and no stone is an animal,' the first figure produces 'some human is not an animal.' This is contrary, indeed contradictory, to the minor of the second figure, namely 'every human is an animal,' which is conceded as true. Hence the conclusion produced from the first figure is false, and its source is nothing but the contradictory of the original conclusion, so that contradictory is false, and the original conclusion is true. This is the very conclusion of the second figure. Apply this reasoning to the other moods. 'Contradicts the minor': this impossibility did not arise from the major or from that composition, because the major is assumed true like the minor, and the first figure is intuitively productive. So its source is only the contradictory of the conclusion, so it is false, thus the conclusion is true. Apply this to the production of the other moods. (Isma‘il) This: meaning contradiction applies to all four moods, for the contradictory of the conclusion in each of these moods is only particular, because the conclusion in all of them is universal or particular negative, and the contradictory of a negative is affirmative, and the major of the syllogism is universal without doubt, because the second figure requires in terms of quantity the universality of the major. (Isma‘il) That: meaning conversion of the major, and its depiction: we say: every human is an animal, and no stone is an animal, produces: no human is a stone. If we convert the major, it becomes a first figure: because the minor of the second figure is like the minor of the first figure, since the middle term is predicated in both, and the difference is only in the major. So when we convert, it necessarily becomes a first figure: thus, every human is an animal, and no animal is a stone, produces that same conclusion. And because conversion applies to two moods but not the third, he presented it before it. ('Abd)
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of syllogistic figures, now focusing on the third figure. The author states the conditions and applies the two proof methods—contradiction (khalf) and conversion of the major (aks al-kubra)—to the third figure. He explains that contradiction works for all four moods of the third figure: one takes the contradictory of the conclusion (which is particular affirmative, since conclusions are negative) and uses it as a minor premise with the original major in the first figure to deduce something that contradicts the given minor premise, thereby proving the original conclusion. The author then provides a detailed example: 'Every human is an animal, no stone is an animal, therefore no human is a stone.' If we assume the conclusion false, its contradictory 'some human is a stone' becomes true; using this as a minor with the original major 'no stone is an animal' in the first figure yields 'some human is not an animal,' which contradicts the given minor 'every human is an animal' (which is true). Thus the assumption leads to a contradiction, proving the original conclusion true. The author emphasizes that this contradiction does not arise from the major or the form but solely from the assumption of the contradictory conclusion.

Next, the author discusses conversion of the major. This method only works for two moods of the third figure (the first and third moods) because in those, the major premise is universal negative, which converts to itself (i.e., a universal negative). In the other two moods, the major is universal affirmative, which converts only to a particular affirmative, which cannot serve as a major in the first figure; additionally, those minors are negative and unsuitable as minors in the first figure. The author gives an example: converting 'no stone is an animal' to 'no animal is a stone' turns the second figure into a valid first-figure syllogism: 'every human is an animal, no animal is a stone, therefore no human is a stone.' The marginal glosses by Isma‘il and ‘Abd clarify that the contradictory of the conclusion is always particular because the conclusion is negative, and that conversion applies to two moods.

Al-Khalf (Reductio ad Absurdum) in the Second Figure
Explains the method of al-khalf to prove negative conclusions in the second figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a reductio ad absurdum proof for a negative conclusion in the second figure of a categorical syllogism.

Core Idea
Al-khalf proves a conclusion by showing its contradictory leads to absurdity via the first figure.

In this passage, the author demonstrates how to prove the negative conclusion of a second-figure syllogism by reductio ad absurdum (al-khalf). The method relies on the self-evident validity of the first figure: if assuming the opposite of the desired conclusion produces a contradiction with known premises, the original conclusion must be true. Let's walk through the steps using the provided example.

Guided Path
1
Identify the second figure syllogism and its conclusion
In the example, the minor premise is 'Every human is an animal' (A), the major premise is 'No stone is an animal' (E), and the conclusion is 'No human is a stone' (E). Note the conclusion is universal negative.
The second figure requires a universal major.
2
Assume the contradictory of the conclusion
The contradictory of 'No human is a stone' is 'Some human is a stone' (I). This is the assumption that will be tested.
The contradictory of a universal negative is a particular affirmative.
3
Combine with the original major in the first figure
Take the assumed contradictory as the new minor and the original major 'No stone is an animal' as the major. This forms a first figure syllogism: Minor: Some human is a stone, Major: No stone is an animal, Conclusion: Some human is not an animal.
This is the mood Ferio in the first figure, which is valid.
4
Observe the contradiction and reject the assumption
The derived conclusion 'Some human is not an animal' contradicts the original minor 'Every human is an animal'. Since the original minor is true and the first figure is valid, the only source of falsehood is the assumed contradictory. Therefore, the contradictory is false, and the original conclusion 'No human is a stone' is true.
This is the essence of al-khalf: proving a conclusion by refuting its opposite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكبرى القياس أي وأن يجعل كبرى الشكل الثاني كبرى الشكل الأول؛ لأن كلية الكبرى شرط في الشكل الثاني، فيكون كبراه صالحة لكبروية الشكل الأول ما ينافي الصغرى وتصوير الخلف أن نقول: كل إنسان حيوان، ولا شيء من الحجر بحيوان ينتج: لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر، فإذا لم تصدق هذه النتيجة يصدق نقيضها؛ لأن ارتفاع النقيضين محال، وهو بعض الإنسان حجر، فإذا جعل هذا النقيض صغرى لتلك الكبرى، ويقال بعض الإنسان حجر، ولا شيء من الحجر بحيوان ينتج من الشكل الأول بعض الإنسان ليس بحيوان، وهذا مناف بل مناقض لصغرى الشكل الثاني أعني كل إنسان حيوان وهذا مسلم الثبوت، فيكون النتيجة الحاصلة من الشكل الأول كاذبة ومنشأه ليس إلا الصغرى فيكون نقيضها حقا، وهو لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر، وهذا عين نتيجة الشكل الثاني، وقس عليه العمل في سائر الضروب ما ينافي الصغرى: وهذا المحال لم ينشأ تلقاء الكبرى ولا من تلك الهيئة؛ فإن الكبرى مفروض الصدق كالصغرى والشكل الأول بديهي الإنتاج، فمنشأه ليس إلا نقيض النتيجة فهو باطل، فالنتيجة حق، وقس على هذا إنتاج الضروب الأخر. إسماعيل) وهذا: أي الخلف جار في الضروب الأربعة كلها؛ فإن نقيض النتيجة في كل من هذه الضروب لا يكون إلا جزئية؛ فإن النتيجة في كلها سالبة كلية أو جزئية، ونقيض السالبة يكون موجبة وكبرى القياس كلية بلا ريب؛ فإن الشكل الثاني يشترط بحسب الكمية فيه كلية الكبرى. إسماعيل)
And the major premise of the syllogism: meaning that one makes the major premise of the second figure the major premise of the first figure, because the universality of the major is a condition in the second figure, so its major is suitable for being a major of the first figure that contradicts the minor. The illustration of reductio is that we say: 'Every human is an animal; no stone is an animal' produces: 'No human is a stone.' If this conclusion is not true, its contradictory is true, because the denial of both is impossible, and it is 'Some human is a stone.' Then if this contradictory is made the minor for that major, and it is said 'Some human is a stone; no stone is an animal,' it produces from the first figure: 'Some human is not an animal.' This is contrary and contradictory to the minor of the second figure, namely 'Every human is an animal,' and this is admitted as true. Therefore the conclusion obtained from the first figure is false, and its source is only the minor, so its contradictory is true, which is 'No human is a stone,' and this is exactly the conclusion of the second figure. Apply this to the remaining moods (the contradictory of the minor). This impossibility did not arise from the major nor from that arrangement, because the major is assumed true like the minor, and the first figure is intuitively productive. So its source is only the contradictory of the conclusion, so it is false, thus the conclusion is true. Apply this to the production of the other moods. (Isma'il) And this: i.e., al-khalf applies to all four moods; for the contradictory of the conclusion in each of these moods is only particular, because the conclusion in all of them is universal negative or particular negative, and the contradictory of a negative is affirmative, and the major of the syllogism is universal without doubt, because the second figure requires universality of the major in terms of quantity. (Isma'il)
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage explains the method of reductio ad absurdum (al-khalf) for proving negative conclusions in the second figure of the syllogism. The author begins by stating that the major premise of the second figure is also suitable to be the major of the first figure, because the second figure requires a universal major. Then he illustrates with an example: 'Every human is an animal; no stone is an animal' yields 'No human is a stone.' To prove this conclusion via reductio, we assume its contradictory, 'Some human is a stone,' and combine it with the original major 'No stone is an animal' in the first figure, yielding 'Some human is not an animal.' This contradicts the original minor 'Every human is an animal,' which is known to be true. Since the first figure is necessarily valid, the only source of falsehood is the assumed contradictory, which must therefore be false, so the original conclusion is true. The commentator Isma'il notes that this method works for all four moods of the second figure because the conclusion is always a universal or particular negative, and its contradictory is affirmative and particular, and the major remains universal—a condition of the second figure.

Key Terms
الخلف (al-khalf): A proof method assuming the contradictory of the desired conclusion to derive an absurdity, thereby establishing the conclusion. [Note: Stable term in logic; also used in legal reasoning for reductio ad absurdum.]
نقيض (naqīḍ): The contradictory opposite; for a universal negative, the contradictory is a particular affirmative. [Note: Standard definition in logic; no significant shift.]
الشكل الثاني (al-shakl al-thānī): The second figure of the categorical syllogism, where the middle term is predicate of the minor and subject of the major; requires a universal major. [Note: Standard definition across Islamic logic texts.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The contradictory of the major premise
C) The contradictory of the conclusion
D) The contradictory of the middle term

Key Takeaway
Al-khalf proves a negative conclusion in the second figure by assuming its contradictory, combining it with the original major in the first figure, and showing a contradiction with the original minor.

Reducing the Second Figure by Converting the Major
Explains reducing the second figure to the first by converting the major premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reduce a second-figure syllogism to the first figure by converting the major premise and verify the validity of the resulting syllogism.

Core Idea
Converting the major premise unlocks the first figure from the second.

The second figure of the categorical syllogism can be reduced to the first figure by converting the major premise. This technique works because the minor premise is already in first-figure form. In this lesson, we will walk through the reduction using the passage's example and understand its limitations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the second-figure structure
Note that the middle term (al-ḥadd al-awsaṭ) is the predicate of the minor premise and the subject of the major premise. In the passage, the minor is 'Every human is an animal' (middle: 'animal' as predicate) and the major is 'No stone is an animal' (middle: 'animal' as subject).
2
Recognize the minor is in first-figure form
In the first figure, the middle term is the predicate of the minor. Here the minor already has that structure, so it does not need any conversion.
This is why the author says the minor of the second figure is like the minor of the first figure.
3
Convert the major premise
The major is a universal negative (E proposition). Apply simple conversion: swap the subject and predicate while preserving quantity and quality. 'No stone is an animal' becomes 'No animal is a stone'.
Universal negatives convert simply and validly.
4
Reassemble the first-figure syllogism
Combine the unchanged minor with the converted major: 'Every human is an animal; no animal is a stone.' The conclusion remains 'No human is a stone,' which is exactly the same as the original. The syllogism is now in the first figure (Barbara or Celarent depending on the mood? Actually this is Celarent: EAE in first figure).
This reduction works only for moods where the major is E (Cesare and Camestres in the second figure), not for the third mood (Baroco) where the major is A.

(عبد)
And that is: the conversion of the major. Its illustration: we say: 'Every human is an animal; no stone is an animal' produces: 'No human is a stone.' If the major were converted, it would become a first figure, because the minor of the second figure is like the minor of the first figure, since the middle term is predicated in both, and the difference is only in the major. So when we converted, it necessarily became a first figure: thus 'Every human is an animal; no animal is a stone' produces that same conclusion. And since conversion applies only to two moods (not the third), he presented it before it. (Abd)

This passage presents an alternative method for the second figure: converting the major premise to reduce it to the first figure. The author illustrates with the same example. In the second figure, the minor 'Every human is an animal' already has the middle term as predicate, same as in the first figure. The major 'No stone is an animal' has the middle as subject; to convert it to the first figure, we simply convert the major to 'No animal is a stone,' which is valid because universal negatives convert simply. The resulting syllogism is first figure: 'Every human is an animal; no animal is a stone' yields 'No human is a stone.' The commentator 'Abd notes that this method works only for two moods of the second figure (where the major is universal negative), not for the third mood (where the major is universal affirmative), which does not convert to a universal negative suitable for the first figure.

The Syllogism

Reducing the Third Figure by Converting the Minor
Explains reduction of third figure to first by converting minor and reversing order.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reduce a third-figure categorical syllogism to the first figure by converting the minor premise and reversing the order of premises.

Core Idea
Converting the minor universally and reversing premises directly yields a first-figure syllogism, whose conclusion is then converted to the desired one.

The third figure often produces indirect conclusions. To verify their validity, logicians reduce them to the first figure. This lesson explains a specific reduction method: convert the minor premise, reverse the order of premises, then convert the conclusion. Understanding this method is essential for validating syllogistic arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence, where premises may be ordered in ways that do not immediately conform to the natural first figure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original third-figure syllogism
Locate the minor premise (containing the subject of the conclusion) and the major premise (containing the predicate). For example: 'No human is a donkey; every brayer is a donkey.'
The minor is the premise with the subject of the conclusion, 'human'.
2
Convert the minor premise universally
The minor must be convertible as a universal. Here 'No human is a donkey' converts to 'No donkey is a human.' This conversion is an immediate inference (conversio simplex).
This only works if the minor is a universal negative; affirmative minors convert as particular, which is not sufficient.
3
Reverse the order of premises
Make the converted minor the new major premise, and the original major premise the new minor premise. Now the syllogism becomes: 'Every brayer is a donkey; no donkey is a human.' This is a first-figure syllogism.
The order reversal is not a logical inference but a rearrangement to reveal the figure.
4
Derive the intermediate conclusion and convert it
The first-figure syllogism directly yields a conclusion: 'No brayer is a human.' This is not yet the original desired conclusion. Convert this conclusion again: 'No human is a brayer,' which matches the original conclusion of the third-figure syllogism.
This second conversion is needed because the first-figure conclusion has the terms in reverse order relative to the original.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالث أن يعكس الصغرى فيصير شكلا رابعا، ثم يعكس الترتيب يعني يجعل عكس الصغرى كبرى والكبرى صغرى فيصير شكلا أولا؛ لينتج نتيجة تنعكس إلى النتيجة المطلوبة، وذلك إنما يتصور فيما يكون عكس الصغرى كلية ليصلح لكبروية الشكل الأول، وهذا إنما هو في الضرب الثاني؛ فإن صغراه سالبة كلية تنعكس كنفسها، وأما الأول والثالث فصغراهما موجبة لا تنعكس إلا جزئية. وأما الرابع فصغراه سالبة جزئية لا تنعكس ولو فرض انعكاسها لا تكون إلا جزئية أيضاً. فتدبر. قوله: إيجاب الصغرى و فعليتها لأن الحكم في كبراه سواء كان إيجابا أو سلبا على أوسط بالفعل كما مر. فلو لم يتحد الأصغر مع الأوسط بالفعل بأن لا يتحد أصلا وتكون الصغرى سالبة أو يتحد لكن لا بالفعل وتكون الصغرى موجبة ممكنة ما هو أن يعكس الصغرى وتصويره: أن نقول لا شيء من الإنسان بحمار وكل ناهق حمار، ينتج: لاشيء من الإنسان بناهق؛ لأنه لو عكس الصغرى إلى لا شيء من الحمار بإنسان يصير شكلا رابعا، ثم إذا عكس الترتيب وقيل هكذا كل ناهق حمار، ولا شيء من الحمار بإنسان يصير شكلا أولا ينتج لا شيء من الناهق بإنسان، ثم إذا عكس ثاني النتيجة وقيل لا شيء من الإنسان بناهق يحصل عين تلك النتيجة الحاصلة من الشكل الثاني. (إسماعيل) تنعكس كنفسها فعكسها كلية تجعل صغرى في الشكل الرابع ثم بعد عكس الترتيب يجعل ذلك العكس كبرى في الشكل الأول للكلية. (برهان) فتدبر إشارة إلى دليل إنتاج الضرب الرابع، وهو إما الخلف أو الافتراض إذا كانت السالبة الجزئية مركبة ليتحقق وجود الموضوع. لا يتحد إلخ: أي بأن لا يكون بينهما حمل إيجابي فضلا أن يكون بالفعل أو بالإمكان فيكون الصغرى حينئذ سالبة لا محالة.
And the third is to convert the minor so it becomes a fourth figure, then reverse the order, meaning make the converted minor the major and the major the minor, so it becomes a first figure, to produce a result that converts to the desired conclusion. This is only conceivable when the conversion of the minor is universal, so it is suitable for the major of the first figure. This occurs only in the second mood (of the third figure), for its minor is universal negative converting like itself. As for the first and third moods, their minor is affirmative and converts only as particular. As for the fourth mood, its minor is particular negative and does not convert; even if it were assumed to convert, it would only be particular. So reflect. His saying: positivity and actuality of the minor, because the judgment in its major, whether affirmative or negative, is about the middle actually, as has passed. For if the minor does not unite with the middle actually—either not uniting at all so the minor is negative, or uniting but not actually so the minor is possible affirmative—then what? The illustration: we say: 'No human is a donkey; every brayer is a donkey' produces: 'No human is a brayer.' Because if you convert the minor to 'No donkey is a human,' it becomes a fourth figure. Then if you reverse the order and say: 'Every brayer is a donkey; no donkey is a human,' it becomes a first figure producing 'No brayer is a human.' Then if you convert the result a second time and say 'No human is a brayer,' you get exactly the conclusion produced from the second figure. (Abd) Regarding the second mood etc.: if we say 'No J is B; every A is B' then the conclusion is 'No J is A.' We say: this conclusion is true. For if we convert the minor of this mood and make the converted minor the major and the major the minor, we get the first figure: 'Every A is B; no B is J' produces 'No A is J.' This conclusion is true because it is a result of the intuitively productive first figure; its converse, i.e., our statement 'No J is A,' is absolutely true, and it is the desired conclusion. (Isma'il) 'Converts like itself'—its conversion is universal, making it a minor in the fourth figure, then after reversing the order, that conversion becomes the major in the first figure due to universality. (Burhan) 'So reflect' is an indication of the proof of the fourth mood's productivity, which is either by reductio or supposition if the particular negative is composite to realize the existence of the subject. 'Does not unite' etc.: meaning there is no affirmative predication between them, let alone actually or potentially, so the minor is necessarily negative.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage details the method of reducing the third figure to the first figure by first converting the minor premise. The author explains the steps: take a third-figure syllogism—e.g., 'No human is a donkey; every brayer is a donkey'—with the conclusion 'No human is a brayer.' First, convert the minor (negation universal) to 'No donkey is a human,' which places it as the major of a fourth-figure arrangement, then reverse the order so that the converted minor becomes the major and the original major becomes the minor, yielding a first-figure syllogism: 'Every brayer is a donkey; no donkey is a human,' which directly produces 'No brayer is a human.' Finally, convert this conclusion to obtain the original conclusion 'No human is a brayer.' The author notes that this method only works when the converted minor is universal, which occurs only in the second mood of the third figure (where the minor is universal negative). For other moods, the conversion yields only a particular, which is unsuitable. The commentators expand: 'Abd provides an abstract example with variables J, B, A, showing the conversion and reversal steps. Isma'il explains why the conversion of the universal negative minor remains universal. Burhan notes that the final reflection points to a proof for the fourth mood (particular negative minor) via reductio or supposition, since its minor does not convert directly. The passage also touches on the condition that the minor must be affirmative and actual (فعلية) for a valid third-figure syllogism; otherwise the minor is negative.

Key Terms
عكس الصغرى (ʿaks al-ṣughrā): Conversion of the minor premise in the third figure to a fourth-figure arrangement before reversal. [Note: This specific reduction technique is typical for the third figure in Aristotelian logic.]
عكس الترتيب (ʿaks al-tartīb): Reversing the order of premises after conversion: making the converted minor the major and the original major the minor. [Note: Unique to this reduction method; not a standard term elsewhere.]
فعلية الصغرى (fiʿliyyat al-ṣughrā): The actuality of the minor premise; the subject must actually have the predicate, not merely potentially. [Note: Refers to a condition for validity in categorical syllogisms; used in later logic texts.]

Conditions of the Third Figure and Its Six Productive Moods
Author states conditions of third figure and lists the six productive moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions of the third figure and enumerate its six productive moods using categorical syllogism forms.

Core Idea
The third figure requires an affirmative actual minor and at least one universal premise.

In this passage, the author explains the two key conditions for a valid third-figure syllogism and lists the six moods that satisfy them. Understanding these conditions allows you to evaluate any argument in the third figure and generate valid conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
The minor premise must be affirmative and actual (fi‘liyya), and at least one premise must be universal. These ensure the middle term truly connects the extremes.
The minor is the premise containing the subject of the conclusion.
2
Understand why both premises cannot be particular
If both are particular, the middle term may refer to different subsets, blocking the transfer of judgment. The passage illustrates this with the example: 'Some animal is a human, some animal is a horse' yields no necessary conclusion about humans and horses.
The example shows a third-figure syllogism (middle term 'animal' is subject in both).
3
Enumerate the six productive moods
Combine a universal affirmative minor with each of the four major types (A, E, I, O) to get four moods, and combine a particular affirmative minor with the two universal majors (A and E) to get two more. Total six.
These correspond to the classical moods Darapti, Felapton, Disamis, Datisi, Bocardo, Ferison.
4
Apply the conditions to test validity
Given any third-figure argument, check that the minor is affirmative and actual, and that at least one premise is universal. If both conditions hold, the mood is among the six; otherwise, it is invalid.
Use the conditions as a quick filter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شرائط الشكل الثالث: إيجاب الصغرى و فعليتها و كلية إحداهما؛ لينتج الموجبتان مع الموجبة الكلية أو بالعكس موجبة جزئية، ومع السالبة لم يتعد الحكم من الأوسط بالفعل إلى الأصغر. قوله مع كلية إحداهما: لأنه لو كانت المقدمتان جزئيتين الجاز أن يكون البعض من الأوسط المحكوم عليه بالأصغر غير البعض المحكوم عليه بالأكبر، فلا يلزم تعدية الحكم من الأكبر إلى الأصغر، مثلا يصدق بعض الحيوان إنسان وبعض الحيوان فرس ولا يصدق بعض الإنسان فرس. قوله لينتج الموجبتان الضروب المنتجة في هذا الشكل بحسب الشرائط المذكورة ستة حاصلة من ضم الصغرى الموجبة الكلية إلى الكبريات الأربع وضم الصغرى الموجبة الجزئية إلى الكبريين الكليتين.
Conditions of the third figure: the minor premise must be affirmative and actual, and one of the two premises must be universal; so that the two affirmative premises produce a universal affirmative or conversely a particular affirmative, and with a negative premise, the judgment does not transfer from the middle term actually to the minor term. His saying 'with the universality of one of them': because if both premises were particular, it is possible that some of the middle term judged by the minor is not the same as some judged by the major, so the transfer of the judgment from the major to the minor is not necessary; for example, 'Some animal is a human' and 'Some animal is a horse' are true, but 'Some human is a horse' is not true. His saying 'so that the two affirmative premises produce' – the productive moods in this figure according to the mentioned conditions are six, resulting from combining the universal affirmative minor with the four major premises, and combining the particular affirmative minor with the two universal major premises.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage explains the conditions of the third figure of the categorical syllogism and derives its productive moods. The author first states two conditions: (1) the minor premise must be affirmative and actual (i.e., asserting a real connection, not a possible one), and (2) at least one premise must be universal. The reason for these conditions is then elaborated. If both premises were particular, the middle term could refer to different subsets, so no necessary conclusion follows. For example, 'Some animal is a human' and 'Some animal is a horse' do not entail that some human is a horse. The passage then enumerates the six valid moods: combining a universal affirmative minor with each of the four types of major (universal affirmative, particular affirmative, universal negative, particular negative), and combining a particular affirmative minor with the two universal majors. This yields six moods, consistent with the standard list in classical logic.

The Syllogism
intermediate
12 min
Conditions and Productive Moods of the Third Figure
Author details conditions of the third figure and enumerates its six productive moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the six valid moods of the third figure, explain why one premise must be universal, and verify that all conclusions are particular.

Core Idea
Third-figure syllogisms yield only particular conclusions because the middle term is the subject in both premises.

In the third figure of the categorical syllogism, the middle term serves as the subject in both premises. This unique arrangement imposes a key condition: at least one premise must be universal. The author explains the rationale and then enumerates exactly six productive moods—three yielding affirmative conclusions and three yielding negative ones. All these moods share the feature that they produce only particular conclusions, never universal.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the structure of the third figure
The middle term is the subject in both the major and minor premises. Note that the extremes (major and minor terms) are predicates in their respective premises.
This structure is unique; in the first figure the middle term is subject in minor and predicate in major.
2
Identify the necessary condition
One premise must be universal. The author argues that if both were particular, the middle term might refer to different subsets, making inference impossible.
The condition is 'one of them universal' (كلية إحداهما).
3
Understand the example of the condition
Examine the counterexample: 'some animal is human' and 'some animal is horse' do not yield 'some human is horse'. This illustrates why the condition is necessary.
Both premises are particular affirmative; no necessary conclusion follows.
4
Enumerate the six moods systematically
List the three affirmative moods: (a) both universal affirmative, (b) particular affirmative minor + universal affirmative major, (c) universal affirmative minor + particular affirmative major. Then the three negative moods: (d) universal affirmative minor + universal negative major, (e) particular affirmative minor + universal negative major.
All moods produce only particular conclusions (جزئية).
5
Apply the pattern to an Islamic example
Use a real example from Islamic law or theology to confirm that the conclusion is particular. For instance, 'every khamr is an intoxicant' and 'every khamr is forbidden' yields 'some intoxicant is forbidden'.
This is a valid third-figure mood (Darapti).

شرائط الشكل الثالث كلية إحداهما؛ لينتج الموجبتان مع الموجبة الكلية أو بالعكس موجبة جزئية، ومع السالبة لم يتعد الحكم من الأوسط بالفعل إلى الأصغر. قوله مع كلية إحداهما: لأنه لو كانت المقدمتان جزئيتين الجاز أن يكون البعض من الأوسط المحكوم عليه بالأصغر غير البعض المحكوم عليه بالأكبر، فلا يلزم تعدية الحكم من الأكبر إلى الأصغر، مثلا يصدق بعض الحيوان إنسان وبعض الحيوان فرس ولا يصدق بعض الإنسان فرس. قوله لينتج الموجبتان الضروب المنتجة في هذا الشكل بحسب الشرائط المذكورة ستة حاصلة من ضم الصغرى الموجبة الكلية إلى الكبريات الأربع وضم صغرى الموجبة الجزئية إلى الكبريين الكليتين الموجبة والسالبة وهذه الضروب كلها مشتركة في أنها لا تنتج إلا جزئية لكن ثلاثة منها ينتج الإيجاب وثلاثة منها ينتج السلب. أما المنتجة للإيجاب فأولها المركب من موجبتين كليتين نحو كل ج ب وكل ج آ فبعض ب آ. ثانيهما: المركب من موجبة جزئية صغرى وموجبة كلية كبرى، وإلى هذين أشار المصنف بقوله: "لينتج الموجبتان" أي الصغرى مع الموجبة الكلية أي الكبرى والثالث عكس الثاني أعني المركب من موجبة كلية صغرى وموجبة جزئية كبرى وإليه أشار بقوله: "أو بالعكس فليس المراد من العكس عكس الضربين المذكورين؛ إذ ليس عكس الأول الأول. فتأمل. وأما المنتجة للسلب فأولها: المركب من موجبة كلية وسالبة كلية والثاني من موجبة جزئية وسالبة كلية وإليهما أشار بقوله: "ومع السالبة الكلية" أي لينتج الموجبتان السالبة الكلية. لا ينتج إلا جزئية: يعني أن النتيجة الكلية غير لازمة في بعض المواد والنتيجة لابد وأن تكون لازمة، فعلم أن النتيجة اللازمة لها إنما هي الجزئية لا غير. أما المنتجة للإيجاب إلخ إنما قدم الموجبات على السوالب؛ لكون الإيجاب أشرف من السلب. (إسماعيل) فأولها المركب إلخ نحو كل إنسان حيوان وكل إنسان ناطق ينتج بعض الحيوان ناطق، ومن ههنا تبين أن النتيجة الصادقة إنما هي جزئية لا كلية وإن كان مقتضى المقدمتين الكليتين أن لا يكون النتيجة إلا كلية. فتأمل: لعله إشارة إلى أن عبارة المصنف ههنا مشتملة على الركاكة كما لا يخفى على من له أدنى فهم في العبارات العربية والفنون الأدبية إلا أن المصنف اختارها؛ لأنه بصدد الاختصار؛ فإنه لو قال أو الموجبة الكلية مع الموجبة الجزئية لطال الكلام، فكأنه رجح الركاكة على الطول ولو بالعكس في الفصاحة والقبول. فافهم. (إسماعيل) فتأمل لغموضه، ويمكن أن يكون إشارة إلى أن كلام المصنف يحلله يوهم خلاف الواقع فكان الواجب إتيان ما لايوهمه.
Conditions of the third figure: one of them [the premises] must be universal, so that the two affirmative [moods] with the universal affirmative [major premise] or conversely produce a particular affirmative, and with the negative [major premise] the judgment does not transfer from the middle [term] in actuality to the minor [term]. His saying 'with one of them universal': because if both premises were particular, it would be possible that some of the middle [term] judged upon by the minor is not the same as that judged upon by the major, so transference of the judgment from the major to the minor is not necessitated. For example, 'some animal is human' and 'some animal is horse' are true, but 'some human is horse' is not true. His saying 'so that the two affirmative produce': the productive moods in this figure according to the mentioned conditions are six, resulting from joining the affirmative universal minor to the four majors, and joining the affirmative particular minor to the two universal majors, affirmative and negative. And all these moods share that they produce only a particular, but three of them produce affirmation and three produce negation. As for those producing affirmation: the first is composed of two universal affirmatives, e.g., 'every J is B' and 'every J is A', so 'some B is A'. The second: composed of a particular affirmative minor and a universal affirmative major, and to these two the author pointed with his saying 'so that the two affirmative produce' i.e., the minor with the universal affirmative major. The third is the converse of the second, i.e., composed of a universal affirmative minor and a particular affirmative major, and to it he pointed with his saying 'or conversely' — but the intended meaning is not the conversion of the two mentioned moods; for it is not the converse of the first. So reflect. As for those producing negation: the first: composed of a universal affirmative and a universal negative; the second: of a particular affirmative and a universal negative, and to them he pointed with his saying 'and with the universal negative', i.e., so that the two affirmative produce the universal negative? It produces only a particular: meaning that the universal conclusion is not necessary in some materials, and the conclusion must be necessary, so it is known that the necessary conclusion for it is only the particular. As for those producing affirmation etc., he only presented the affirmatives before the negatives because affirmation is more honorable than negation. (Isma'il) So the first composed etc., e.g., 'every human is animal' and 'every human is rational' produces 'some animal is rational'. From here it is clear that the true conclusion is only particular, not universal, even though the two universal premises require that the conclusion be nothing but universal. So reflect: perhaps it is an indication that the author's phrasing here contains awkwardness, as is clear to anyone with minimal understanding of Arabic expressions and literary arts; but the author chose it because he was aiming for brevity; for if he had said 'or the universal affirmative with the particular affirmative' the speech would have been long, so it is as if he preferred awkwardness over length, even if the opposite is better in eloquence and acceptability. So understand. (Isma'il) So reflect due to its obscurity, and it could be an indication that the author's speech when analyzed suggests something contrary to reality, so it was obligatory to bring what does not suggest that.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage explains the conditions for valid syllogisms in the third figure and enumerates its six productive moods. The author begins by stating the key condition: one premise must be universal. This is because if both premises were particular, the middle term might refer to different subsets in each premise, preventing the transference of the judgment. The example given is 'some animal is human' and 'some animal is horse' — they do not entail 'some human is horse'. The six valid moods are derived by combining an affirmative universal minor with any of the four majors, or an affirmative particular minor with the two universal majors (affirmative and negative). All conclusions in the third figure are particular. Three moods yield affirmative conclusions: (1) both premises universal affirmative (e.g., every J is B, every J is A → some B is A), (2) particular affirmative minor + universal affirmative major, (3) universal affirmative minor + particular affirmative major. The latter two are referred to by the author's phrases 'so that the two affirmative produce' and 'or conversely', though 'conversely' here does not mean conversion of the first mood. Three moods yield negative conclusions: (1) universal affirmative minor + universal negative major, (2) particular affirmative minor + universal negative major. The commentary then clarifies that the conclusion can only be particular, even if the premises are universal, because the middle term is the subject in both premises, making it impossible to infer a universal relation between the extremes. The passage ends with a marginal note discussing the author's stylistic choice to use slightly awkward phrasing for brevity.

Checkpoint
Why does the third figure require at least one universal premise?
A) Because the middle term is the predicate in both premises.
B) Because otherwise the middle term might refer to different subsets, preventing inference.
C) Because universal premises are always true.
D) Because particular premises cannot produce any conclusion.

Key Takeaway
In the third figure, the necessity of one universal premise prevents ambiguous middles, and all valid conclusions are particular.

The Syllogism
intermediate
15 min
The Third Figure: Its Six Moods and Proofs
Author enumerates the six valid moods of the third figure and describes three methods of reduction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the six valid moods of the third figure and apply the three methods of reduction to prove them.

Core Idea
The third figure yields only particular conclusions, and its moods are proven by reduction to the first figure.

The third figure of the categorical syllogism is distinct: its minor must be affirmative, and all its conclusions are particular. Mastering its six moods and the three reduction methods (khalf, aks al-sughra, aks al-kubra) is essential for evaluating arguments in usul al-fiqh and kalam, where such patterns often appear.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the conditions of the third figure
The minor premise must be affirmative particular, and the premises combine with two universals. All moods yield only a particular conclusion, never a universal one. Identify why a universal conclusion is impossible.
The condition is stated at the beginning of the passage.
2
List the six moods
Three moods produce affirmation: (1) two universal affirmatives, (2) particular affirmative minor + universal affirmative major, (3) universal affirmative minor + particular affirmative major. Three produce negation: (1) universal affirmative + universal negative, (2) particular affirmative + universal negative, (3) universal affirmative + particular negative.
The author uses terse phrases like 'let the two affirmatives produce'.
3
Understand reduction by contradiction (khalf)
Take the contradictory of the conclusion (which is universal because the conclusion is particular) and make it the major premise. With the original minor premise, derive from the first figure a conclusion that contradicts the original major premise.
In the third figure, the contradictory is made major, unlike the second figure where it is made minor.
4
Understand reduction by conversion of the minor (aks al-sughra)
When the major is universal, convert the minor premise (which is affirmative) to return to the first figure. The converted minor becomes the minor of a first-figure syllogism with the original major, giving the desired conclusion.
This works for moods where the major is universal.
5
Understand reduction by conversion of the major (aks al-kubra)
When the major is affirmative, convert it to become the minor of a first-figure syllogism. Then convert the resulting conclusion to obtain the original desired conclusion. This effectively passes through a fourth figure.
The commentator notes that this method applies when the major is affirmative.
