شرائط الشكل الثالث أنها لا صغرى الموجبة الجزئية إلى الكبريين الكليتين الموجبة والسالبة وهذه الضروب كلها مشتركة في ينتج إلا جزئية لكن ثلاثة منها ينتج الإيجاب وثلاثة منها ينتج السلب. أما المنتجة للإيجاب فأولها المركب من موجبتين كليتين نحو كل ج ب وكل ج آ فبعض ب آ. ثانيهما: المركب من موجبة جزئية صغرى وموجبة كلية كبرى، وإلى هذين أشار المصنف بقوله: "لينتج الموجبتان" أي الصغرى مع الموجبة الكلية أي الكبرى والثالث عكس الثاني أعني المركب من موجبة كلية صغرى وموجبة جزئية كبرى وإليه أشار بقوله: "أو "بالعكس فليس المراد من العكس عكس الضربين المذكورين؛ إذ ليس عكس الأول الأول. فتأمل. وأما المنتجة للسلب فأولها: المركب من موجبة كلية وسالبة كلية والثاني من موجبة جزئية وسالبة كلية وإليهما أشار بقوله: "ومع السالبة الكلية" أي لينتج الموجبتان السالبة الكلية. واعلم أن في هذا الجدول ترتيبا موضوعا للضروب الباقية اختير في الكتب المطولة المعتبرة لكنه مخالف لمختار المصنف لأنه قدم الضروب المنتجة للإيجاب على الضروب المنتجة للسلب للاختصار والشارح اقتفى إثره؛ ليسهل شرح الكتاب كما لا يخفى على أولي الألباب. لا ينتج إلا جزئية: يعني جزئية: يعني أن النتيجة الكلية غير لازمة في بعض المواد والنتيجة لابد وأن تكون لازمة، فعلم أن النتيجة اللازمة لها إنما هي الجزئية لا غير. أما المنتجة للإيجاب إلخ إنما قدم الموجبات على السوالب؛ لكون الإيجاب أشرف من السلب. (إسماعيل) فأولها المركب إلخ نحو كل إنسان حيوان وكل إنسان ناطق ينتج بعض الحيوان ناطق، ومن ههنا تبين أن النتيجة الصادقة إنما هي جزئية لا كلية وإن كان مقتضى المقدمتين الكليتين أن لا يكون النتيجة إلا كلية. فتأمل: لعله إشارة إلى أن عبارة المصنف ههنا مشتملة على الركاكة كما لا يخفى على من له أدنى فهم في العبارات العربية والفنون الأدبية إلا أن المصنف اختارها؛ لأنه بصدد الاختصار؛ فإنه لو قال أو الموجبة الكلية مع الموجبة الجزئية لطال الكلام، فكأنه رجح الركاكة على الطول ولو بالعكس في الفصاحة والقبول. فافهم. (إسماعيل) فتأمل لغموضه، ويمكن أن يكون إشارة إلى أن كلام المصنف يحلله يوهم خلاف الواقع فكان الواجب إتيان ما لايوهمه. والثالث: من موجبة كلية وسالبة جزئية كما قال: "أو الكلية مع الجزئية" أي الموجبة الكلية مع السالبة الجزئية قوله بالخلف يعني بيان إنتاج هذه الضروب لهذه النتائج إما بالخلف: وهو ههنا أن يؤخذ نقيض النتيجة ويجعل لكليته كبرى وصغرى القياس لإيجابها صغرى؛ لينتج من الشكل الأول ما ينافي الكبرى، وهذا يجري في الضروب كلها. وأما بعكس الصغرى ليرجع إلى الشكل الأول، وذلك حيث تكون الكبرى كلية كما في الضروب الأول والثاني والرابع والخامس، وأما بعكس الكبرى ليصير شكلا رابعا ثم عكس الترتيب؛ ليرتد شكلا أولا وينتج نتيجة ثم تعكس هذه النتيجة؛ فإنه المطلوب، وذلك حيث تكون الكبرى موجبة؛ ليصلح عكسها صغرى للشكل الأول وهو ههنا أي في الشكل الثالث وإنما قال ههنا؛ لأن الخلف ههنا غير ما ذكر هناك أي في الشكل الثاني؛ لأن نقيض النتيجة يجعل ههنا كبرى وهناك صغرى ما ينافي الكبرى إلخ مثل كل إنسان حيوان، وكل إنسان ناطق ينتج: بعض الحيوان ناطق وإلا لصدق نقيضه، وهو لا شيء من الحيوان بناطق، ويجعل هذا النقيض كبرى فيقال: كل إنسان حيوان ولا شيء من الحيوان بناطق ينتج: لا شيء من الإنسان بناطق، وهو مناف لكبرى الشكل الثالث، وهي كل إنسان ناطق وهو مسلم ألبتة دائما. قال: ينافي لا يناقض؛ لأن نقيض كل إنسان ناطق إنما هو السالبة الجزئية، أعني بعض الإنسان ليس بناطق لا سالبة كلية. وهذا يجري إلخ: فإن نتائج هذه الضروب ليس إلا جزئية موجبة أو سالبة، فنقائضها تكون كلية ألبتة فهو صالح لأن يجعل كبرى الشكل الأول، وصغريات هذه الضروب كلها أيضاً موجبات؛ لما علمت أن الشكل الثالث أيضاً من شرائطها إيجاب الصغرى فهي تصلح لأن تقع صغرى الشكل الأول. (إسماعيل) ليرجع أي الشكل الثالث إلى الشكل الأول والشكل الثالث يخالف للشكل الأول في الصغرى ويوافق له في الكبرى فيعكس الصغرى ويكون راجعاً إلى الشكل الأول بالضرورة، مثل كل إنسان حيوان، وكل إنسان ناطق ينتج بعض الحيوان ناطق؛ لأنه إذا عكس الصغرى ويقال بعض الحيوان إنسان، وكل إنسان ناطق ينتج من الشكل الأول تلك النتيجة المطلوبة. وأما بعكس الكبرى إلخ تصويره كل إنسان حيوان، وكل إنسان ناطق ينتج بعض الحيوان ناطق؛ لأنه لو عكس الكبرى إلى بعض الناطق إنسان، وكل إنسان حيوان، ينتج: بعض الناطق حيوان وينعكس إلى بعض الحيوان ناطق، وهذا هو المطلوب من الضرب الأول من الشكل الثالث.
Conditions of the third figure: that its minor is affirmative particular [and it combines] with the two universal premises, affirmative and negative. And these moods all share that they produce only a particular, but three of them produce affirmation and three produce negation. As for those producing affirmation: the first is composed of two universal affirmatives, e.g., 'Every B is C and every A is C, so some B is A.' The second is composed of an affirmative particular minor and an affirmative universal major. And to these two the author referred by his saying: 'Let the two affirmatives produce,' meaning the minor with the universal affirmative major. And the third is the converse of the second, i.e., composed of an affirmative universal minor and an affirmative particular major. And to it he referred by his saying: 'or by conversion,' but what is meant by conversion is not the conversion of the two mentioned moods, for it is not the converse of the first. So reflect. As for those producing negation: the first is composed of a universal affirmative and a universal negative; the second of a particular affirmative and a universal negative. And to these two he referred by his saying: 'And with the universal negative,' meaning let the two affirmatives produce with the universal negative. And know that in this table there is an arrangement of the remaining moods chosen in the reliable detailed books, but it differs from the author's choice because he placed the moods producing affirmation before those producing negation for brevity, and the commentator followed his trail to facilitate the explanation of the book, as is not hidden from those of understanding. 'It produces only a particular' means that a universal conclusion is not necessary in some matters, and the conclusion must be necessary, so it is known that the necessary result is only the particular, not otherwise. 'As for those producing affirmation, etc.' He indeed placed affirmatives before negatives because affirmation is nobler than negation. (Isma'il) 'So the first is composed, etc.' for example: 'Every human is an animal and every human is rational' produces 'Some animal is rational.' From here it is clear that the true conclusion is only particular, not universal, even if the two universal premises demand that the conclusion should be universal. So reflect: perhaps it is an indication that the author's expression here contains awkwardness, as is not hidden from one who has the least understanding of Arabic expressions and literary arts, but the author chose it because he aimed at brevity; for if he had said 'or the universal affirmative with the particular affirmative,' the speech would have been long, so he preferred awkwardness over length, even if the opposite is more eloquent and acceptable. So understand. (Isma'il) 'So reflect' because of its obscurity, and it could be an indication that the author's speech, if analyzed, may suggest something contrary to reality, so it was necessary to produce something that does not suggest that. And the third: from a universal affirmative and a particular negative, as he said: 'or the universal with the particular,' i.e., the universal affirmative with the particular negative. His saying 'by reduction' means the demonstration of the production of these moods for these results is either by reduction (khalf): which here is that the contradictory of the conclusion is taken and, because of its universality, made the major of the syllogism, and the minor of the syllogism is made the minor of the original inference, so that from the first figure something contradictory to the major is produced. And this applies in all moods. And as for conversion of the minor to return to the first figure, that is when the major is universal, as in the first, second, fourth, and fifth moods. And as for conversion of the major to become a fourth figure, then conversion of order to revert to the first figure, producing a conclusion, then that conclusion is converted; for that is what is sought. That is when the major is affirmative, so that its conversion can serve as minor for the first figure. And it is here, i.e., in the third figure. He only said 'here' because the reduction here is different from what was mentioned there, i.e., in the second figure; because the contradictory of the conclusion is made major here and minor there. 'What contradicts the major, etc.' Example: 'Every human is an animal and every human is rational' produces 'Some animal is rational'; otherwise its contradictory would be true, which is 'No animal is rational'. And this contradictory is made major, so it is said: 'Every human is an animal and no animal is rational' produces 'No human is rational', which contradicts the major of the third figure, i.e., 'Every human is rational', which is absolutely conceded always. He said 'contradicts', not 'contradicts as contradictory', because the contradictory of 'Every human is rational' is only the particular negative, i.e., 'Some human is not rational', not the universal negative. 'And this applies, etc.': For the conclusions of these moods are only particular affirmative or negative, so their contradictories are necessarily universal, so they are fit to be made major of the first figure, and the minors of all these moods are also affirmative; because you know that the third figure also has as one of its conditions the affirmation of the minor, so they are fit to be placed as minor of the first figure. (Isma'il) 'To return', i.e., the third figure returns to the first figure. The third figure differs from the first in the minor and agrees with it in the major, so the minor is converted and it becomes ( ) back to the first figure necessarily. Example: 'Every human is an animal and every human is rational' produces 'Some animal is rational'; because if the minor is converted and it is said 'Some animal is human' and 'Every human is rational', from the first figure that desired result is produced. 'And as for conversion of the major, etc.' Its illustration: 'Every human is an animal and every human is rational' produces 'Some animal is rational'; because if the major is converted to 'Some rational is human' and 'Every human is animal', it produces 'Some rational is animal', and this is converted to 'Some animal is rational', and this is what is desired from the first mood of the third figure.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the conditions and moods of the third figure of the categorical syllogism. It opens by restating the conditions: the minor premise must be affirmative particular, and it combines with two universal premises (one affirmative, one negative). All moods in this figure share the property that they produce only a particular conclusion. Among the six valid moods, three yield affirmative conclusions and three yield negative ones. The author first lists the three affirmative moods: (1) both premises universal affirmative — e.g., 'Every B is C and every A is C, therefore some B is A'; (2) minor particular affirmative, major universal affirmative; (3) the converse of the second, i.e., minor universal affirmative, major particular affirmative. The commentator notes that the author's terse expression 'let the two affirmatives produce' refers to the first two, and his 'or by conversion' refers to the third. He warns that 'conversion' here does not mean the simple conversion of the two moods, so one must reflect. Next, the negative moods: (1) universal affirmative and universal negative; (2) particular affirmative and universal negative. The author's phrase 'and with the universal negative' indicates these. The commentator then explains that the arrangement in the table differs from the author's own choice (the author placed affirmative moods first for brevity), and the commentator follows the author's arrangement for ease of explanation. He also clarifies that 'it produces only a particular' means that a universal conclusion is not always necessary, and the necessary conclusion is only particular. The commentator adds that the author's preference for placing affirmatives before negatives is because affirmation is nobler than negation. He then provides an example using human, animal, and rational: 'Every human is an animal and every human is rational' yields 'Some animal is rational'. He remarks that although the premises are both universal, the conclusion is only particular. He then discusses the author's stylistic choice of brevity over eloquence. The passage then moves to methods of proving these moods, i.e., reducing them to the first figure. Three methods are described: (a) reduction by contradiction (khalf): take the contradictory of the conclusion (which is universal because the conclusion is particular), make it the major premise of a new syllogism with the original minor, and from the first figure produce a contradiction to the original major premise. An example is given: if we assume 'No animal is rational' (contradictory of 'Some animal is rational'), with 'Every human is animal', we deduce 'No human is rational', which contradicts the original major 'Every human is rational'. The commentator clarifies that 'contradicts' here does not mean strict logical contradiction because the contradictory of a universal affirmative is a particular negative, not a universal negative. (b) Conversion of the minor: when the major is universal, convert the minor premise to revert to the first figure. Example: 'Every human is animal' converted to 'Some animal is human', then with 'Every human is rational' we get 'Some animal is rational'. (c) Conversion of the major and then conversion of the result: when the major is affirmative, convert it to become the minor of a first-figure syllogism, then conclude and convert the conclusion to get the desired result. Example: 'Every human is rational' converted to 'Some rational is human', with 'Every human is animal' gives 'Some rational is animal', then converted to 'Some animal is rational'. The commentator notes that this method applies when the major is affirmative, and the conversion of the major effectively creates a fourth figure which then reverts to the first.

Key Terms
شكل ثالث
ضرب
خلف
عكس الصغرى
عكس الكبرى

Checkpoint
In the third figure, which reduction method is used when the major premise is universal?
A) Reduction by contradiction (khalf)
B) Conversion of the minor premise
C) Conversion of the major premise then conversion of the conclusion
D) No reduction is needed; the syllogism is already in the first figure

Key Takeaway
The third figure's six moods all yield particular conclusions, and their validity is proven by reduction to the first figure using khalf, aks al-sughra, or aks al-kubra depending on the premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a valid third-figure syllogism on a fiqh or kalam topic, identifying which of the six moods it belongs to. For example, using 'كل قاتل عمد حرام' and 'كل قاتل عمد عدوان' to deduce 'بعض المحرمات عدوان'.
?
Take the syllogism you constructed and apply all three reduction methods (khalf, aks al-sughra, aks al-kubra) to show it is valid. Write out each step explicitly.

The Syllogism

Proving Conclusions in the Third Figure Using Reductio (Khalf)
Author explains the khalf method in the third figure with an example and its applicability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the khalf method to derive the conclusion of a third-figure categorical syllogism and verify its validity by constructing a first-figure reductio.

Core Idea
In the third figure, khalf takes the contradictory of the desired conclusion as the major premise.

The third figure of the categorical syllogism has the middle term as subject of both premises. One way to prove its conclusion is the khalf (reductio ad absurdum) method. Unlike the second figure, here the contradictory of the conclusion is taken as the major premise, not the minor. This lesson walks through the process with an example and explains why it works, highlighting the logical precision required.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original third-figure syllogism
Locate the two premises and the desired conclusion. In the example: 'Every human is animal' (minor), 'Every human is rational' (major), conclusion 'Some animal is rational'.
The minor must be affirmative, major universal affirmative or particular negative.
2
State the contradictory of the conclusion
The conclusion 'Some animal is rational' is a particular affirmative. Its contradictory is the universal negative 'No animal is rational'.
The contradictory of a particular affirmative is a universal negative, and vice versa.
3
Construct a new first-figure syllogism with the contradictory as major
Take the original minor 'Every human is animal' as the minor premise, and the contradictory 'No animal is rational' as the major premise. This gives a first-figure syllogism: Minor: Every human is animal, Major: No animal is rational.
The middle term here is 'animal'.
4
Derive the conclusion of the new syllogism
From 'Every human is animal' and 'No animal is rational', we conclude 'No human is rational' (Celarent mood). This conclusion contradicts the original major premise 'Every human is rational'.
If the original major is true, this contradiction shows that our assumption (that the conclusion is false) is impossible.
5
Confirm the original conclusion follows
Since assuming the opposite leads to a contradiction, the desired conclusion must be true. The reductio is complete.
The text notes that this works for all moods of the third figure.

بالخلف أو عكس الصغرى أو الكبرى ثم الترتيب ثم النتيجة
•
والثالث: من موجبة كلية وسالبة جزئية كما قال: "أو الكلية مع الجزئية" أي الموجبة الكلية مع
السالبة الجزئية قوله بالخلف يعني بيان إنتاج هذه الضروب لهذه النتائج إما بالخلف: وهو ههنا أن يؤخذ نقيض النتيجة ويجعل لكليته كبرى وصغرى القياس لإيجابها صغرى؛ لينتج من الشكل الأول ما ينافي الكبرى، وهذا يجري في الضروب كلها. وأما بعكس الصغرى ليرجع إلى الشكل الأول، وذلك حيث تكون الكبرى كلية كما في الضروب الأول والثاني والرابع والخامس، وأما بعكس الكبرى ليصير شكلا رابعا ثم عكس الترتيب؛ ليرتد شكلا أولا وينتج نتيجة ثم تعكس هذه النتيجة؛ فإنه المطلوب، وذلك حيث تكون الكبرى موجبة؛ ليصلح عكسها صغرى للشكل الأول
وهو ههنا أي في الشكل الثالث وإنما قال ههنا؛ لأن الخلف ههنا غير ما ذكر هناك أي في الشكل الثاني؛ لأن نقيض النتيجة يجعل ههنا كبرى وهناك صغرى ما ينافي الكبرى إلخ مثل كل إنسان حيوان، وكل إنسان ناطق ينتج: بعض الحيوان ناطق وإلا لصدق نقيضه، وهو لا شيء من الحيوان بناطق، ويجعل هذا النقيض كبرى فيقال: كل إنسان حيوان ولا شيء من الحيوان بناطق ينتج: لا شيء من الإنسان بناطق، وهو مناف لكبرى الشكل الثالث، وهي كل إنسان ناطق وهو مسلم ألبتة دائما. قال: ينافي لا يناقض؛ لأن نقيض كل إنسان ناطق إنما هو السالبة الجزئية، أعني بعض الإنسان ليس بناطق لا سالبة كلية.
وهذا يجري إلخ: فإن نتائج هذه الضروب ليس إلا جزئية موجبة أو سالبة، فنقائضها تكون كلية ألبتة فهو صالح لأن يجعل كبرى الشكل الأول، وصغريات هذه الضروب كلها أيضاً موجبات؛ لما علمت أن الشكل الثالث أيضاً من شرائطها إيجاب الصغرى فهي تصلح لأن تقع صغرى الشكل الأول. (إسماعيل) ليرجع أي الشكل الثالث إلى الشكل الأول والشكل الثالث يخالف للشكل الأول في الصغرى ويوافق له في الكبرى فيعكس الصغرى ويكون راجعاً إلى الشكل الأول بالضرورة، مثل كل إنسان حيوان، وكل إنسان ناطق ينتج بعض الحيوان ناطق؛ لأنه إذا عكس الصغرى ويقال بعض الحيوان إنسان، وكل إنسان ناطق ينتج من الشكل الأول تلك النتيجة المطلوبة. وأما بعكس الكبرى إلخ تصويره كل إنسان حيوان، وكل إنسان ناطق ينتج بعض الحيوان ناطق؛ لأنه لو عكس الكبرى إلى بعض الناطق إنسان، وكل إنسان حيوان، ينتج: بعض الناطق حيوان وينعكس إلى بعض الحيوان ناطق، وهذا هو المطلوب من الضرب الأول من الشكل الثالث.
Conditions of the third figure: By reductio ad absurdum (khalf) or conversion (aks) of the minor premise or the major premise, then rearrangement, then the conclusion. And the third: from a universal affirmative and a particular negative, as he said: 'or the universal with the particular' meaning the universal affirmative with the particular negative. His statement 'by khalf' means the explanation of the production of these moods for these results either by khalf: and it is here that the contradictory of the conclusion is taken and made, due to its universality, the major premise and the minor premise of the syllogism because of its affirmative quality as minor; so that from the first figure something is produced that contradicts the major premise, and this applies to all moods. And as for by conversion of the minor to return to the first figure, and that is when the major is universal, as in the first, second, fourth, and fifth moods. And as for by conversion of the major to become a fourth figure, then reversal of order so that it returns to being a first figure and produces a result, then that result is converted; for that is what is desired, and that is when the major is affirmative, so that its conversion is suitable as a minor for the first figure. And it is here, i.e., in the third figure; and he only said 'here' because the khalf here is different from what was mentioned there, i.e., in the second figure; because the contradictory of the conclusion is made here the major and there the minor. What contradicts the major etc., for example: 'Every human is an animal, and every human is rational' produces 'Some animal is rational'; otherwise its contradictory would be true, which is 'No animal is rational', and this contradictory is made the major, and it is said: 'Every human is an animal, and no animal is rational' produces 'No human is rational', which is contradictory to the major of the third figure, which is 'Every human is rational' and it is absolutely always conceded. He said: it contradicts, not opposes; because the contradictory of 'Every human is rational' is only the particular negative, namely 'Some human is not rational', not the universal negative. And 'this applies' etc.: for the results of these moods are only particular affirmative or negative, so their contradictories are always universal, so it is suitable to be made the major of the first figure, and the minors of all these moods are also affirmative; because you know that the third figure also has as its conditions the affirmation of the minor, so they are suitable to occur as minors of the first figure. (Isma'il) 'to return' i.e., the third figure to the first figure; and the third figure differs from the first in the minor and agrees with it in the major, so the minor is converted and it becomes returning to the first figure necessarily, for example: 'Every human is an animal, and every human is rational' produces 'Some animal is rational'; because if the minor is converted and it is said 'Some animal is a human', and 'Every human is rational' produces from the first figure that desired result. And as for by conversion of the major etc., its illustration: 'Every human is an animal, and every human is rational' produces 'Some animal is rational'; because if the major is converted to 'Some rational is a human', and 'Every human is an animal', it produces 'Some rational is an animal' and it is converted to 'Some animal is rational', and this is what is desired from the first mood of the third figure.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the conditions of the third figure of the categorical syllogism. It explains three methods by which the moods of this figure yield their conclusions: reductio ad absurdum (khalf), conversion of the minor premise, and conversion of the major premise followed by reordering. The commentary begins by stating the condition for the third figure: the minor must be affirmative and the major universal affirmative, or the minor universal affirmative and the major particular negative. Then it details each method. First, khalf: the contradictory of the desired conclusion is taken as the major premise in a first-figure syllogism, producing a contradiction of the original major premise. An example is given: to prove 'Some animal is rational' from 'Every human is animal' and 'Every human is rational', we assume the opposite 'No animal is rational', combine it with 'Every human is animal' to get 'No human is rational', which contradicts the original premise 'Every human is rational'. The text clarifies that this is a contradiction (munafat) not a direct negation (naqd), because the contradictory of a universal affirmative is a particular negative. Second, conversion of the minor: since the third figure has a universal major and affirmative minor, converting the minor (e.g., 'Every human is animal' becomes 'Some animal is human') allows the syllogism to be reduced to the first figure. Third, conversion of the major: if the major is affirmative, it can be converted to become the minor of a first-figure syllogism after reordering. The commentary illustrates with the example of the first mood of the third figure. Throughout, the author (Isma'il) uses logical precision and Islamic examples, such as 'human', 'animal', and 'rational'.

Reducing the Third Figure by Converting Premises (Aks al-Sughra and Aks al-Kubra)
Author explains two conversion methods to reduce the third figure to the first.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reduce a third-figure categorical syllogism to the first figure by converting the minor or major premise and derive the conclusion.

Core Idea
Converting the minor premise (when major universal) or the major premise (when affirmative) reduces the third figure to the first.

Besides reducio, the third figure can be reduced to the first figure through conversion of premises. If the major is universal, converting the minor premise yields a first-figure syllogism directly. If the major is affirmative, converting it and reordering produces a fourth-figure syllogism, which after further conversion yields the desired conclusion. This lesson illustrates both methods with examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original third-figure syllogism
In the example: minor 'Every human is animal', major 'Every human is rational', conclusion 'Some animal is rational'. The major is universal and affirmative, so both conversion methods are applicable.
2
Method 1: Aks al-sughra (convert minor)
Convert the minor 'Every human is animal' to 'Some animal is human'. Form a first-figure syllogism: minor = converted minor ('Some animal is human'), major = original major ('Every human is rational'). Derive conclusion 'Some animal is rational' (Darii). This directly matches the desired conclusion.
No further conversion needed.
3
Method 2: Aks al-kubra (convert major) — part 1
Convert the major 'Every human is rational' to 'Some rational is human'. Form a fourth-figure syllogism by keeping the original minor as the minor and placing the converted major as the major: minor = 'Every human is animal', major = 'Some rational is human'.
Middle term 'human' is subject of minor and predicate of major — fourth figure.
4
Method 2: Aks al-kubra — part 2: reverse order
Swap the premises to obtain a first-figure syllogism: now minor = 'Some rational is human', major = 'Every human is animal'.
Middle term 'human' is now predicate of minor and subject of major — first figure.
5
Method 2: Aks al-kubra — part 3: derive intermediate conclusion
From the first figure, derive 'Some rational is animal' (Darii).

Source Text

Why the Fourth Figure Is Problematic: Its Conditions and Authority Rejection
Author states the conditions for the fourth figure and explains why modal conditions are omitted.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a fourth-figure syllogism satisfies the productivity conditions and explain why modal conditions were omitted.

Core Idea
The fourth figure is so unnatural that many logicians exclude it entirely.

The fourth figure of the categorical syllogism is the most controversial: it has strict conditions for productivity, and even then, its conclusions are often unstable. This passage from the Tahzeeb Sharh explains the two alternative conditions required and then reveals why the author chose not to discuss modal conditions for this figure—because it is so far from natural intuition that leading authorities like al-Farabi and Ibn Sina dropped it from science.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two alternative conditions
The passage states that for the fourth figure to produce a necessary conclusion, either both premises must be affirmative with the minor universal, or the premises must differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) with at least one universal.
2
Understand the three problematic cases
If neither condition holds, three scenarios arise: both premises negative; both affirmative with minor particular; or two particular premises differing in quality. In each, the syllogism is sterile ('aqim) because it yields conflicting results.
3
Trace the examples of sterility
The text gives concrete examples: e.g., 'No stone is human and no rational is stone' leads to an affirmative conclusion, but swapping to 'No horse is stone' gives negative—showing inconsistency. Follow each example to see why the figure fails.
4
Grasp the reason for omitting modal conditions
The author skipped modal conditions because the fourth figure is so distant from natural reasoning that authorities like al-Farabi and Ibn Sina excluded it from the sciences and demonstration. Some even removed it from the division of figures.

وتكون الصغرى كلية؛ لتصلح كبرى له كما في الضرب الأول والثالث لا غير. قوله: وفي الرابع: أي يشترط في إنتاج الشكل الرابع بحسب الكم والكيف أحد الأمرين: إما إيجاب المقدمتين مع كلية الصغرى، وإما اختلاف المقدمتين في الكيف مع كلية إحداهما؛ وذلك لأنه لولا أحدهما لزم إما كون المقدمتين سالبتين أو موجبتين مع كون الصغرى جزئية أو جزئيتين مختلفتين في الكيف، وعلى التقادير الثلاثة يحصل الاختلاف، وهو دليل العقم. أما على الأول؛ فلأن الحق في قولنا: لا شيء من الحجر بإنسان ولا شيء من الناطق بحجر هو الإيجاب، ولو قلنا: لا شيء من الفرس بحجر كان الحق السلب. وأما على الثاني: فلأنا إذا قلنا بعض الحيوان إنسان، وكل ناطق حيوان كان الحق الإيجاب، ولو قلنا كل فرس حيوان كان الحق السلب. وأما على الثالث: فلأن الحق في قولنا بعض الحيوان إنسان وبعض الجسم ليس بحيوان هو الإيجاب، ولو قلنا: بعض الحجر ليس بحيوان كان الحق السلب. ثم إن المصنف لم يتعرض لبيان شرائط الرابع بحسب الجهة؛ لقلة الاعتداء بهذا الشكل لكمال بعده عن الطبع حتى أسقطه الشيخان عن الاعتبار في العلوم والحجة، بل أخرجه البعض عن التقسيم أيضاً.
And in the fourth: their affirmation with the universality of the minor, and their difference with the universality of one of them. Conditions of the fourth figure: And the minor is universal, so that it may serve as a major for it (the fourth figure), as in the first and third moods only. His statement: 'and in the fourth': i.e., it is required for the production of the fourth figure in terms of quantity and quality one of two things: either both premises are affirmative with the minor universal, or the two premises differ in quality with the universality of one of them; because if neither of these obtained, then either both premises are negative, or both are affirmative with the minor particular, or two particulars differing in quality; and in the three cases difference occurs, which is a sign of sterility. As for the first: because the truth in our statement 'No stone is human and no rational is stone' is affirmative, and if we said 'No horse is stone' the truth would be negative. As for the second: because if we say 'Some animal is human and every rational is animal' the truth is affirmative, and if we said 'Every horse is animal' the truth would be negative. And as for the third: because the truth in our statement 'Some animal is human and some body is not animal' is affirmative, and if we said 'Some stone is not animal' the truth would be negative. Then the author did not address the explanation of the conditions of the fourth figure in terms of modality, due to the scant regard for this figure because of its complete distance from natural intuition, to the extent that the two shaykhs (al-Farabi and Ibn Sina) dropped it from consideration in the sciences and demonstration, and some even excluded it from the division.

Commentary
This passage discusses the conditions for the fourth figure of the categorical syllogism and explains why the author omitted modal conditions. The commentary begins by stating that the fourth figure requires either both premises affirmative with the minor universal, or the two premises differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) with one of them universal. This is because if neither condition holds, three problematic cases arise: both premises negative, both affirmative with a particular minor, or two particular premises differing in quality. In each case, the syllogism produces conflicting results (difference), indicating sterility (adam al-intaj). The text gives examples: for the first case, 'No stone is human and no rational is stone' yields an affirmative conclusion, but if we change to 'No horse is stone' it becomes negative, showing inconsistency. Similarly for the other cases. Then the author notes that the original author (al-musannif) did not address modal conditions (shara'it bi-hasb al-jiha) for the fourth figure because of its lack of naturalness and practical utility. The two shaykhs (al-Farabi and Ibn Sina) excluded it from the sciences and demonstration, and some even omitted it from the division of figures altogether.

Two Alternative Conditions for the Fourth Figure of the Categorical Syllogism
Sharh explains the two alternative conditions for the fourth figure's productivity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a fourth-figure categorical syllogism produces a necessary conclusion by checking the two alternative conditions given by the logicians.

Core Idea
The fourth figure has two distinct conditions for productivity; without them, the syllogism is barren.

In the first three figures of the categorical syllogism, the conditions for a valid conclusion are relatively straightforward. The fourth figure, however, presents a special case: it requires either that both premises be affirmative with a universal minor, or that the premises differ in quality with at least one universal. In this lesson, we will dissect the commentator's explanation of why these conditions are necessary and test them with examples from the Islamic natural philosophy tradition.

Guided Path
1
Locate the two conditions in the passage
The commentator states two alternative conditions for the fourth figure: (1) both premises affirmative with the minor premise universal, or (2) the premises differ in quality with at least one of them universal. Identify these in the Arabic text.
The conditions are introduced by 'إما إيجاب المقدمتين مع كلية الصغرى، وإما اختلاف المقدمتين في الكيف مع كلية إحداهما'.
2
Identify the three barren combinations
If neither condition holds, three cases arise: both premises negative, both affirmative with particular minor, or two premises differing in quality both particular. Recognize that each fails to yield a necessary conclusion.
The commentator uses the phrase 'وعلى التقادير الثلاثة يحصل الاختلاف' to introduce the barrenness proof.
3
Analyze the proof of barrenness via counterexample
For each barren case, the commentator substitutes a term to show that the truth value of the conclusion changes, proving the syllogism does not necessitate a single result. Examine the examples: 'stone', 'human', 'rational', 'horse', 'animal', 'body'.
The first case: both negative premises yield an affirmative conclusion with 'rational' but a negative with 'horse', showing inconsistency.
4
Apply the conditions to a new fourth-figure syllogism
Given a fourth-figure syllogism with quantified premises, test whether it meets either condition. If yes, the syllogism is productive; if no, it is barren. Practise with an Islamic example.
Remember: the fourth figure has middle term as predicate of major and subject of minor.

Conditions of the fourth figure. And the minor premise must be universal so that it can serve as a major for it (the fourth figure), as in the first and third moods only. His statement: 'And in the fourth' means: required for the productivity of the fourth figure with respect to quantity and quality is one of two matters: either both premises are affirmative with the minor premise universal, or the two premises differ in quality with one of them universal. This is because if neither (condition) holds, then either both premises are negative, or both are affirmative with the minor premise particular, or both are particular and differing in quality. In all three cases, difference (in the results) occurs, which is evidence of barrenness. As for the first (case): because the true judgment in our statement 'No stone is a human and no rational being is a stone' is affirmative, while if we said 'No horse is a stone' the true judgment would be negative. As for the second: because if we say 'Some animal is a human and every rational being is an animal' the true judgment is affirmative, while if we said 'Every horse is an animal' the true judgment would be negative. As for the third: because the true judgment in our statement 'Some animal is a human and some body is not an animal' is affirmative, while if we said 'Some stone is not an animal' the true judgment would be negative.

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the matn stating the conditions for the fourth figure of the categorical syllogism. The commentator explains that there are two alternative conditions for productivity: either both premises are affirmative with the minor premise universal, or the premises differ in quality (one affirmative, one negative) with at least one of them universal. If neither condition holds, then one of three possible combinations occurs: both negative, both affirmative with particular minor, or two particular premises differing in quality. In each of these three cases, the syllogism fails to produce a necessary conclusion because one can find true premises that lead to contradictory results, as illustrated with examples. For instance, with both premises negative, 'No stone is a human' and 'No rational being is a stone' yield a true affirmative conclusion (since the middle term 'human'? actually the example shows that the combination leads to an affirmative result, but replacing with 'horse' yields a negative, showing inconsistency). This difference indicates barrenness. The examples use common terms from the Islamic sciences: 'stone', 'human', 'rational', 'horse', 'animal', 'body' — all from natural philosophy and logic.

Why Modal Conditions for the Fourth Figure Were Omitted
Commentator explains why modal conditions for the fourth figure were omitted.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the practical and authoritative reasons behind the classical neglect of the fourth figure in Islamic logic and evaluate whether its omission from modal discussion is justified.

Core Idea
The fourth figure was dropped not for invalidity, but for being unnatural.

In this lesson, we examine why the author of the matn chose not to discuss the modality conditions for the fourth figure of the categorical syllogism. The commentator provides a critical rationale: the fourth figure is so unnatural that even the two shaykhs (al-Farabi and Ibn Sina) excluded it from scientific demonstrations. Understanding this omission gives insight into the practical priorities of classical Islamic logicians.

Guided Path
1
Identify the omission
Notice that the author did not address the modal conditions (shara'it al-jiha) for the fourth figure. Modal conditions specify whether premises must be necessary, possible, etc. The commentator is explaining this deliberate gap.
2
Understand the justification
The commentator gives the reason: 'lack of regard for this figure due to its complete unnaturalness.' This means the fourth figure is rarely used because its form is not intuitive. Ask yourself: what makes a syllogistic figure 'natural'? Typically, the first figure is the most natural; the fourth is the least.
3
Assess the authoritative dismissal
Note that 'the two shaykhs' (al-Farabi and Ibn Sina) dropped the fourth figure from consideration in the sciences and demonstration. This is a strong authoritative move. The commentator also says some even excluded it from the division of figures entirely, meaning they reduced syllogistic figures to three.
4
Evaluate the practical validity
Consider: does the fourth figure still produce valid syllogisms? The commentator does not say it is invalid; only that it is neglected for practical reasons. This distinction is important: neglect does not entail logical invalidity. In Islamic logic, the preference for natural forms does not abrogate the formal correctness of other figures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن المصنف لم يتعرض لبيان شرائط الرابع بحسب الجهة؛ لقلة الاعتداد بهذا الشكل لكمال بعده عن الطبع حتى أسقطه الشيخان عن الاعتبار في العلوم والحجة، بل أخرجه البعض عن التقسيم أيضاً.
Then indeed the author did not address the exposition of the conditions of the fourth figure with respect to modality because of the lack of regard for this figure due to its complete unnaturalness, so much so that the two shaykhs dropped it from consideration in the sciences and demonstration, rather some even excluded it from the division [of figures] as well.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on why the author of the matn did not discuss the modal conditions (conditions related to the modality of premises: necessary, possible, etc.) for the fourth figure. The reason given is that the fourth figure is rarely used in logical practice because it is highly unnatural (بعيد عن الطبع). The commentator mentions that 'the two shaykhs' (probably al-Farabi and Ibn Sina, or perhaps earlier authorities) even dropped it from consideration in the sciences and demonstration (العلوم والحجة). Moreover, some logicians have excluded it entirely from the standard division of figures (i.e., they recognize only three figures). This reflects a critical perspective: the fourth figure, while theoretically valid, is practically neglected in Islamic logic.

الجهة (al-jiha)
الشيخان (al-shaykhan)

Key Takeaway
The fourth figure was omitted from modal discussion not because it is formally invalid, but because it is practically unnatural and rarely used in demonstration.

Eight Productive Moods of the Fourth Figure
Commentary enumerates the eight productive moods of the fourth figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the eight productive moods of the fourth figure by combining a universal affirmative minor with the four majors, a particular affirmative minor with a universal negative major, two negative minors with a universal affirmative major, and a universal negative minor with a particular affirmative major.

Core Idea
The fourth figure yields eight productive moods, not just four.

The fourth figure of the categorical syllogism is often neglected, yet it has eight productive moods under the two conditions of the minor being universal affirmative or the premises differing in quality with one universal. This lesson unpacks those moods systematically.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two base conditions
The two conditions are: (a) the minor is universal affirmative, or (b) the premises differ in quality and at least one is universal. These conditions generate the eight moods.
These conditions were explained earlier in the text.
2
List the four combinations when minor is universal affirmative
When the minor is universal affirmative, combine it with each of the four majors: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative. This yields four moods.
3
Add the combination with particular affirmative minor and universal negative major
When the minor is particular affirmative, combine only with the universal negative major. This yields one mood.
4
Add the combination with two negative minors and universal affirmative major
Both the universal negative and particular negative minors can be combined with the universal affirmative major. This yields two moods.
5
Add the final combination: universal negative minor with particular affirmative major
Combine the universal negative minor with the particular affirmative major. This yields one more mood, totalling eight.

So that the universal affirmative (premise) with the four (premises) produces, and the particular with the universal negative, and the two negatives with the universal affirmative, due to lack of regard for this figure because of its complete unnaturalness. And he also did not address the results of mixtures arising from modal propositions in any of the four figures, due to the length of discussion about them, with their details delegated to lengthy works of the art. His statement: 'So that the universal affirmative, etc.' The productive moods in this figure according to one of the two previous conditions are eight, resulting from combining: the universal affirmative minor with the four majors; the particular affirmative minor with the universal negative major; the two negative minors (universal and particular) with the universal affirmative major; and the combination of its universality, i.e., the universal negative minor with the particular affirmative major. The first two of these moods: He did not address, etc. And the conditions of this figure with respect to modality are five matters: First, that the possible (premise) is not used in this figure at all, whether affirmative or negative. Second, that the negative (premise) used in it is convertible. Third, one of two matters: the truth of permanence (al-dawam) on the minor of the third mood, or general customary (al-'urfi al-'amm) on its major. Fourth, that the major in the sixth mood is from convertible negative propositions. Fifth, that the minor in the second is from one of the two special (premises) and the major is such that the general customary applies to it. The detailed exposition of why these are conditions is sought from expansive books like Sharh al-Matali' and others. This much is beneficial for you. So memorize (Isma'il). And he also did not address, etc. as if a questioner says: The author addressed the exposition of conditions of the first three figures with respect to modality, but did not address the exposition of their results when modal (premises) are mixed with one another in any of those three. So he answered with his statement: due to the length of discussion about their results. 'For the results of mixtures' i.e., for the results of syllogisms arising from mixtures of modal premises with one another. For example, we say in the first figure, when mixing certain modalities, the result is such and such. Example: if the major is absolute general and the minor is diffused absolute, then the result is diffused: like 'Every J is B at some time and every B is A actually' produces 'Every J is A at some time'.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is an expansion that first states the productive moods of the fourth figure, then gives the modal conditions. The commentator explains that based on the two earlier conditions (affirmative + universal minor, or difference in quality with one universal), there are eight productive moods. These are formed by combining a universal affirmative minor with any of the four majors (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative) — three? Actually 'الأربع' likely means the four types of propositions by quantity and quality. Then particular affirmative minor with universal negative major, and the two negative minors (universal and particular) with universal affirmative major, and universal negative minor with particular affirmative major. The commentator then lists five modal conditions: (1) No possibility premise (possible) is used in the fourth figure at all; (2) Any negative premise must be convertible (i.e., 'aks); (3) In the third mood, either the minor is perpetually true (al-dawam) or the major is true in a general customary sense (al-'urfi al-'amm); (4) In the sixth mood, the major must be convertible negative; (5) In the second mood, the minor must be one of the two special types (likely necessary or perpetual) and the major must be general customary. The commentator notes that detailed justification is in longer works. He then gives an example of mixed modality in the first figure: If the major is absolute general and the minor is diffused absolute (i.e., 'at some time'), the result is diffused, using schematic letters (ج, ب, أ). This illustrates the complexity of modal mixtures, which the author omitted due to length.

Five Modal Conditions for the Fourth Figure
Commentary lists five modal conditions for the fourth figure and provides an example of mixed modalities in the first figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five modal conditions for the fourth figure and apply them to check a syllogism's validity, including identifying when a premise is 'possible', 'convertible negative', 'perpetual', or 'general customary'.

Core Idea
The fourth figure bans possibility premises and requires negative premises to be convertible, among other modal restrictions.

Beyond the basic conditions, the fourth figure has five modal conditions that regulate which types of modal premises can be used. These conditions ensure the syllogism's validity. This lesson also includes an example of mixed modalities from the first figure to illustrate the complexity.

Guided Path
1
First condition: No possible premises
The possible (al-mumkina) proposition cannot be used in the fourth figure, whether affirmative or negative. This is a strict ban.
2
Second condition: Negatives must be convertible
Any negative premise used must be convertible (i.e., its converse is true). This is necessary for the deduction.
3
Third condition: For third mood, either minor is perpetual or major is general customary
In the third mood (which involves particular premises), either the minor must be perpetually true (al-dawam) or the major must be true in a general customary sense (al-'urfi al-'amm).
4
Fourth condition: Major in sixth mood must be convertible negative
In the sixth mood, the major premise must be a negative proposition that is convertible.
5
Fifth condition: For second mood, minor must be from the two specials and major general customary
In the second mood, the minor must be one of the two special types (necessary or perpetual), and the major must be general customary.

لينتج الموجبة الكلية مع الأربع والجزئية مع السالبة الكلية والسالبتان مع الموجبة الكلية لقلة الاعتداد بهذا الشكل؛ لكمال بعده عن الطبع ولم يتعرض أيضاً لنتائج الاختلاطات الحاصلة من الموجهات في شيء من الأشكال الأربعة؛ لطول الكلام فيها وتفصيلها موكول إلى مطولات الفن. قوله لينتج الموجبة الكلية إلخ الضروب المنتجة في هذا الشكل بحسب أحد الشرطين السابقين ثمانية حاصلة من ضم الصغرى الموجبة الكلية مع الكبريات الأربع والصغرى الموجبة الجزئية مع الكبرى السالبة الكلية وضم الصغريين السالبتين الكلية والجزئية مع الكبرى الموجبة الكلية وضم كليتها أي الصغرى السالبة الكلية مع الكبرى الموجبة الجزئية، فالأولان من هذه الضروب: لم يتعرض إلخ وشرائط هذا الشكل بحسب الجهة خمسة أمور: الأول أنه لا يستعمل الممكنة في هذا الشكل أصلا موجبة كانت أو سالبة. الثاني: أن يكون السالبة المستعملة فيه قابلة للانعكاس الثالث: أحد الأمرين صدق الدوام على صغرى الضرب الثالث أو العرفي العام على كبراه. الرابع: أن يكون الكبرى في الضرب السادس من القضايا المنعكسة السوالب الخامس: أن يكون الصغرى في الثاني من إحدى الخاصتين والكبرى مما يصدق عليه العرفي العام والبيان التفصيلي، ووجوه كونها شرائط تطلب من الكتب المبسوطة كشرح المطالع وغيره فينفعك هذا القدر. فاحفظ إسماعيل) ولم يتعرض أيضاً إلخ كأن سائلا يقول: إن المصنف تعرض لبيان شرائط الأشكال الثلاثة الأول بحسب الجهة ولم يتعرض لبيان نتائجها عند اختلاط الموجهات بعضها مع بعض في شيء تلك الثلاثة فأجاب بقوله: لطول الكلام في نتائجها. لنتائج الاختلاطات أي لنتائج الأقيسة الحاصلة من اختلاطات الموجهات بعضها إلى بعض، مثلا: نقول في الشكل الأول عند اختلاط الموجهات الفلانية النتيجة كذا مثلا كالكبرى إن كانت مطلقة عامة والصغرى منتشرة مطلقة فالنتيجة منتشرة نحو كل ج ب وقتاما وكل ب أ بالفعل ينتج كل ج آ وقتاما.
So that the universal affirmative (premise) with the four (premises) produces, and the particular with the universal negative, and the two negatives with the universal affirmative, due to lack of regard for this figure because of its complete unnaturalness. And he also did not address the results of mixtures arising from modal propositions in any of the four figures, due to the length of discussion about them, with their details delegated to lengthy works of the art. His statement: 'So that the universal affirmative, etc.' The productive moods in this figure according to one of the two previous conditions are eight, resulting from combining: the universal affirmative minor with the four majors; the particular affirmative minor with the universal negative major; the two negative minors (universal and particular) with the universal affirmative major; and the combination of its universality, i.e., the universal negative minor with the particular affirmative major. The first two of these moods: He did not address, etc. And the conditions of this figure with respect to modality are five matters: First, that the possible (premise) is not used in this figure at all, whether affirmative or negative. Second, that the negative (premise) used in it is convertible. Third, one of two matters: the truth of permanence (al-dawam) on the minor of the third mood, or general customary (al-'urfi al-'amm) on its major. Fourth, that the major in the sixth mood is from convertible negative propositions. Fifth, that the minor in the second is from one of the two special (premises) and the major is such that the general customary applies to it. The detailed exposition of why these are conditions is sought from expansive books like Sharh al-Matali' and others. This much is beneficial for you. So memorize (Isma'il). And he also did not address, etc. as if a questioner says: The author addressed the exposition of conditions of the first three figures with respect to modality, but did not address the exposition of their results when modal (premises) are mixed with one another in any of those three. So he answered with his statement: due to the length of discussion about their results. 'For the results of mixtures' i.e., for the results of syllogisms arising from mixtures of modal premises with one another. For example, we say in the first figure, when mixing certain modalities, the result is such and such. Example: if the major is absolute general and the minor is diffused absolute, then the result is diffused: like 'Every J is B at some time and every B is A actually' produces 'Every J is A at some time'.

Commentary
This passage is an expansion that first states the productive moods of the fourth figure, then gives the modal conditions. The commentator explains that based on the two earlier conditions (affirmative + universal minor, or difference in quality with one universal), there are eight productive moods. These are formed by combining a universal affirmative minor with any of the four majors (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative) — three? Actually 'الأربع' likely means the four types of propositions by quantity and quality. Then particular affirmative minor with universal negative major, and the two negative minors (universal and particular) with universal affirmative major, and universal negative minor with particular affirmative major. The commentator then lists five modal conditions: (1) No possibility premise (possible) is used in the fourth figure at all; (2) Any negative premise must be convertible (i.e., 'aks); (3) In the third mood, either the minor is perpetually true (al-dawam) or the major is true in a general customary sense (al-'urfi al-'amm); (4) In the sixth mood, the major must be convertible negative; (5) In the second mood, the minor must be one of the two special types (likely necessary or perpetual) and the major must be general customary. The commentator notes that detailed justification is in longer works. He then gives an example of mixed modality in the first figure: If the major is absolute general and the minor is diffused absolute (i.e., 'at some time'), the result is diffused, using schematic letters (ج, ب, أ). This illustrates the complexity of modal mixtures, which the author omitted due to length.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the five modal conditions for the fourth figure?
A) Possible premises are not used.
B) Negative premises must be convertible.
C) The minor in the second mood must be necessary.
D) The major in the sixth mood must be convertible negative.

Key Takeaway
The fourth figure has five strict modal conditions that regulate the use of possibility, convertibility, perpetuity, and general customary premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given a fourth figure syllogism with the minor being 'Every ruling is either obligatory or prohibited' (possible premise) and the major being 'Some prohibited things are sinful' (particular affirmative), explain why this syllogism violates the first modal condition and is thus invalid.
?
Construct a valid fourth figure syllogism in an Islamic context that satisfies conditions 2 and 5: use a convertible negative major and a minor from the two specials.
